The Exception for Litigation in Joint Agency
Ruling on two joint agents and exception for litigation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on two joint agents acting alone, identify the exception for litigation, and evaluate Zufar's opposing argument.

Core Idea
Joint agents cannot act alone unless for litigation — necessity overrides joint consent.

When a principal appoints two agents jointly, the general rule is that both must act together because the principal consented to their combined judgment. However, the Hanafi school carves out an exception for litigation (khusumah) due to practical necessity. This lesson examines the rationale, the intra-Hanafi disagreement with Zufar, and the disagreement with the other schools.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
State that two agents appointed jointly cannot act alone because the principal consented to both opinions. This applies even if the price is fixed.
2
Recognize the exception
Note the exception for litigation: one agent may conduct litigation alone. The reason: requiring both to attend causes hardship and confusion.
3
Compare Zufar's view
Zufar held that litigation also requires joint deliberation because the principal did not consent to one alone. His proof: litigation involves opinion.
4
Evaluate the Hanafi response
The Hanafi school responds that the difficulty of joint attendance outweighs the need for joint opinion. This is a case of istihsan based on necessity.
5
Note the cross-madhhab disagreement
The three other schools (Shafi'i, Maliki, Hanbali) agree with Zufar. The practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf): in Hanafi, one agent can represent alone in court; in others, both must appear.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي رواية «كتاب البيوع» من «الأصل» يلزم الموكَّل من غير خصومة؛ لأنَّ الردّ متعين، فإنّهما فعلا عين ما يفعله القاضي لو رفع الأمر إليه، فإنهما لو رفعا الأمر إلى القاضي في عيب لا يحدث مثله ردَّه على الوكيل، ولا يكلفه لإقامة الحجة على ذلك، وكان ذلك رداً على الموكل، ووجه الأوّل أنَّ الردَّ ثبت بالتراضي فصار كالبيع الجديد، ولا نُسَلّم أنهما فعلا عين ما يفعله القاضي، وكيف يكون ذلك مع التفاوت في الولاية). [٢] قوله : ولا يصح ... الخ ؛ يعني إذا وكل رجلٌ وكيلين فلا يجوز تصرُّف أحدهما إلا في خصومة" فقظ فيما وكلا به؛ لأنَّ الموكَّل إنما رضي برأيها لا برأي أحدهما ولو كان البدل مقدّراً، لأن تقديره لا يمنع استعمال الرأي في الزيادة والنقصان، وفي اختيار البائع والمشتري ونحو ذلك، وهذا إذا وكلهما بكلام واحد ؛ بأن قال : وكلتكما بيع عبدي هذا، ونحو ذلك. أما إذا كان توكيلهما على التعاقب، فيجوز لأحدهما الإنفراد في التصرف؛ لأنَّ الموكل رضي برأي كلّ واحدٍ منهما على الإنفراد وقت توكيله، فلا يتغير بعد ذلك، بخلاف الوصيين، فإنه إذا أوصى إلى كلِّ واحدٍ منهما بكلام على حدة حيث لم يجز لأحدهما الإنفراد في الأصح؛ لأن وجوب الوصية بالموت، وعند الموت صارا وصيين جملة واحدة. وحكم الوكالة يثبت بنفس التوكيل، فإذا أقرّ كل واحدٍ منهما بالعقد استبدل كل واحد منهما بالتصرف، وإذا وكلهما بكلامٍ واحدٍ لا ينفرد بـه أحدهما، وإن كان أحدهما حراً بالغاً والآخرُ عبداً أو صبياً. صرح به في «المنح»، وغيره. ولو باع أحدهما بحضرة صاحبه فإن أجازه صاحبه جاز، وإلا فلا، ولو كان غائباً فأجازه لم يجز في قول أبي حنيفة له، وإن مات أحدهما أو ذهب عقله لم يجز للآخر أن يتصرف وحده؛ لعدم رضاه برأيه وحده. صرّح به العيني(٢)، والزيلعي (۳) ، وغيرهما. [١]قوله : إلا في الخصومة؛ يعني إلا أنه يجوز تصرف أحدهما في الخصومة، فلأحدهما أن يخاصم وحده، وقال زفر الله : لا يجوز؛ لأنه يحتاج إلى الرأي، والموكَّلُ لم يرض أحدهما، وبه قالت الثلاثة. ولنا : إن اجتماعهما في الخصومة متعذر؛ لأنه يؤدي إلى التعسف والتشويش
And in a narration from 'Kitab al-Buyu'' from 'al-Asl', it is binding upon the principal without litigation because the return is definite, for they did exactly what the judge would do if the matter were referred to him. For if they referred the matter to the judge regarding a defect that does not occur later, he would return it upon the agent, and not burden him to provide evidence for that, and that would be a return upon the principal. The first view is that the return is established by mutual consent, so it becomes like a new sale. But we do not concede that they did exactly what the judge would do; how can that be given the difference in authority? [2] His saying: 'And it is not valid...' etc. means if a man appoints two agents, then the action of one of them is not permissible except in litigation, regarding what they were appointed for, because the principal only consented to both of their opinions, not to one of them, even if the price is fixed, because fixing it does not prevent exercising opinion in increase or decrease, and in choosing the seller and buyer, etc. This is if he appointed them with one speech, such as saying: 'I appoint you both to sell this slave of mine', etc. But if his appointment was successive, then one of them may act alone in the transaction because the principal consented to each one's opinion individually at the time of appointment, so it does not change after that, unlike the case of two executors (wasiyyayn), for if he bequeathed to each one individually, it is not permissible for one to act alone in the correct view, because the obligation of the bequest is at death, and at death they become two executors as one entity. The ruling of agency is established by the appointment itself. So if each one acknowledges the contract, each one substitutes for the other in action. If he appointed them with one speech, one of them cannot act alone, even if one is a free adult and the other a slave or minor. This is stated in 'al-Minah' and others. If one of them sells in the presence of the other, and the other permits it, it is valid; otherwise no. If one was absent and later permits, it is not valid according to Abu Hanifa. If one dies or loses his mind, the other cannot act alone due to the lack of consent to his opinion alone. This is stated by al-'Ayni (2), al-Zayla'i (3), and others. [1] His saying: 'Except in litigation' means that it is permissible for one of them to act in litigation, so one may conduct litigation alone. Zufar said: It is not permissible because it requires opinion and the principal did not consent to either one alone, and the three (imams?) said the same. But our proof is that meeting together in litigation is impracticable because it leads to inconvenience and confusion.
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage addresses the rulings on two agents appointed jointly. The general rule is that neither can act alone because the principal consented to both opinions together. This applies even if the price is fixed, because discretion remains in other aspects. If appointed together with one statement, they cannot act individually. If appointed successively, they may act individually because consent was given to each at the time. However, this differs for executors (wasiyyayn): even if appointed successively, they cannot act alone because at death they become joint executors. The passage then discusses cases such as one agent acting in the presence of the other, or ratification after absence (according to Abu Hanifa, ratification by an absent agent is invalid). Additionally, if one agent dies or loses sanity, the other cannot act alone. An important exception is litigation: one agent may conduct litigation alone. Zufar disagreed, holding that litigation requires deliberation and the principal did not consent to one alone; the three other schools also held this view. The Hanafi response is that requiring both to attend is impractical and causes hardship and confusion.

Key Terms
وكيلين (wakilayn): Two agents appointed jointly to act on behalf of the principal.
خصومة (khusumah): Litigation or legal proceeding.
وصيان (wasiyyayn): Two executors appointed in a will.
توكيل على التعاقب (tawkeel 'ala al-ta'aqub): Successive appointment; each appointed at a different time.
زفر (Zufar): Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, a prominent Hanafi scholar who differed from Abu Hanifa on this issue.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, in which situation may one of two joint agents act alone?
A) In selling property
B) In buying goods
C) In litigation
D) In receiving payments

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, two joint agents must act together except in litigation, where one may act alone to avoid hardship.

The Agent's Confession in Litigation: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Ruling on agent's confession in court with intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on an agent's confession in litigation, identify the disagreements among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Zufar, and the Shafi'i school, and evaluate the Hanafi istihsan argument against the qiyas counterargument.

Core Idea
An agent authorized for litigation can confess before a judge because confession is a form of response, not a separate act.

When a principal appoints an agent to litigate a case, can that agent confess on behalf of the principal? This question sparked disagreement among the Hanafi imams and with other schools. In this lesson, we explore the reasoning that led Abu Hanifa and Muhammad to accept the validity of such a confession, while others like Abu Yusuf, Zufar, and al-Shafi'i restricted or rejected it. The discussion showcases the Hanafi use of istihsan over strict qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Core Ruling and Its Subject
The ruling concerns the agent's confession (iqrar) during litigation (khusumah). Determine the precise act and its legal consequence: it binds the principal.
The passage specifies that the confession is valid only before a judge and excludes hudud and qisas.
2
Map the Positions of the Key Imams
List the four positions: Abu Hanifa & Muhammad (valid before a judge only), Abu Yusuf (valid even outside court), Zufar and al-Shafi'i (invalid absolutely).
Within Hanafi, the dispute is between the three primary masters; Zufar's view aligns with the Shafi'i position.
3
Analyze the Qiyas Argument Against Confession
The opposing view uses qiyas: litigation (khusumah) means dispute (munaza'a), while confession is amicable agreement (musalamah). A command for one does not include its opposite.
This is the argument of Zufar, al-Shafi'i, and initially Abu Yusuf.
4
Analyze the Hanafi Istihsan Argument
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad use istihsan: the term 'litigation' is used metaphorically to mean any response. The agent must be free to give the appropriate response; restricting it would cause harm.
The Hanafi usul principle: 'al-amr bi al-shay' amr bi muqaddimatih' (command for a thing implies command for its prerequisites).
5
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf and the Mufta Bihi
Practical consequence: under the Hanafi view, the principal is bound by the agent's confession in court; under Shafi'i view, it has no effect. The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is the position of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
This is the relied-upon (mufta bihi) position, as indicated by the commentator.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وصح إقرار الوكيل بالخصومة عند القاضي فالقياس : إنَّ العبد يدفعُ إلى الوكيل، ولا يقبلُ ببينةِ أنَّ الموكل باعَ من صاحب اليد؛ لأنَّ البينة قامت على غير خصم، وفي الاستحسان: يقصر يدُ الوكيل من غير أن يثبت البيع في حق الموكل؛ لأنَّه خصم في قصر اليد، وإن لم يكن خصماً في إثبات البيع على الموكل.

قوله : إقرار الوكيل; سواء كان وكيل المدعي أو وكيل المدعى عليه، وإقرار وكيل المدعي هو أن يقر أن موكله قبض هذا المال، وإقرار وكيل المدعى عليه هو أن يقر بوجوب المال على المدعى عليه.
قوله : بالخصومة; قيَّد بالخصومة ; لأنَّ الوكيل بغيرها لا يصح إقراره مطلقاً،

وعند غيره لا، هذا عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد ، وعند أبي يوسف يجوز وإن كان عند غير القاضي، وعند زفر وهكذا عند الشافعي لا يجوز أصلاً ; لأنه مأمور بالخصومة، لا بالإقرار، ولنا : أنّ الخصومة يراد بها الجواب، فيتضمن الإقرار وأطلقها وهي مقيدة بغير الحدود والقَوَد، فلا يصح إقرار الوكيل على موكله بهما للشبهة. كذا في «المنح»، وغيره ; ولذا قيدها به في «التنوير»، و«مجمع الأنهر». قوله : وعند غيره لا ; هي لا يصح إقرار الوكيل بالخصومة عند غير القاضي. قوله : وعند زفر وهكذا عند الشافعي ... الخ ; وكذا عند ابن أبي ليلى وأحمد وهو قول أبي يوسف له أولاً ، وهو القياس ; لأنَّ الوكيل مأمور بالخصومة لا بالإقرار وتوضيحه : إنَّ الخصومة كلامٌ يجري بين اثنين على سبيل المنازعة، والإقرار كلام يجري بين اثنين على سبيل المسالمة والموافقة. ومن الظاهر أن الأمر بالشيء لا يتناولُ ضدّه ، وبهذا الوكيل بالخصومة لا يملك الإبراء; لأنه إسقاط ، وهو مأمور بالاستيفاء ، وكذا لا يملك الصلح ; لأنَّ الصلح حقٌّ البعض، وهو مأمور باستيفاء الكل. قوله : ولنا...الخ ; هذا وجه الاستحسان، وتقريره : إنَّ التوكيل بالخصومة جائز إجماعاً ، فيدخل تحته ما يملكه الموكّل ، وهو الجواب مطلقاً دون أحدهما معيَّناً، فإنّه ربّما يكون الجواب بأحدهما معيَّناً حراماً. ألا ترى أنه لو كان خصمُهُ محقاً فلا يمكن الإنكار شرعاً، ولو كان مبطلاً يكون حقه في الإنكار لا غير، فلا يملك المعين منهما قطعاً، والخصومة يراد بها مطلق الجواب عرفاً مجازاً ; لأنَّ الخصومة بسبب الجواب، وذكر السبب وإرادة المسبب شائع.
And the confession of the agent in litigation before the judge is valid. By analogy: the slave is handed over to the agent, and evidence that the principal sold to the possessor is not accepted because the evidence is against a non-litigant. By istihsan: the agent's hand is restrained without establishing the sale against the principal, because he is a litigant regarding restraining the hand, even if he is not a litigant regarding proving the sale against the principal. His statement: the confession of the agent, whether he is the agent of the plaintiff or the agent of the defendant. The confession of the agent of the plaintiff is that he confesses that his principal received this wealth, and the confession of the agent of the defendant is that he confesses the obligation of the wealth against the defendant. His statement: in litigation, he restricted it to litigation because the agent without it, his confession is absolutely invalid. And according to others, no. This is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, it is valid even if it is before other than the judge. According to Zufar and likewise according to al-Shafi'i, it is not valid at all, because he is commanded to litigate, not to confess. Our proof: litigation is intended to mean the response, so it includes confession, and it is unrestricted, but it is restricted to other than hudud and qisas, so the confession of the agent against his principal regarding these two is not valid due to ambiguity. So it is in al-Manh, and others, and therefore he restricted it in al-Tanwir and Majma' al-Anhur. His statement: and according to others, no: meaning that the confession of the agent in litigation is not valid before other than the judge. His statement: and according to Zufar and likewise according to al-Shafi'i... etc. And likewise according to Ibn Abi Layla and Ahmad, and it is the opinion of Abu Yusuf first, and it is the analogy, because the agent is commanded to litigate, not to confess. Its clarification: litigation is speech that takes place between two people in the manner of dispute, and confession is speech that takes place between two people in the manner of peace and agreement. It is obvious that the command for something does not include its opposite. Based on this, the agent in litigation does not have the power of discharge, because it is a relinquishment, and he is commanded to collect, and likewise he does not have the power of settlement, because settlement is a right of some, and he is commanded to collect the whole. His statement: and our proof... etc. This is the aspect of istihsan, and its explanation: agency in litigation is unanimously permissible, so it includes what the principal possesses, which is the response in an absolute sense, not one specified, because maybe the response as one specified could be forbidden. Do you not see that if his opponent is truthful, denial is not possible legally, and if he is false, his right is only denial, so he definitely does not possess the specified one between them. Litigation is intended to mean the absolute response metaphorically in custom, because litigation is due to the response, and mentioning the cause and intending the effect is common.
Source pages p. 70

Commentary
This passage deals with the validity of an agent's confession in litigation. The main ruling is that an agent appointed for litigation can validly confess before a judge, according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. Abu Yusuf allows it even outside court, while Zufar and al-Shafi'i forbid it absolutely. The Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) is based on istihsan: the power of litigation encompasses the ability to respond, and a confession is a form of response. The opposing view (Zufar, Shafi'i) argues that the agent is authorized only for dispute, not for admission. The commentary explains that the confession is limited to matters other than hudud and qisas due to ambiguity. It also clarifies that the agent does not have the power to release (ibra') or settle (sulh) because those are contrary to his mandate to collect the full right. The discussion demonstrates the Hanafi methodology of using istihsan over strict qiyas.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): A legal admission or confession by a party that acknowledges a right or obligation owed.
خصومة (khuṣūmah): Litigation or legal dispute; the process of arguing a case before a judge.
قياس (qiyās): Analogical reasoning in Islamic law; applying a ruling from an original case to a new case due to shared effective cause (ʿillah).
استحسان (istiḥsān): Juristic preference; a method of departing from strict analogy in favor of a more equitable solution based on public interest or ease.
حدود (ḥudūd): Fixed corporal punishments prescribed by Islamic law for specific crimes (e.g., theft, fornication).
قود (qiṣāṣ): Retaliatory punishment, specifically for murder or bodily harm, where the victim or heirs may demand equal retaliation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, when is an agent's confession in litigation valid?
A) Only before a judge, and not in matters of hudud and qisas.
B) In any setting, including outside court.
C) Never, because the agent is only authorized for dispute, not confession.
D) Only if the principal explicitly authorizes confession in the agency contract.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) permits an agent's confession in litigation before a judge, based on istihsan, because the agent's mandate to respond (jawab) includes confession, except for hudud and qisas.

Agent's Power to Confess in Litigation: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on scope of agency for litigation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three Hanafi positions on whether an agent appointed for litigation can confess, and explain why the mufta bihi view restricts confession to the court session.

Core Idea
An agent for litigation can only confess before a judge, because the agency is for responding, and confession in court is a response (majaz).

This lesson examines a nuanced dispute within the Hanafi school (and with other schools) regarding the scope of an agent's authority when appointed for litigation (wakalah bil khusumah). The core question: can the agent confess on behalf of the principal? The passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah presents three views, with detailed reasoning based on linguistic analysis (haqiqah/majaz) and istihsan. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to apply principles of agency and legal interpretation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions
State the views of Abu Hanifa & Muhammad (confession valid only before a judge), Abu Yusuf (valid anywhere), and Zufar/Shafi'i (invalid at all).
The mufta bihi is the view of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad.
2
Understand the opposing dalil (qiyas)
The agent is commanded to litigate (deny), not confess. Confession is the opposite of contention; a command for one does not include its opposite.
This is the strict analogical reasoning (qiyas) that Zufar and al-Shafi'i follow.
3
Understand the Hanafi istihsan
The term 'khusumah' figuratively means any response in a dispute. Confession in court is a response, so it falls under the agency. The judge commands a response, not litigation per se.
Istihsan overrides the strict qiyas based on customary usage.
4
Trace the thamarah (practical consequence)
Under the mufta bihi, the confession is valid only if made before the judge. Under Abu Yusuf, it is valid anywhere. Under Zufar, it is never valid.
The difference impacts the binding effect of out-of-court confessions.
5
Distinguish related powers
The agent cannot grant acquittal (ibra') or settle (sulh) because those are not responses; they are relinquishments of rights.
This follows from the principle that the agent is commanded to exact the full right.

الإبراء؛ لأنه إسقاط ، وهو مأمور بالاستيفاء ، وكذا لا يملك الصلح ؛ لأنَّ الصلح حقٌّ البعض، وهو مأمور باستيفاء الكل. قوله : ولنا...الخ ؛ هذا وجه الاستحسان، وتقريره : إنَّ التوكيل بالخصومة جائز إجماعاً ، فيدخل تحته ما يملكه الموكّل ، وهو الجواب مطلقاً دون أحدهما معيَّناً، فإنّه ربّما يكون الجواب بأحدهما معيَّناً حراماً. ألا ترى أنه لو كان خصمُهُ محقاً فلا يمكن الإنكار شرعاً، ولو كان مبطلاً يكون حقه في الإنكار لا غير، فلا يملك المعين منهما قطعاً، والخصومة يراد بها مطلق الجواب عرفاً مجازاً ؛ لأنَّ الخصومة بسبب الجواب، وذكر السبب وإرادة المسبب شائع. وإذا ثبت أنَّ التوكيل بالخصومة ينصرف إلى مطلق الجواب، فقال أبو يوسف : إنَّ الوكيل قائم مقامَ الموكل، وإقرار موكله لا يختص بمجلس القضاء، فكذا إقرار وكيله، والسر فيه : إنَّ الشيء إنّما يختص بمجلس القاضي إذا لم يكن موجباً إلاً بانضمام القضاء إليه، كالبينة والنكول، وأمّا الإقرار فهو موجب بنفسه ، فلا يختص بمجلس القضاء. وقال أبو حنيفة ومحمد : إنَّ التوكيل يتناولُ ما يملكه الموكَّلُ وهو الجواب، إذ الخصومة يراد بها مطلق الجواب عرفاً مجازاً ، والجواب يكون بما يسمى خصومة حقيقة ، وهو الإنكار، وبما يسمى خصومة مجازاً، وهو الإقرار في مجلس القضاء، فإنه يسمى خصومة ؛ لأنه خرج في مقابلة الخصومة كما في تسمية جزاء السيئة سيئة، أو لأنَّ الخصومة سبب له. فيكون من إطلاق اسم السبب على المسبب، أو لأنَّ مجلس القضاء مجلس الخصومة فيما يجري فيه يسمّى خصومة، والخصومة تتناول الإقرار، والإنكار من عموم المجاز، لا من استعمال اللفظ في حقيقته ومجازه، فيملك الوكيل الإقرار من حيث إنّه جواب لا من حيث أنه إقرار، والجواب يستحق عند القاضي. والدليل على أنَّ المراد بالخصومة هاهنا الجواب مطلقاً أن القاضي يأمر بالجواب ، فيقول له : أجب خصمك لا بالخصومة، وفي حملها على الجواب هاهنا يصح التوكيلُ به على كل تقدير، وفي حملها على حقيقتها وهو الإنكار لا يصح به التوكيل إلا على تقدير أن يكون محقاً في الإنكار لا مبطلاً فيه.
And according to others (it is not valid); this is according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf, it is valid even if it is not before the judge. According to Zufar and likewise according to al-Shafi'i, it is not valid at all because he is commanded to litigate, not to confess. Our proof: Litigation is intended to mean the response, so it includes confession, and it was unrestricted but qualified to exclude hudud and qisas, so the agent's confession on behalf of his principal regarding these two is not valid due to doubt (shubha). Thus it is stated in al-Minah and others; hence he qualified it in al-Tanwir and Majma' al-Anhur. His statement: 'And according to others, no' means that the agent's confession in litigation is not valid except before the judge. His statement: 'According to Zufar and likewise according to al-Shafi'i' etc. It is also according to Ibn Abi Layla and Ahmad, and it was Abu Yusuf's first opinion; it is analogical reasoning (qiyas) because the agent is commanded to litigate, not to confess. Explanation: Litigation is speech between two parties in a contentious manner, while confession is speech between two parties in a conciliatory and agreeable manner. It is obvious that a command for something does not include its opposite. Thus the agent for litigation does not have the power to grant acquittal (ibra'), because it is a relinquishment (isqat) while he is commanded to exact (istifa'), and likewise he does not have the power to settle (sulh) because settlement is for part of the right while he is commanded to exact the whole. His statement: 'Our proof' etc. This is the face of istihsan. Its explanation: Agency for litigation is unanimously valid, so it includes what the principal possesses, which is the absolute response, not one of the two (affirmation or denial) specifically, for sometimes a specific response may be forbidden (haram). Do you not see that if the opponent is truthful, denial cannot be made legally, and if he is false, his right is only denial, so he definitely does not possess a specific one of them. Litigation (khusumah) is used figuratively (majazan) in customary usage to mean absolute response because litigation is caused by the response, and mentioning the cause while intending the effect is common. Once it is established that agency for litigation is directed to absolute response, Abu Yusuf said: The agent stands in the place of the principal, and the principal's confession is not restricted to the court session, so too the agent's confession. The secret behind it: A thing is restricted to the court session only if it requires the judge's involvement to be effective, like evidence (bayyinah) and refusal to take an oath (nukul). Confession, however, is effective by itself, so it is not restricted to the court session. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad said: The agency includes what the principal possesses, which is the response, for litigation is used figuratively to mean absolute response in custom (urf), and the response includes what is called litigation in reality (haqiqah), which is denial (inkar), and what is called litigation figuratively, which is confession in the court session, for it is called litigation because it comes in response to litigation, like calling the punishment of an evil deed an evil deed, or because litigation is the cause of it. So it is using the name of the cause for the effect, or because the court session is a place of litigation, so what occurs there is called litigation. Litigation includes confession and denial through a comprehensive figurative sense, not by using the word in both its literal and figurative senses. So the agent possesses confession insofar as it is a response, not insofar as it is confession, and the response must be given before the judge. The evidence that what is meant by litigation here is absolute response is that the judge commands the response, saying 'Answer your opponent,' not 'Litigate.' Interpreting it here as response makes agency valid in all cases, while interpreting it in its literal sense (denial) makes agency valid only if he is truthful in denial, not if he is false.
Source pages p. 71

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute (and also compares with the Shafi'i school) concerning the scope of an agent's authority when appointed for litigation (wakalah bil khusumah). The specific issue is whether such an agent can make a confession (iqrar) on behalf of the principal. The text presents three positions: (1) Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani hold that the agent can confess only before a judge (i.e., in a court session). (2) Abu Yusuf holds that the agent can confess even outside court, just as the principal can. (3) Zufar, al-Shafi'i, Ibn Abi Layla, and Ahmad (and Abu Yusuf's early opinion) hold that the agent cannot confess at all, because the agency is only for litigation (denial/defense), not for confession. The Hanafi school's relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad (istihsan), supported by the reasoning that the term 'khusumah' is customarily used to mean any response (jawab) in a legal dispute, and confession in court is a type of response. The text explains that the agent's confession is valid only as a response in court, not as an independent act. The dalil for istihsan is that agency for litigation is unanimously valid, and it must include what the principal can do, which is to respond. The response can be either denial (haqiqah of khusumah) or confession (majaz). However, the agent cannot specifically choose to confess if it harms the principal's case; he only has the authority to give whatever response is appropriate. The text also clarifies that the agent cannot grant acquittal (ibra') or settle (sulh) because these contradict the purpose of litigation (to exact the full right). The passage uses detailed analogical reasoning to refute the opposing views. The authority of Abu Hanifa and Muhammad is considered the sounder position (istihsan) over the strict qiyas of Zufar and al-Shafi'i.

Key Terms
الخصومة (al-khusumah): Litigation or legal dispute; in this context, it is interpreted as the response (jawab) given by a defendant in court, whether denial (haqiqah) or confession (majaz).
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Confession or admission of a claim by the defendant; considered a type of response (jawab) when made in court.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of departing from strict analogy (qiyas) for a stronger evidence or custom. Here, istihsan allows the agent to confess in court despite the apparent prohibition.
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning; here, strict qiyas would prevent the agent from confessing because the agency is for litigation not confession.
الإبراء (al-ibra'): Acquittal or release from a debt; the agent cannot grant this because it is a relinquishment (isqat) while he is commanded to exact (istifa').
الصّلح (al-sulh): Settlement or compromise; the agent cannot settle because it involves giving up part of the right, while he is commanded to exact the whole.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi (relied-upon) position in the Hanafi school, where can an agent appointed for litigation (wakalah bil khusumah) validly confess on behalf of his principal?
A) Anywhere, like the principal himself.
B) Only before a judge (in a court session).
C) He cannot confess at all.
D) Before a judge or any two witnesses.

Key Takeaway
The mufta bihi in the Hanafi school is that an agent for litigation may confess only before a judge, because his authority is to respond, and confession in court is a response.

Definition of Claim, Plaintiff, and Defendant
Defining the legal claim and identifying plaintiff and defendant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define da'wa (claim), muddai (plaintiff), and muddai 'alayh (defendant) according to Hanafi fiqh, distinguishing them from related concepts like shahada (testimony) and iqrar (confession).

Core Idea
Plaintiff is not compelled to litigate; defendant is compelled.

This passage from the Hanafi legal tradition defines the foundational concepts of claim, plaintiff, and defendant. These definitions are critical in qada (judiciary) because they determine who bears the burden of proof and who must respond in court. The text presents multiple scholarly views and clarifies the preferred definition from al-Hidayah. Your task is to parse these definitions, understand their nuances, and see how they apply in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three defined terms
Locate the definitions of da'wa, muddai, and muddai 'alayh in the passage. Note that da'wa is defined first, then the plaintiff and defendant.
The definitions are given both in the matn and in the sharth.
2
Analyze each definition linguistically
Break down each definition: 'da'wa is an assertion of a right belonging to him against another' distinguishes it from testimony and confession. 'Plaintiff is one not compelled to litigate' – focus on the concept of compulsion. 'Defendant is one compelled' – note the asymmetry.
Compulsion refers to being forced by the judge to participate in the lawsuit.
3
Distinguish from similar concepts
Compare da'wa with shahada and iqrar. Testimony asserts a right for another against another; confession asserts a right of another against oneself. Da'wa asserts one's own right against another.
This differentiation is crucial for understanding who can be a plaintiff or witness.
4
Understand objections and responses
The passage raises an objection regarding the guardian of an orphan who is compelled to litigate but is still the plaintiff. Analyze the response: the definition refers to the voluntary initiation of the claim, not the compulsion to participate once the claim is made.
This shows how the definition is applied to nuanced cases.
5
Recognize the preferred definition and its basis
Identify the mufta bihi definition: 'plaintiff is one not compelled to litigate' from al-Hidayah. Note that it is comprehensive and exclusive, and that other definitions are supplementary.
The tarjih is based on the authority of al-Hidayah and its commentators.

هي إخبار بحق له على غيره، والمدعي : مَن لا يُجبر على الخصومة، والمدعى عليه : من يجبر

التصحيف، فيجب المصير إليه، وقد قاسَ عليه ابنُ حِنِّي . انتهى مختـصـرا بـقــدر الضرورة.

وقيل : الدعوى في اللغة : قول يَقْصد به الإنسانُ إيجابَ الشَّيء على الغير، إلا أنَّ اسم المدعي يتناولُ مَن لا حجة له في العرف ، ولا يتناولُ مَن له حجة ، فإِنَّ القاضي يسميه مدعياً قبل إقامة البينة، وبعده يسميه محقاً لا مدعياً ، ويقال لمسيلمة الكتاب : مدعي النبوة ؛ لأنه عجز عن إثباتها، ولا يقال للنَّبي : مدَّعي النبوة ؛ لأنه قد أثبتها بالمعجزة. وفي الشريعة : ما ذكره المصنّف الله بقوله : هي إخبار بحق... الخ. وركنها : إضافة الحقِّ إن كان أصيلاً كقوله : لى عليه كذا أو قضيته أو برأته ونحوه. [واله إضافته إلى من ناب منابه ، كما في الوكيل وربِّ الصغير ووصيه عند ا النزاع. وأهلها : العاقل المميز، فلا تصح من المجنون والصبي الغير المميز، ومن شرط جوازها مجلس القاضي، وحضور خصمه ومعلومية المدعى، وكونها ملزمة وكون المدَّعَى ما يحتمل الثبوت كما سيأتي فلا تصح في مجلس غير القاضي، ولا يقضى على غائب، ولا يمكن القضاء لو كان المدَّعَى مجهولاً ، ودعوى ما يستحيل وجوده باطلة، كما إذا قال لمن لا يولد مثله مثله : هذا ابن، وقس على هذا.

وحكمها : وجوب الجواب على الخصم المدعي عليه، والتفصيل في المبسوطات. [1]قوله : هي ؛ أي الدعوى إخبار عن شخص بحق له على غيره، أي للخبر على غير المخبر، فاحترز بقوله : له ؛ عن الشَّهادة، فإنّها إخبار بحق لآخـر علـى غيـره ، وبقوله : على غيره ؛ عن الإقرار بحقِّ لآخر على نفسه، ويشترط أن يكون هذا الإخبار عند القاضي أو المحكم ، بحضورِ ذلك الغير، حتى لو لم يكن هذا الإخبار عند القاضي أو المُحكّم ، أو لم يكن بحضور ذلك الغير، لا يسمى دعوى ، ، ويشكل هذا بدعوى الوكيل أو الوصي إلا أن يقال : هما نائبان عن الأصل، فيمكن أن يضاف الحق إليها. (٢) قوله : والمدعى عليه ... الخ ؛ لما كانت معرفةُ الفرق بين المدعي والمدعى عليه من أهم

كتاب الدعوى تعريفها وأحكامها
۸۹
[1]
لَمَّا فسر الدعوى ، كان المدعي على هذا التَّفسير هو المخبر يحق له على غيره. فقولُه : المدعي من لا يجبر على الخصومة ؛ تفسير آخر ذكره بعض المشايخ ، وقد قيل : المدعي : مَن يلتمس خلاف الظاهر، وهو الأمر الحادث، والمدعى عليه : مَن يتمسك بالظاهر، كالعدم الأصلي ما تبتني عليه مسائل الدعوى، فسَّرَهما مع الإشارة إلى الحكم فقال : والمدعي... الخ وقد اختلفت عبارات المشايخ في حده :
فمنها ما ذكره المصنف له تبعاً لصاحب «الهداية»، وهو حد صحيح ؛ لكونه جامعاً للحدود، مانعاً من دخول غيره فيه ، وقد يشكل هذا التعريف بوصي اليتيم، فإنّه إذا كان لليتيم حق على آخر فإنَّ القاضي يجبر الوصي على الخصومة، نظراً لليتيم. وقد يجاب عنه بأنَّ المراد هو الإخبارُ الواقع بطلب أحد الخصمين، فلا إشكال، وكذا لا يشكل بما إذا كان فيه مخاصماً من وجه آخر ، كما إذا قال : قضيتُ الدَّين بعد الدعوى، فإنّه لا يجبر على هذه الخصومة إذا تركها.
[١] قوله : لما فسر... الخ ؛ لما كان يَرِدُ على المصنف الله أن تعريف المدعي والمدعى عليه قد ظهر بما عرف به الدعوى، فما وجه إيراد تعريفهما، وأيضاً لَمَّا ظهر أنَّ الدعوى إخبار بحق له على غيره. فظهر أن المدعي مَن يخبرُ القاضي بحق له على غيره، والمدعي عليه من يخبرُ القاضي غيره أن له عليه حقاً، وما عرفهما به يخالف ما ذكرنا ، فدفعه الشَّارِحُ الله البارع، وأجاب بأنَّ هذا تفسير آخر ذكره بعض المشايخ.
(٢) قوله : وقد قيل : المدَّعي من يلتمس... الخ ؛ وقيل : المدعي : من لا يستحق إلا بحجته كمَن يدعي عيناً في يد رجل، فإنّه لا يستحق إلا بالبينة أو الإقرار، والمدعى عليه : مَن يستحق بقولِهِ من غير حجّةٍ كصاحب اليد. وقال في «العناية»(۱) : لعله غيرُ صحيح ؛ لأنَّ المدعى عليه من يدفع استحقاق غيره. انتهى.
وقيل : المدعي : مَن يشتمل كلامه على الإثبات، فلا يصير خصماً بالتكلم في النفي، فإنَّ الخارج لو قال لذي اليد : هذا الشيء ليس لك، لا يكون خصماً ومدعياً
It is an assertion of a right belonging to him against another. The plaintiff: one who is not compelled to litigation. The defendant: one who is compelled. ... It is said: linguistically, a claim is a statement by which a person intends to establish something against another, except that the term 'plaintiff' applies to one who has no proof in custom, and does not apply to one who has proof, for the judge calls him a plaintiff before presenting evidence, and after that he calls him a rightful claimant, not a plaintiff. ... In Islamic law, it is what the author mentioned: 'It is an assertion of a right...' Its pillar is attributing the right if one is the principal... Its conditions: the plaintiff must be sane and discerning... Its ruling: the defendant is obligated to respond.
Source pages p. 88

Commentary
This passage defines the legal concept of a claim (da'wa) in Hanafi fiqh. It opens by giving the primary definition: a da'wa is an assertion of a right belonging to the claimant against another person. The author then identifies the plaintiff (muddai) as one who is not compelled to engage in litigation, while the defendant (muddai alyh) is compelled to respond. This definition distinguishes the claim from testimony (shahada) and confession (iqrar). Testimony is an assertion of a right for another against another, while confession is an assertion of a right for another against oneself. For a statement to constitute a valid claim, it must be made before a judge or arbitrator and in the presence of the opponent. The passage then discusses different scholarly interpretations of the plaintiff and defendant, including the view that the plaintiff is one who seeks to establish something contrary to the apparent state of affairs, while the defendant is one who clings to the apparent state, such as the original absence of the claimed right. The author notes that the definition given in the text follows that of al-Hidayah and is comprehensive and exclusive. However, an objection arises concerning the guardian of an orphan: if the orphan has a right against someone, the judge compels the guardian to litigate, yet the guardian is the plaintiff. The answer is that the definition refers to the assertion made at the request of one of the litigants, so the guardian's compulsion does not contradict the definition. The passage concludes with further definitions: the plaintiff is one who does not deserve the claimed right except through his own proof (like one claiming ownership of an object in another's hand), while the defendant is one who deserves the claimed right by his mere statement without proof (like the possessor).

Key Terms
الدعوى (al-da'wa): In Islamic law, it is an assertion of a right belonging to the claimant against another, made before a judge or arbitrator in the presence of the opponent.
المدعي (al-muddai): The plaintiff: one who is not compelled to engage in litigation; alternatively, one who seeks to establish something contrary to the apparent state of affairs, or one who does not deserve the claimed right except through his own evidence.
المدعى عليه (al-muddai 'alayh): The defendant: one who is compelled to litigate; alternatively, one who clings to the apparent state (like the original absence of the right) or one who deserves the claimed right by his own statement (like the possessor) without needing proof.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the primary Hanafi definition in this passage, what distinguishes a plaintiff from a defendant?
A) The plaintiff has evidence while the defendant does not.
B) The plaintiff is not compelled to litigate, while the defendant is compelled.
C) The plaintiff is the one who speaks first in court.
D) The plaintiff is the claimant seeking a right, while the defendant is the one against whom the claim is made.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, a claim (da'wa) is an assertion of a personal right before a judge, with the plaintiff defined by lack of compulsion to litigate and the defendant by compulsion to respond.

Definitions of Plaintiff and Defendant in Hanafi Procedural Law
Explores multiple definitions of plaintiff and defendant

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the relied-upon Hanafi definition of plaintiff and defendant, distinguish alternative definitions, and apply the form-vs-meaning distinction to classify procedural roles.

Core Idea
In Hanafi procedural law, the plaintiff is the one not compelled to litigate; the defendant is the one compelled, but roles can flip when form contradicts meaning.

This passage from a Hanafi commentary explores the definitions of plaintiff (mudda'i) and defendant (mudda'a 'alayh) in legal claims. While the relied-upon definition from al-Hidayah uses the criterion of compulsion, various mashayikh proposed alternatives based on burden of proof, speech content, or possession. The passage also introduces a crucial nuance: a person may be plaintiff in form but defendant in meaning, as illustrated by the depositary who claims return of a deposit. Understanding these definitions is essential for determining who bears the burden of proof and who is compelled to litigate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core definitions
List the various definitions of plaintiff and defendant presented in the passage: from al-Hidayah (not compelled/compelled), khilaf al-zahir, burden of proof, speech content, and possession-based.
The passage begins with the definition based on notification of a right.
2
Analyze the 'not compelled' criterion
Understand why this definition is considered correct (jami' li al-hudud). Consider the objection regarding the orphan's guardian and the response that compulsion is at the litigant's request.
3
Examine the 'khilaf al-zahir' definition
The plaintiff seeks to overturn the apparent state (e.g., claiming ownership of an object in another's hand); the defendant holds the apparent (original non-existence). Understand how this relates to the burden of proof.
The passage mentions that the defendant is like 'al-'adam al-asli'.
4
Explore the 'burden of proof' definition and its critique
Some defined plaintiff as one who needs proof (bayyinah or iqrar) and defendant as one who does not. Note the critique from al-'Inayah that the defendant repels the right of another, not merely relies on his own statement.
5
Trace the 'form vs. meaning' distinction using the depositary example
The depositary claiming return is plaintiff in form (he makes a claim) but denier/defendant in meaning (he denies liability). The consequences: his evidence is accepted in form, but he is compelled to litigate and take an oath in meaning.
This shows that procedural roles are not fixed; they depend on which aspect (surah or ma'na) is considered.

لَمَّا فسر الدعوى ، كان المدعي على هذا التَّفسير هو المخبر يحق له على غيره. فقولُه : المدعي من لا يجبر على الخصومة ؛ تفسير آخر ذكره بعض المشايخ ، وقد قيل : المدعي : مَن يلتمس خلاف الظاهر، وهو الأمر الحادث، والمدعى عليه : مَن يتمسك بالظاهر، كالعدم الأصلي ما تبتني عليه مسائل الدعوى، فسَّرَهما مع الإشارة إلى الحكم فقال : والمدعي... الخ وقد اختلفت عبارات المشايخ في حده : فمنها ما ذكره المصنف له تبعاً لصاحب «الهداية»، وهو حد صحيح ؛ لكونه جامعاً للحدود، مانعاً من دخول غيره فيه ، وقد يشكل هذا التعريف بوصي اليتيم، فإنّه إذا كان لليتيم حق على آخر فإنَّ القاضي يجبر الوصي على الخصومة، نظراً لليتيم. وقد يجاب عنه بأنَّ المراد هو الإخبارُ الواقع بطلب أحد الخصمين، فلا إشكال، وكذا لا يشكل بما إذا كان فيه مخاصماً من وجه آخر ، كما إذا قال : قضيتُ الدَّين بعد الدعوى، فإنّه لا يجبر على هذه الخصومة إذا تركها. [١] قوله : لما فسر... الخ ؛ لما كان يَرِدُ على المصنف الله أن تعريف المدعي والمدعى عليه قد ظهر بما عرف به الدعوى، فما وجه إيراد تعريفهما، وأيضاً لَمَّا ظهر أنَّ الدعوى إخبار بحق له على غيره. فظهر أن المدعي مَن يخبرُ القاضي بحق له على غيره، والمدعي عليه من يخبرُ القاضي غيره أن له عليه حقاً، وما عرفهما به يخالف ما ذكرنا ، فدفعه الشَّارِحُ الله البارع، وأجاب بأنَّ هذا تفسير آخر ذكره بعض المشايخ. (٢) قوله : وقد قيل : المدَّعي من يلتمس... الخ ؛ وقيل : المدعي : من لا يستحق إلا بحجته كمَن يدعي عيناً في يد رجل، فإنّه لا يستحق إلا بالبينة أو الإقرار، والمدعى عليه : مَن يستحق بقولِهِ من غير حجّةٍ كصاحب اليد. وقال في «العناية»(۱) : لعله غيرُ صحيح ؛ لأنَّ المدعى عليه من يدفع استحقاق غيره. انتهى. وقيل : المدعي : مَن يشتمل كلامه على الإثبات، فلا يصير خصماً بالتكلم في النفي، فإنَّ الخارج لو قال لذي اليد : هذا الشيء ليس لك، لا يكون خصماً ومدعياً لكن الاعتبار في هذا للمعنى حتى أنَّ المودَعَ إذا ادعى رد الوديعة ، فهو مدع في الظاهر ، لكنَّه في المعنى منكر للضمان. وهـي إنما تصح بذكر شيءٍ عُلِمَ جنسه وقدره ما لم يقل : هو لي، والمدعى عليه : هو مَن يشتمل كلامه على النفي فيكتفي به منه ، فإن ذا اليد لو قال : ليس هذا لك، كان خصماً بهذا القدر، وقوله : لي فضلة في الكلام غير محتاج إليه. وقيل : كلُّ مَن شهد بما في يدِ غيرِهِ لنفسه فهو مدَّعي ، وكلُّ مَن شهد بما في نفسِهِ لنفسه فهو منكر، ومدعى عليه، وكلُّ مَن شهد بما في يد غيره فهو شاهد، وكلُّ مَن شهد بما في يد نفسه لغيره فهو مقرّ، وقال محمد في «الأصل»: إنَّ المدعى عليه هو المنكر، والآخر هو المدعي. (1) قوله : لكن الاعتبار... الخ؛ جواب عن سؤالِ مقدر، تقريره : إنَّ المودَع بصيغة اسم المفعول بدعوى الرَّدِ متمسك بما ليس بثابت، إذ الرَّدُّ لم يكن ثابتاً وهو يدعيه ، والمودع بصيغة اسم الفاعل متمسك بما هو ثابت، وهو عدم الرد، فإنه كان ثابتاً، وهو يدعيه، وكان ينبغي أن يكون المودَعُ بصيغة اسم المفعول هو المدعي، والمودع بصيغة اسم الفاعل هو المنكر. وتقرير الجواب : إنَّ المودَع بصيغة اسم المفعول يدَّعي فراغ ذمته عن الضمان وهو أصل، والمودع بصيغة اسم الفاعل يدَّعي شغلَ ذمّته ؛ لأنه لم يكن ثابتاً ؛ ولهذا تقبلُ بينتُهُ إذا أقامها اعتباراً للصورة، ويجبر على الخصومة، ويحلف اعتباراً للمعنى. حاصله : إن المودِعَ حين ادَّعى ردَّ الوديعة مدَّع صورة ومنكر، ويدعـي علـيـه معنى، واختلاف الحيثيات يستلزم اختلاف الأحكام.
When the claim (da'wa) was defined, the plaintiff (mudda'i) according to this definition is the one who informs of a right he has over another. So his statement: 'The plaintiff is one who is not compelled to litigation' is another definition mentioned by some mashayikh. And it has been said: The plaintiff is one who seeks the opposite of the apparent (khilaf al-zahir), which is the new matter, and the defendant is one who holds on to the apparent, like the original non-existence (al-'adam al-asli), upon which the issues of claims are based. He explained both with reference to the ruling, saying: 'The plaintiff...' etc. The expressions of the mashayikh have differed regarding its definition. Among them is what the author (musannif) mentioned following the author of al-Hidayah, and it is a correct definition because it includes all limits (jami' li al-hudud) and prevents others from entering into it. This definition may be problematic regarding the guardian of an orphan (wasi al-yatim), for if the orphan has a right against someone, the judge compels the guardian to litigate out of consideration for the orphan. It may be answered that what is meant is the notification that occurs at the request of one of the two litigants, so there is no problem. Similarly, it is not problematic if he is a litigant from another aspect, as when he says: 'I paid the debt after the claim,' for he is not compelled to this litigation if he abandons it. [1] His statement: 'When he defined...' etc; since it would be objected to the author that the definition of the plaintiff and defendant has become apparent by what the claim was defined with, so what is the point of mentioning their definitions? Also, since it became apparent that the claim is an notification of a right he has over another, it becomes apparent that the plaintiff is the one who informs the judge of a right he has over another, and the defendant is the one whom the judge informs that another has a right against him, and what the author defined them with contradicts what we mentioned. So the distinguished commentator (sharih) refuted this and answered that this is another definition mentioned by some mashayikh. (2) His statement: 'And it has been said: The plaintiff is one who seeks...' etc; And it has been said: The plaintiff is one who does not deserve except by his proof, like one who claims an object in a man's hand, for he does not deserve it except by evidence (bayyinah) or confession (iqrar), and the defendant is one who deserves by his own statement without proof, like the possessor (sahib al-yad). Al-'Inayah said: Perhaps this is not correct because the defendant is one who repels the right of another. End quote. And it has been said: The plaintiff is one whose speech includes affirmation (ithbat), so he does not become a litigant by speaking of negation (nafy). For if the external person (kharji) says to the possessor: 'This thing is not yours,' he does not become a litigant and plaintiff. But consideration is given to the meaning (ma'na), such that if the depositary (muda') claims return of the deposit, he is a plaintiff in form (zahir) but in meaning he is a denier (munkir) of liability (daman). And it (the claim) is only valid by mentioning something whose genus (jins) and quantity (qadr) are known, unless he says: 'It is mine.' And the defendant is one whose speech includes negation, so it suffices from him. For if the possessor says: 'This is not yours,' he becomes a litigant to this extent, and his saying: 'It is mine' is superfluous speech not needed. And it has been said: Whoever testifies regarding what is in another's hand for himself is a plaintiff; whoever testifies regarding what is in his own hand for himself is a denier (munkir) and defendant; whoever testifies regarding what is in another's hand is a witness; and whoever testifies regarding what is in his own hand for another is a confessor (muqir). Muhammad said in al-Asl: The defendant is the denier, and the other is the plaintiff. (1) His statement: 'But consideration is given to meaning...' etc; is an answer to an implied question. Its purport: The depositary (muda' – passive participle) by claiming return is holding onto something not established, since return was not established and he claims it, while the depositor (mudi' – active participle) is holding onto something established, which is non-return, for it was established and he claims it. So it should have been that the depositary is the plaintiff and the depositor is the denier. The answer: The depositary claims the discharge of his liability (daman) which is an original principle (asl), and the depositor claims the liability of his responsibility because it was not established. Hence, his evidence is accepted if he produces it out of consideration for the form (surah), and he is compelled to litigate and takes an oath out of consideration for the meaning. The gist: The depositor when he claims return of the deposit is a plaintiff in form and a denier in meaning, and a defendant in meaning. The difference in aspects necessitates difference in rulings.
Source pages p. 89

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi discussion of the definitions of plaintiff (mudda'i) and defendant (mudda'a 'alayh) in legal claims (da'wa). The author begins by noting that the definition of da'wa (informing of a right against another) leads to a definition of plaintiff as one who informs of his right. However, the author's immediate definition—'the plaintiff is one who is not compelled to litigation'—is presented by some mashayikh as an alternative criterion. The passage then catalogs various definitions from the mashayikh. One definition focuses on the burden of proof: plaintiff seeks to disprove the apparent (khilaf al-zahir), while defendant adheres to the apparent (e.g., the original non-existence of the right). Another definition, critiqued in al-'Inayah, equates plaintiff with one who needs proof (bayyinah or iqrar) and defendant with one who does not (like the possessor of the object). A third definition distinguishes based on speech content: plaintiff's speech is affirmative (ithbat), defendant's is negative (nafy). The passage then addresses an important nuance: a person may be plaintiff in form but defendant in meaning, as in the case of a depositary (muda') who claims to have returned the deposit. Formally, he is claiming an established fact (return), so he appears as plaintiff; but in meaning, he denies liability (daman), making him a denier. This leads to practical consequences: his evidence is accepted in form, but he is compelled to litigate and takes an oath in meaning. The passage concludes with a summary: the depositor (mudi') who claims return is plaintiff in form and denier/defendant in meaning. This illustrates that the same individual can hold dual procedural roles depending on the aspect considered. The discussion is purely intra-Hanafi, with no reference to other schools.

Key Terms
المدعي (al-mudda'i): The plaintiff; one who initiates a legal claim against another.
المدعى عليه (al-mudda'a 'alayh): The defendant; one against whom a claim is made.
الدعوى (al-da'wa): A legal claim; informing of a right one has against another before a judge.
المنكر (al-munkir): The denier; the party who denies the claim, equivalent to the defendant in meaning.
الوديعة (al-wadi'ah): Deposit; a trust given to another for safekeeping.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of a depositary (muda') who claims to have returned the deposit, what is his procedural role in meaning (ma'na)?
A) Plaintiff
B) Defendant
C) Witness
D) Confessor

Key Takeaway
The plaintiff is the one not compelled to litigate; but form and meaning may split roles, requiring careful analysis in practice.

The Necessity of Specifying Genus and Quantity in a Legal Claim
States a procedural condition for a valid claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi condition of specifying genus and quantity to evaluate the validity of a claim.

Core Idea
A claim must specify genus and quantity — vagueness defeats the purpose of legal obligation.

In Hanafi qada, a claim (da'wa) is not a mere request; it is a legal instrument to obligate the opponent. For this obligation to be enforceable, the subject matter must be precisely identified. The following passage lays down this fundamental condition, explaining why vagueness renders a claim invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is making a legal claim (da'wa). The ruling is that it is conditionally valid: it requires specification of genus and quantity.
The passage begins by stating that a claim is not valid except by mentioning these details.
2
Extract the two essential elements
The claimant must specify the genus (jins) — the broad category (e.g., dirhams, dinars, wheat, cloth) — and the quantity (qadr) — the precise amount (e.g., 1000 dirhams, 100 dinars, 2 sa's of wheat, 1 piece of cloth).
Examples from the text illustrate each element.
3
Understand the legal rationale (wajh)
The purpose of a claim is to impose an obligation (ilzam) on the opponent through evidence or confession. An unknown subject matter cannot be the object of a binding obligation, so the claim must be specific.
This is the core reason (ta'lil): 'wa wajhuhu inna fa'idat al-da'wa hiya al-ilzam...'
4
Apply the condition to examples
Test whether given statements meet the condition. For example, 'You owe me 1000 dirhams' is valid; 'You owe me money' is invalid because genus (dirhams/dinars) is unspecified and quantity is missing.
Use the passage's own examples for practice.
5
Note the scope and additional specifications
The passage also implies that type (naw') and description (sifah) may be required for further clarity. This condition applies to claims for specific property (ayn) or debt (dayn) in qada proceedings.
The source text is cut off but suggests additional detail.

(2) قوله : وهي... الخ ؛ يعني إنَّ الدعوى لا تصح إلا بذكر شيءٍ عُلِمَ جنسُهُ كالدراهم والدنانير والحنطة والثوب مثلاً، وقدره كألف دراهم، ومئة دنانير، وصنوان من الحنطة، وعدل واحدٍ من الثوب. ووجههُ : إِنَّ فائدة الدعوى هي الإلزام على الخصم بواسطة إقامة الحجة من البينة أو الإقرار والإلزام في المجهول لا يتحقق، وإذا بين جنسها ونوعها وقدرها وصفتها
(2) His statement: 'And it...' etc.; meaning that the claim is not valid except by mentioning something whose genus (jins) is known, such as dirhams, dinars, wheat, or cloth for example, and its quantity (qadr), such as a thousand dirhams, a hundred dinars, two sa's (a dry measure) of wheat, or one piece of cloth. And its rationale (wajhuhu): The purpose of the claim is to impose an obligation (ilzam) on the opponent by means of presenting evidence (bayyinah) or confession (iqrar), and obligation regarding the unknown (majhul) is not realizable. And if he specifies its genus, type, quantity, and description,
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement establishing a fundamental condition for the validity of a legal claim (da'wa): the thing claimed must be specified by genus (jins) and quantity (qadr). The commentator explains that the purpose of a claim is to obligate the opponent (ilzam) through evidence (bayyinah) or confession (iqrar). If the subject matter of the claim is unknown (majhul), it is impossible to establish proof or make a meaningful ruling. Therefore, the claimant must clearly identify the type of item (e.g., dirhams, dinars, wheat, cloth) and its precise quantity (e.g., one thousand, one hundred, two sa's, one piece). The passage also implies that further specification of type (naw') and description (sifah) may be required, though the text is cut off. This ruling is foundational in Hanafi procedural law to ensure legal certainty and prevent frivolous claims.

Key Terms
الجنس (al-jins): Genus; broad category of property, e.g., dirhams, dinars, wheat, cloth.
القدر (al-qadr): Quantity; the specific amount or number of the claimed property.
الإلزام (al-ilzam): Imposition of obligation; the legal effect of a claim that obligates the opponent to respond or fulfill.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a claim is valid only if it specifies the genus and quantity of the subject matter, because an unknown thing cannot be the object of a binding obligation.

Condition for Valid Claim and the Form-Meaning Distinction
Defining valid claim and claimant/defendant with deposit example

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the condition for a valid claim (da'wa) and distinguish between claimant and defendant in both form and meaning using the Hanafi deposit example.

Core Idea
A valid claim specifies type and quantity; the true claimant is the one who asserts against the default, not the one who speaks first.

In Hanafi qada (judicial procedure), the validity of a claim (da'wa) depends on clear specification of the subject matter. But identifying who is claimant and who is defendant goes beyond who asserts the affirmative – it requires examining the underlying legal reality. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains both the condition for a valid claim and the subtle distinction between form and meaning using the classic deposit (wadi'ah) example.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for da'wa validity
Locate the statement that a claim is only valid (tashih) if it mentions something known in its type (jins) and quantity (qadr). Understand that this is a formal requirement to avoid ambiguity.
The condition applies to the subject matter of the claim, not to the persons.
2
Analyze the deposit example for form vs meaning
Consider the scenario: the depositary (muda') claims he returned the deposit. In form, he appears as claimant (he asserts the return), but in meaning he is a denier of liability because the default (asl) is that he is liable. The depositor (mudi') is the opposite.
The commentator resolves the tension by considering the default state (bara'at al-dhimmah).
3
Examine the multiple definitions of claimant/defendant
The passage presents three views: (1) the one whose speech contains negation is the defendant; (2) a view (qil) based on possession – whoever claims property in another's hand is claimant, etc.; (3) Imam Muhammad's view that the defendant is simply the denier.
The views are not contradictory; they reflect different perspectives on the same reality.
4
Synthesize: apply the distinction to burden of proof
Determine practical consequences: in the deposit case, the depositor's evidence is accepted (form) but the depositary is compelled to litigate and may be sworn (meaning). This shows the Hanafi approach of balancing form and meaning.
The difference in aspects (i'itibar) leads to different rules.

زبدة النهاية لعمدة الرعاية
وهي إنما تصح بذكر شيءٍ عُلِمَ جنسه وقدره
لكن الاعتبار في هذا للمعنى حتى أنَّ المودَعَ إذا ادعى رد الوديعة ، فهو مدع في الظاهر ، لكنَّه في المعنى منكر للضمان.
ما لم يقل : هو لي، والمدعى عليه : هو مَن يشتمل كلامه على النفي فيكتفي به منه ، فإن ذا اليد لو قال : ليس هذا لك، كان خصماً بهذا القدر، وقوله : لي فضلة في الكلام غير محتاج إليه.
وقيل : كلُّ مَن شهد بما في يدِ غيرِهِ لنفسه فهو مدَّعي ، وكلُّ مَن شهد بما في نفسِهِ لنفسه فهو منكر، ومدعى عليه، وكلُّ مَن شهد بما في يد غيره فهو شاهد، وكلُّ مَن شهد بما في يد نفسه لغيره فهو مقرّ، وقال محمد في «الأصل»: إنَّ المدعى عليه هو المنكر، والآخر هو المدعي.
(1) قوله : لكن الاعتبار... الخ؛ جواب عن سؤالِ مقدر، تقريره : إنَّ المودَع بصيغة اسم المفعول بدعوى الرَّدِ متمسك بما ليس بثابت، إذ الرَّدُّ لم يكن ثابتاً وهو يدعيه ، والمودع بصيغة اسم الفاعل متمسك بما هو ثابت، وهو عدم الرد، فإنه كان ثابتاً، وهو يدعيه، وكان ينبغي أن يكون المودَعُ بصيغة اسم المفعول هو المدعي، والمودع بصيغة اسم الفاعل هو المنكر.
وتقرير الجواب : إنَّ المودَع بصيغة اسم المفعول يدَّعي فراغ ذمته عن الضمان وهو أصل، والمودع بصيغة اسم الفاعل يدَّعي شغلَ ذمّته ؛ لأنه لم يكن ثابتاً ؛ ولهذا تقبلُ بينتُهُ إذا أقامها اعتباراً للصورة، ويجبر على الخصومة، ويحلف اعتباراً للمعنى. حاصله : إن المودِعَ حين ادَّعى ردَّ الوديعة مدَّع صورة ومنكر، ويدعـي عليه معنى، واختلاف الحيثيات يستلزم اختلاف الأحكام.
Select of the conclusion for 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah. 'And it is only valid by mentioning something known in its type and quantity. But consideration in this is for the meaning, such that if the depositary (al-muda') claims return of the deposit, he is a claimant in appearance, but in meaning he is a denier of liability. Unless he says: it is mine. And the defendant is one whose speech includes negation, so it suffices from him. For if the possessor says: this is not yours, he is a litigant to this extent, and his saying 'it is mine' is an unnecessary surplus in speech. And it is said: whoever testifies about what is in the hand of another for himself is a claimant; whoever testifies about what is in his own hand for himself is a denier and defendant; whoever testifies about what is in the hand of another is a witness; whoever testifies about what is in his own hand for another is an acknowledger. And Muhammad said in al-Asl: the defendant is the denier, and the other is the claimant. (1) His saying: 'but consideration... etc.' is an answer to an implicit question. Its explanation: the depositary (al-muda' bi-sigha) by claiming return clings to what is not established, for return was not established and he claims it; the depositor (al-mudi' bi-sigha) clings to what is established, which is non-return, for it was established and he claims it. It should be that the depositary is the claimant and the depositor is the denier. The answer: the depositary claims his liability is free of guarantee, which is an original state; the depositor claims his liability is occupied, because it was not established. For this reason his evidence is accepted if he brings it, consideration of form, and he is compelled to litigate and takes an oath, consideration of meaning. Summary: when the depositor claims return of the deposit, he is claimant in form and defendant in meaning, and the differences in aspects necessitate differences in rulings.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage defines a valid court claim (da'wa) and clarifies the legal identities of claimant and defendant. The matn states a claim is only valid if it mentions something known in its type (jins) and quantity (qadr). The commentator then explains that consideration here is for the underlying meaning (ma'na), not just the literal form (surah). He illustrates with the example of a deposit (wadi'ah): if the depositary (muda' – the one who was given the deposit) claims he returned it, he appears to be a claimant (since he asserts the return), but in meaning he is actually a denier of liability (because the default is that he is liable if he does not return). Conversely, the depositor (mudi' – who gave the deposit) appears to be a denier (since he denies the return), but in meaning he is a claimant (asserting that the depositary still owes the item or its value). The commentator then discusses general rules: the defendant is the one whose speech contains negation; if the possessor says 'this is not yours', that is enough to make him a defendant. There is a view (qil) that distinguishes based on whose hand the property is in: a person claiming for himself about property in another's hand is a claimant; a person claiming for himself about property in his own hand is a denier/defendant; etc. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani stated in al-Asl that the defendant is the denier and the other is the claimant. In the detailed answer to an implicit question, the commentator explains that the depositary claims freedom from liability (which is the original state), while the depositor claims liability (which is not established). So in form, the depositor brings evidence and takes an oath; in meaning, the depositary is the defendant. This difference in aspects leads to different rulings. The passage underscores that the labels of claimant and defendant are determined by the underlying legal meaning, not merely who speaks first or who asserts the affirmative.

Key Terms
دعوى (da'wa): A legal claim or lawsuit in court; must specify the thing claimed by type and quantity.
مدع (mudda'i): A person who asserts a claim that contradicts the default (asl) or requires proof; in the deposit example, the depositor (mudi') is claimant in meaning.
منكر (munkir): A person who denies a claim; the defendant (mudda'a 'alayh) is typically the denier.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The claim must be made in front of two witnesses
B) The claim must mention the item by its type and quantity
C) The claim must be made by the true owner
D) The claim must be written

Key Takeaway
A valid claim requires specification of type and quantity; the legal roles of claimant and defendant are determined by underlying meaning and default states, not merely by who asserts or denies.

Conditions for Valid Claim: Type, Quantity, and Possession
Detailing the requirements for a valid claim: description of type, quantity, and possession by defendant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions for a valid claim (da'wa) in Hanafi fiqh and distinguish the requirements for claims of debt (dayn) versus specific property ('ayn).

Core Idea
A valid claim requires jins and qadr; for present 'ayn, a simple indication suffices.

The Hanafi school sets precise conditions for a claim to be considered valid in court. A claim that does not meet these conditions is legally ineffective. In this lesson, we examine the requirements of specifying the type (jins) and quantity (qadr) of the subject matter, and how the rules differ when claiming a debt versus a specific tangible item ('ayn). We also see that the commentator expands on the base text to clarify that the matn's general wording applies only to debt claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is da'wa (claim). The ruling is that a claim is valid only if it meets certain conditions; otherwise it is batil (invalid). The benefit of a claim is to impose an obligation on the opponent through proof, which is impossible with an unknown subject.
The passage starts by explaining why specification is needed.
2
Distinguish dayn vs 'ayn
For a debt (dayn), one must mention jins (type), qadr (quantity), naw' (subtype), sifat (quality), and sabab al-wujub (cause of obligation). For specific property ('ayn) present in court, a simple indication (e.g., 'this is mine') suffices. If absent, description and value are needed.
This distinction is crucial: the matn's general wording is restricted to debt claims.
3
Apply the condition of yad
The claim must also state that the subject matter is in the hand (yad) of the defendant. Without this, the claim is not valid because it does not identify the proper party.
The passage mentions 'innahu' referring to possession by defendant.
4
Recognize the effect of omission
If any required condition is omitted (e.g., jins missing, or sabab omitted for debt), the claim becomes majhul (unknown) and is invalid. No legal consequences flow from an invalid claim.
The passage cites sources stating that nothing applies to an unknown claim due to its invalidity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[٢] قوله : وهـي... الخ ؛ يعني إنَّ الدعوى لا تصح إلا بذكر شيءٍ عُلِمَ جنسُهُ كالدراهم والدنانير والحنطة والثوب مثلاً، وقدره كألف دراهم، ومئة دنانير، وصنوان من الحنطة، وعدل واحدٍ من الثوب. ووجههُ : إِنَّ فائدة الدعوى هي الإلزام على الخصم بواسطة إقامة الحجة من البينة أو الإقرار والإلزام في المجهول لا يتحقق، وإذا بين جنسها ونوعها وقدرها وصفتها
هذا في دعوى الدين لا في دعوى العين ؛ فإنَّ العين إن كانت حاضرة تكفي الإشارة بأنَّ هذا ملك لي ، وإن كانت غائبة يجب أن يصفها ويذكر قيمتها ، ( وإنَّهُ) وسبب وجوبها صحتِ الدعوى، وتترتب عليها أحكامها من وجوب الإحضار، والمطالبة بالجواب ووجوب الجواب واليمين، وإقامة البينة، ولزوم إحضار ما يدعيه إن لم يكن ديناً، ولا يتعلق بالدعوى المجهولة شيءٌ من ذلك لفسادها في «شرح النقاية» للبرْجنْدِي.
وفي «الذخيرة»: إن في المكيل والموزون لا بُدَّ من ذِكْرِ الجنس والقَدْرِ ومن ذكر النوع بأن يقول : سبقته أو بخسيته خريفته أو ربيعته، ويذكر مع ذلك صفتها بأنها جيدة أو وسطة أو رديئة، ويذكر سبب الوجوب بأنها وجبت بسبب سَلَم ثمن المبيع، فإن الأحكام تختلف باختلاف الأسباب.
في «الفصول العمادية»: في دعوى الديباج : الصحيح أنه يشترط ذكر الوزن، وفي «الخلاصة»: إن كان الدِّيباج عينا يذكرُ أوصافه ولا حاجة إلى ذكر الوزن، وإن كان دينا بسبب السَّلَم لا بُدَّ من بيان الوزن. وفي «الظهيرية» في معنى الدراهم والدنانير لا بُدَّ من ذكر أنه من أي وأي عند بعضهم، وعند آخرين لا يشترط ضربُ ذلك ، وفي «الخزانة» : يشترط في دعوى الدراهم ذكر الوزن؛ لأنَّ وزنه يختلف باختلاف البلدان. انتهى.
[1]قوله : هذا في دعوى الدين ؛ إذ هو يعرفُ به ؛ لأنَّ إلزام الخصم بالمحصول عند قيام البرهان متعذر ، وكذا الشهادة والقضاء غير ممكن بخلاف العين، كما سيجيء ذكره، ولما كانت عبارة المتن عامَّةً شاملة لدعـوى الـديـن والـعـين ، والحكـم لـم يكـن لذلك.
فبيَّنَ الشَّارحُ البارعُ الله أنَّ هذا القول مخصوص بدعـوى الـديـن ، غـير جـار في دعوى العين، فإنَّ العين إن كانت حاضرة يكفي أن يشير إليها ويعينها بالإشارة، ويقول مثلاً : هذا مملولاً لي ، وإن كانت غائبة يجب وصفها وذكر قيمتها ليتعين.
[٢]قوله : إنه... الخ ؛ عطف على قوله : شيء ؛ أي الدعوى إنما تصح بذكر أنّ المدعى في يد المدعى عليه، حتى أنه لو ادَّعى أنّ هذه الدَّارَ في يدي وملكي، والمدعى
[2] His saying: 'and it... etc.' means that a claim is not valid except by mentioning something known in its type, such as dirhams, dinars, wheat, cloth, etc., and its quantity, such as a thousand dirhams, a hundred dinars, two loads of wheat, one load of cloth. The reason: the benefit of a claim is to impose (a duty) on the opponent by means of establishing evidence (bayyinah) or confession, and imposition regarding the unknown is not realized. When one specifies its type, kind, quantity, and quality, this is for claims of debt, not for claims of specific property ('ayn). For if the 'ayn is present, a mere indication that 'this is my property' suffices; if it is absent, he must describe it and mention its value. (And 'that he' and the cause of its obligation) the claim is valid, and its rulings follow: obligation to produce the subject, demand for answer, obligation of answer and oath, establishing evidence, and requirement to bring what he claims if it is not a debt. Nothing of this applies to an unknown claim due to its invalidity, as in Sharh al-Niqayah by al-Barjandi. In al-Dhakhirah: for weighed and measured items, one must mention the type, quantity, and also the type (subtype) by saying 'its spring wheat' or 'its autumn wheat', and also its quality (good, medium, or bad), and the cause of obligation, because rulings differ with different causes. In al-Fusul al-Imadiyyah: for a claim of brocade, the correct view is that mentioning the weight is required. In al-Khulasah: if the brocade is a specific item, he mentions its descriptions and no need to mention weight; if it is a debt due to a salam sale, weight must be stated. In al-Zahiriyyah: regarding dirhams and dinars, some say one must mention the type (which mint), others do not require that. In al-Khazinah: in a claim of dirhams, mentioning weight is required because weight differs by region. End. [1] His saying: 'This is for claims of debt' because it is known thereby; since imposing on the opponent by production when proof is established is difficult, and similarly testimony and judgment are not possible, unlike 'ayn. Since the wording of the matn is general including both debt and 'ayn, but the ruling is not that, so the brilliant commentator (may Allah have mercy) explained that this saying is specific to claims of debt, not applicable to claims of 'ayn. For if the 'ayn is present, it is sufficient to point to it and identify it by gesture and say, for example, 'this is my property'; if it is absent, he must describe it and mention its value to specify it. [2] His saying: 'that he... etc.' is conjunct to 'thing'; meaning a claim is only valid by mentioning that the claimed thing is in the hand of the defendant, such that if he claims 'this house is in my hand and my property', and the claimed (is in his hand)...
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage elaborates on the requirement that a valid claim must specify the subject matter by type (jins) and quantity (qadr). The commentator explains the reason: a claim aims to impose an obligation on the opponent through proof (bayyinah) or confession, and obligation regarding an unknown is impossible. He then distinguishes between a claim for debt (dayn) and a claim for specific property ('ayn). For debt, type, quantity, and even subtype and quality are required, as well as the cause of obligation (sabab al-wujub), because rulings differ by cause. For 'ayn, if it is present in court, a simple indication (e.g., 'this is mine') suffices; if absent, the claimant must describe it and specify its value. The passage also cites several authoritative works: al-Dhakhirah requires for weighed/measured items mention of type, quantity, subtype, quality, and cause; al-Fusul al-Imadiyyah and al-Khulasah discuss brocade claims; al-Zahiriyyah and al-Khazinah discuss whether mint and weight must be mentioned for dirhams and dinars. The commentator then clarifies that the matn's general wording ('and it is only valid by mentioning something known...') is specific to debt claims, not 'ayn claims, as the matn's phrase is general but the ruling is restricted. Finally, the word 'innahu' (that he) is explained as an additional condition: the claim must state that the claimed item is in the hand of the defendant. The passage ends with an example: if someone claims 'this house is in my hand and my property', but the house is actually in his own hand, it is not a valid claim against anyone. The lesson underscores that the conditions for a valid claim are detailed and vary depending on whether the claim is for a debt or a specific item. The mention of multiple scholarly references shows the depth of Hanafi fiqh in this area.

Key Terms
جنس (jins): Type or category of property, e.g., dirhams, dinars, wheat, cloth.
قدر (qadr): Quantity or amount, e.g., 1000 dirhams, 100 dinars.
عين ('ayn): Specific, identifiable property (not a debt), e.g., a particular house, a garment.
دين (dayn): A debt, i.e., a liability measured in weight or count, not a specific item.
سبب الوجوب (sabab al-wujub): The cause that created the obligation, e.g., a sale, loan, or salam contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Yes, both are required
B) No, a simple indication suffices
C) Only the type is required
D) Quantity must be mentioned as weight

Key Takeaway
For a valid claim, the subject matter must be known; present 'ayn needs only indication, while debt requires detailed description including type, quantity, and cause.

Conditions for a Valid Claim: Debt vs. Specific Property
Defines conditions for a valid claim: debt vs. property, description, possession

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi conditions for a valid claim (da'wa) and distinguish between the required specifications for debt (dayn) and specific property ('ayn).

Core Idea
A claim is valid only if the subject matter is sufficiently specified: for debts, mention genus, quantity, quality, and cause; for property, point or describe.

In this passage, the commentator clarifies that the general conditions for a claim apply to debts, not specific property. For a debt claim, you must detail the genus, amount, quality, and cause. For a specific item, pointing suffices if present; if absent, description and value are needed. Understanding this distinction is foundational to litigation in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal act and its ruling
The act here is making a claim (da'wa). The ruling is that a claim is valid (sahih) only if it meets certain conditions. Identify the overall hukm from the text: a valid claim produces legal consequences; a vague claim is invalid.
The passage states that an unknown claim is invalid (فسادها).
2
Distinguish between debt and specific property
The commentator begins by specifying that the detailed conditions apply to claims for debt (dayn), not for specific property ('ayn). Note the reasoning: for a debt, the subject is not physically present to be pointed to. For specific property, different rules apply based on whether the property is present or absent.
The text says: 'هذا في دعوى الدين لا في دعوى العين'.
3
List the required specifications for a debt claim
For a valid debt claim, the claimant must mention: (1) genus (جنس), (2) quantity (قدر), (3) type/nature (نوع, e.g., early or late harvest), (4) quality (صفة, good/medium/poor), and (5) cause of obligation (سبب, e.g., salam contract). The text cites multiple authorities to show this.
The cause matters because different causes have different legal effects.
4
Identify the requirements for a specific property claim
If the specific property is present, simply pointing to it and saying 'this belongs to me' suffices. If it is absent, the claimant must describe it and mention its value. This is because the property must be identifiable.
For property, the claimant must also establish that the defendant is in possession (yad) — that is covered in the next part.

Source Text
