كتاب الدعوى / تعريفها وأحكامها وإِنَّه هذا في دعوى الدين لا في دعوى العين ؛ فإنَّ العين إن كانت حاضرة تكفي الإشارة بأنَّ هذا ملك لي ، وإن كانت غائبة يجب أن يصفها ويذكر قيمتها ، ( وإِنَّهُ) وسبب وجوبها صحتِ الدعوى، وتترتب عليها أحكامها من وجوب الإحضار، والمطالبة بالجواب ووجوب الجواب واليمين، وإقامة البينة، ولزوم إحضار ما يدعيه إن لم يكن ديناً، ولا يتعلق بالدعوى المجهولة شيءٌ من ذلك لفسادها في «شرح النقاية» للبرْجنْدِي.
وفي «الذخيرة»: إن في المكيل والموزون لا بُدَّ من ذِكْرِ الجنس والقَدْرِ ومن ذكر النوع بأن يقول : سبقته أو بخسيته خريفته أو ربيعته، ويذكر مع ذلك صفتها بأنها جيدة أو وسطة أو رديئة، ويذكر سبب الوجوب بأنها وجبت بسبب سَلَم ثمن المبيع، فإن الأحكام تختلف باختلاف الأسباب.
في «الفصول العمادية»: في دعوى الديباج : الصحيح أنه يشترط ذكر الوزن، وفي «الخلاصة»: إن كان الدِّيباج عينا يذكرُ أوصافه ولا حاجة إلى ذكر الوزن، وإن كان دينا بسبب السَّلَم لا بُدَّ من بيان الوزن. وفي «الظهيرية» في معنى الدراهم والدنانير لا بُدَّ من ذكر أنه من أي وأي عند بعضهم، وعند آخرين لا يشترط ضربُ ذلك ، وفي «الخزانة» : يشترط في دعوى الدراهم ذكر الوزن؛ لأنَّ وزنه يختلف باختلاف البلدان. انتهى.
[1]قوله : هذا في دعوى الدين ؛ إذ هو يعرفُ به ؛ لأنَّ إلزام الخصم بالمحصول عند قيام البرهان متعذر ، وكذا الشهادة والقضاء غير ممكن بخلاف العين، كما سيجيء ذكره، ولما كانت عبارة المتن عامَّةً شاملة لدعـوى الـديـن والـعـين ، والحكـم لـم يكـن لذلك. فبيَّنَ الشَّارحُ البارعُ الله أنَّ هذا القول مخصوص بدعـوى الـديـن ، غـير جـار في دعوى العين، فإنَّ العين إن كانت حاضرة يكفي أن يشير إليها ويعينها بالإشارة، ويقول مثلاً : هذا مملولاً لي ، وإن كانت غائبة يجب وصفها وذكر قيمتها ليتعين.
[٢]قوله : إنه... الخ ؛ عطف على قوله : شيء ؛ أي الدعوى إنما تصح بذكر أنّ المدعى في يد المدعى عليه، حتى أنه لو ادَّعى أنّ هذه الدَّارَ في يدي وملكي، والمدعى عليه أنكر أنها ملك المدعي ، وأقرَّ بأنها في يد المدعي لا تسمع هذه الدعوى. ذكـر البرْجَنْدِي نقلاً . عن «الخلاصة»، وإنّما شرط ذلك في الدعوى ؛ لأنَّ المدعى عليه لا يكون خصماً إلا إذا كان العين في يده.
[1] قوله : يد المدعى عليه ؛ واليد أعم من أن يكون تحقيقاً أو تقديراً حتى أنه لو أتي بدايته، وقال: أخذتُها من يد هذا ؛ لأنها كانت ملكي، وأقام بينةً على ذلك، يقبلُ الدعوى ؛ لأنَّ المدَّعي أقرَّ أَنَّ ذا اليد في الحقيقةِ إنَّما هو الخارج. كذا في «الفصول العمادية».
[٢]قوله : وفي المنقول... الخ ؛ أي إذا كان المدعى منقولاً يزيدُ المدعي على ما ذكر من الجنس والقدر، وإنّه في يد المدعى عليه.
[٣] قوله : بغير حق ؛ لاحتمال أن يكون مرهوناً في يده أو محبوساً بمثل الثمن، فلا يكون في يده بغير حق، هكذا قالوا ، ويرد عليه ما ذكره المصنف له بقوله : أقول.
[٤] قوله : أقول ؛ حاصله : إن احتمال كون الشَّيء مرهوناً أو محبوساً كما يجري في المنقول، كذلك يجري في العقار أيضاً، فلا وجه لتخصيص المنقول بهذا الحكم. وقد يجاب عنه : بأنّ دراية وجههِ موقوف على مقدمتين مسلّمتين : إحداهما : إنّ دعوى الأعيان لا تصح إلا على ذي اليد. كما ذكر في «الهداية) ، وغيرها : إنّ المدعى عليه إنّما ينتصب خصماً إذا كان في يده.
عن
(۱) ردَّ ما قاله الشارح الله ملا خسرو له في (الدرر (۲) : ٣٣٠) بكلام طويل، وأجاب رده الشرنبلالي له في حاشيته (۲) : (۳۳۰) عليه، وأثبت ما قال صدر الشريعة، إذ قال في نهاية كلامه : فتصريحهم بأنه يجب في المنقول أن يقول فيده بغير حق لا ينفي الحكم عما عداه وقد وجد في تصويرهم الدعوى في العقار التصريح به وقال القاري الله في «فتح باب العناية»(۳ : ١٦٣) عمّا قاله الصدر له : ووجهه بعض الفضلاء بوجوه وردها غيرهم.
Book of Claim / Its Definition and Rulings. And it [the claim] is this: It concerns the claim of a debt, not the claim of a specific property. For if the specific property is present, pointing to it suffices by saying 'this belongs to me'. If it is absent, he must describe it and mention its value. (And it) and the cause of its obligation — the claim becomes valid, and its rulings follow from it, such as the obligation to produce the subject, demanding a response, the obligation to respond and take an oath, presenting evidence, and the obligation to bring what he claims if it is not a debt. Nothing of that applies to an unknown claim because it is invalid according to 'Sharh al-Nuqayah' by al-Barjandi. In 'al-Dhakhira': For weighed and measured items, it is necessary to mention the genus and quantity, and to mention the type — e.g., saying 'its early variety' or 'its late variety' etc., and to mention its quality — good, medium, or poor — and to mention the cause of obligation — e.g., it became obligatory due to a salam contract of the price of the sold item, because rulings differ with different causes. In 'al-Fusul al-Imadiyya': Regarding a claim for brocade, the correct view is that mentioning the weight is required. In 'al-Khulasa': If the brocade is a specific property, he mentions its descriptions and there is no need to mention weight; if it is a debt due to a salam contract, weight must be specified. In 'al-Zahiriya': Concerning dirhams and dinars, some say it is necessary to mention which type (e.g., which mint), while others say that specification of mint is not required. In 'al-Khizana': In a claim for dirhams, mentioning weight is required because their weight varies by region. End. [1] He said: This concerns the claim of a debt because it is known by it; for compelling the opponent regarding the product when proof is established is impossible, and similarly testimony and judgment are not possible unlike specific property, as will be mentioned later. Since the expression of the matn is general including claim of debt and specific property, and the ruling was not like that, the excellent commentator — may Allah have mercy on him — clarified that this statement is specific to claim of debt, not applicable to claim of specific property. For specific property, if present, it suffices to point to it and identify it by pointing, and say e.g., 'this is my property'. If absent, he must describe it and mention its value to identify it. [2] He said: 'that it...' etc.; it is connected to his statement 'something'; i.e., the claim is only valid by mentioning that the claimed item is in the possession of the defendant. So if he claimed that this house is in my possession and ownership, and the defendant denied that it is the plaintiff's property but acknowledged it is in the plaintiff's possession, this claim is not heard. al-Barjandi narrated it from 'al-Khulasa'. This condition is made in the claim because the defendant is not a party unless the property is in his possession. [1] He said: 'in the possession of the defendant'; possession is more general, whether actual or constructive, such that if he brought his [؟] and said, 'I took it from the hand of this person because it was my property', and provided evidence for that, the claim is accepted because the plaintiff acknowledged that the real possessor is actually the [؟]. So in 'al-Fusul al-Imadiyya'. [2] He said: 'and regarding movable...' etc.; i.e., if the claimed item is movable, the plaintiff adds to what was mentioned of genus and quantity, and that it is in the possession of the defendant. [3] He said: 'without right'; because it is possible that it is mortgaged in his possession or detained for the price, so it is not in his possession without right. Thus they said, and it is countered by what the author (may Allah have mercy on him) said in his statement: I say. [4] He said: 'I say'; its summary: The possibility that the property is mortgaged or detained, as it applies to movable property, also applies to immovable property. So there is no reason to restrict this ruling to movable property. It may be answered: The understanding of its reason depends on two accepted premises: One, that the claim for specific properties is only valid against the possessor. As mentioned in 'al-Hidayah', etc.: The defendant is only established as a party if it is in his possession. (Footnote: He refuted what the commentator Mulla Khisraw said in 'al-Durar' (2:330) with lengthy speech, and al-Shurnbulali answered his refutation in his gloss (2:330), and affirmed what Sadr al-Shari'ah said, as he said at the end of his speech: Their explicit statement that for movable property one must say 'in his possession without right' does not negate the ruling for other than it, and indeed in their description of the claim for immovable property they have explicitly stated it. And al-Qari said in 'Fath Bab al-‘Inayah' (3:163) regarding what al-Sadr said: Some virtuous people explained it with reasons, but others refuted them.)
Source pages p. 91

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the conditions for a valid claim (da'wa) in Hanafi law. The author begins by distinguishing between a claim for a debt (dayn) and a claim for a specific property ('ayn). For a debt, the claimant must specify the genus (jins), quantity (qadr), quality (sifa), and cause (sabab) of the obligation. For a specific property, if it is present, simply pointing to it is sufficient; if absent, a description and valuation are required. The text then cites several authoritative works: al-Dhakhira emphasizes that for weighed and measured items, the type (e.g., early or late harvest) must be mentioned, along with quality and the cause of the obligation. Al-Fusul al-Imadiyya discusses brocade, noting that weight is required for a debt claim but not for a specific property. Al-Zahiriya mentions disagreement about specifying the mint for dirhams and dinars, while al-Khizana insists on weight due to regional variation.

Following this, the commentator (sharh) clarifies that the initial statement about claim conditions applies only to debts, not to specific property. He then explains that a valid claim requires the property to be in the defendant's possession. If the defendant acknowledges the property is with the plaintiff, the claim is not heard. The definition of possession (yad) is broad, including constructive possession, as illustrated by a hypothetical where a person brings his [؟] and claims he took it from someone else.

The text then addresses a specific condition for movable property: the claimant must state that the property is in the defendant's possession 'without right' (bighayri haqq). This is because the property might be held under a valid right, such as a mortgage or detention for price. The author of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya objects, arguing that this possibility equally applies to immovable property, so the distinction is arbitrary. The text proposes a possible answer relying on two premises, but notes that later scholars debated the issue, with some supporting the original distinction and others defending the objection. This intra-Hanafi disagreement shows the rigor of Hanafi legal analysis, where principles are tested against practical possibilities.

Key Terms
دعوى (da'wa): A legal claim made by one party against another before a judge, demanding a right.
دين (dayn): A debt or obligation that is not a specific physical property, but a liability.
عين ('ayn): A specific tangible property that can be pointed to.
يد (yad): Possession, either actual (direct) or constructive (deemed).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, which of the following is required for a valid claim for a debt?
A) The claimant must physically bring the item to court.
B) The claimant must mention the genus, quantity, quality, and cause of the debt.
C) The claimant must have two witnesses ready at the time of filing.
D) The claimant must swear an oath that the debt is due.

Key Takeaway
A valid claim in Hanafi law requires the subject matter to be sufficiently specified: for debts, detail genus, quantity, type, quality, and cause; for specific property, point or describe, and the defendant must be in possession.

The Dispute on the 'Without Right' Condition for Movable Property
Intra-Hanafi dispute on requiring 'without right' for movable property claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the requirement that in a claim for movable property, the claimant must state that it is in the defendant's possession 'without right', and evaluate the opposing arguments.

Core Idea
The majority require stating 'without right' for movables to exclude valid holds; the commentator objects that immovables have the same possibility.

This passage presents a nuanced debate within the Hanafi school. The established position is that when claiming movable property, the plaintiff must add that the property is in the defendant's possession 'without right' (bighayri haqq), to exclude cases where it is held under a valid right like mortgage. However, the commentator argues that the same reasoning applies to immovable property, so the distinction is arbitrary. This dispute shows Hanafi usul at work: testing a rule by its underlying rationale.

Guided Path
1
State the majority Hanafi position
The majority of Hanafi scholars require that in a claim for movable property, the claimant explicitly states that the property is in the defendant's possession 'without right'. This is to exclude situations where the property is held under a valid right, such as a mortgage or detention for the price.
The text says: 'وفي المنقول يزيد المدعي على ما ذكر من الجنس والقدر وإنه في يد المدعى عليه بغير حق'.
2
Identify the objection from the commentator
The author of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya objects, arguing that the possibility of being held under a right applies equally to immovable property (e.g., a house can be mortgaged or detained). Therefore, requiring 'without right' only for movables is inconsistent.
The commentator's 'أقول' shows his objection.
3
Analyze the response (defense of the distinction)
The text mentions a possible defense based on two premises: (1) claims for specific properties are only against the possessor, and (2) for movables, the default is that possession is without right unless proven otherwise. However, the commentator questions this rationale.
The defense is not conclusively accepted; the text notes that later scholars debated it.
4
Understand the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If the condition is required, a claim for a movable without stating 'without right' may be rejected. If not required, the claim is accepted and the defendant can raise the right as a defense. This affects litigation strategy.
The dispute remains unresolved in the text, but the established position in fatawa favors the majority.

وفي «الذخيرة»: إن في المكيل والموزون لا بُدَّ من ذِكْرِ الجنس والقَدْرِ ومن ذكر النوع بأن يقول : سبقته أو بخسيته خريفته أو ربيعته، ويذكر مع ذلك صفتها بأنها جيدة أو وسطة أو رديئة، ويذكر سبب الوجوب بأنها وجبت بسبب سَلَم ثمن المبيع، فإن الأحكام تختلف باختلاف الأسباب.
في «الفصول العمادية»: في دعوى الديباج : الصحيح أنه يشترط ذكر الوزن، وفي «الخلاصة»: إن كان الدِّيباج عينا يذكرُ أوصافه ولا حاجة إلى ذكر الوزن، وإن كان دينا بسبب السَّلَم لا بُدَّ من بيان الوزن. وفي «الظهيرية» في معنى الدراهم والدنانير لا بُدَّ من ذكر أنه من أي وأي عند بعضهم، وعند آخرين لا يشترط ضربُ ذلك ، وفي «الخزانة» : يشترط في دعوى الدراهم ذكر الوزن؛ لأنَّ وزنه يختلف باختلاف البلدان. انتهى.
[1]قوله : هذا في دعوى الدين ؛ إذ هو يعرفُ به ؛ لأنَّ إلزام الخصم بالمحصول عند قيام البرهان متعذر ، وكذا الشهادة والقضاء غير ممكن بخلاف العين، كما سيجيء ذكره، ولما كانت عبارة المتن عامَّةً شاملة لدعـوى الـديـن والـعـين ، والحكـم لـم يكـن لذلك. فبيَّنَ الشَّارحُ البارعُ الله أنَّ هذا القول مخصوص بدعـوى الـديـن ، غـير جـار في دعوى العين، فإنَّ العين إن كانت حاضرة يكفي أن يشير إليها ويعينها بالإشارة، ويقول مثلاً : هذا مملولاً لي ، وإن كانت غائبة يجب وصفها وذكر قيمتها ليتعين.
[٢]قوله : إنه... الخ ؛ عطف على قوله : شيء ؛ أي الدعوى إنما تصح بذكر أنّ المدعى في يد المدعى عليه، حتى أنه لو ادَّعى أنّ هذه الدَّارَ في يدي وملكي، والمدعى عليه أنكر أنها ملك المدعي ، وأقرَّ بأنها في يد المدعي لا تسمع هذه الدعوى. ذكـر البرْجَنْدِي نقلاً . عن «الخلاصة»، وإنّما شرط ذلك في الدعوى ؛ لأنَّ المدعى عليه لا يكون خصماً إلا إذا كان العين في يده.
[1] قوله : يد المدعى عليه ؛ واليد أعم من أن يكون تحقيقاً أو تقديراً حتى أنه لو أتي بدايته، وقال: أخذتُها من يد هذا ؛ لأنها كانت ملكي، وأقام بينةً على ذلك، يقبلُ الدعوى ؛ لأنَّ المدَّعي أقرَّ أَنَّ ذا اليد في الحقيقةِ إنَّما هو الخارج. كذا في «الفصول العمادية».
[٢]قوله : وفي المنقول... الخ ؛ أي إذا كان المدعى منقولاً يزيدُ المدعي على ما ذكر من الجنس والقدر، وإنّه في يد المدعى عليه.
[٣] قوله : بغير حق ؛ لاحتمال أن يكون مرهوناً في يده أو محبوساً بمثل الثمن، فلا يكون في يده بغير حق، هكذا قالوا ، ويرد عليه ما ذكره المصنف له بقوله : أقول.
[٤] قوله : أقول ؛ حاصله : إن احتمال كون الشَّيء مرهوناً أو محبوساً كما يجري في المنقول، كذلك يجري في العقار أيضاً، فلا وجه لتخصيص المنقول بهذا الحكم. وقد يجاب عنه : بأنّ دراية وجههِ موقوف على مقدمتين مسلّمتين : إحداهما : إنّ دعوى الأعيان لا تصح إلا على ذي اليد. كما ذكر في «الهداية) ، وغيرها : إنّ المدعى عليه إنّما ينتصب خصماً إذا كان في يده.
عن
(۱) ردَّ ما قاله الشارح الله ملا خسرو له في (الدرر (۲) : ٣٣٠) بكلام طويل، وأجاب رده الشرنبلالي له في حاشيته (۲) : (۳۳۰) عليه، وأثبت ما قال صدر الشريعة، إذ قال في نهاية كلامه : فتصريحهم بأنه يجب في المنقول أن يقول فيده بغير حق لا ينفي الحكم عما عداه وقد وجد في تصويرهم الدعوى في العقار التصريح به وقال القاري الله في «فتح باب العناية»(۳ : ١٦٣) عمّا قاله الصدر له : ووجهه بعض الفضلاء بوجوه وردها غيرهم.
Book of Claim / Its Definition and Rulings. And it [the claim] is this: It concerns the claim of a debt, not the claim of a specific property. For if the specific property is present, pointing to it suffices by saying 'this belongs to me'. If it is absent, he must describe it and mention its value. (And it) and the cause of its obligation — the claim becomes valid, and its rulings follow from it, such as the obligation to produce the subject, demanding a response, the obligation to respond and take an oath, presenting evidence, and the obligation to bring what he claims if it is not a debt. Nothing of that applies to an unknown claim because it is invalid according to 'Sharh al-Nuqayah' by al-Barjandi. In 'al-Dhakhira': For weighed and measured items, it is necessary to mention the genus and quantity, and to mention the type — e.g., saying 'its early variety' or 'its late variety' etc., and to mention its quality — good, medium, or poor — and to mention the cause of obligation — e.g., it became obligatory due to a salam contract of the price of the sold item, because rulings differ with different causes. In 'al-Fusul al-Imadiyya': Regarding a claim for brocade, the correct view is that mentioning the weight is required. In 'al-Khulasa': If the brocade is a specific property, he mentions its descriptions and there is no need to mention weight; if it is a debt due to a salam contract, weight must be specified. In 'al-Zahiriya': Concerning dirhams and dinars, some say it is necessary to mention which type (e.g., which mint), while others say that specification of mint is not required. In 'al-Khizana': In a claim for dirhams, mentioning weight is required because their weight varies by region. End. [1] He said: This concerns the claim of a debt because it is known by it; for compelling the opponent regarding the product when proof is established is impossible, and similarly testimony and judgment are not possible unlike specific property, as will be mentioned later. Since the expression of the matn is general including claim of debt and specific property, and the ruling was not like that, the excellent commentator — may Allah have mercy on him — clarified that this statement is specific to claim of debt, not applicable to claim of specific property. For specific property, if present, it suffices to point to it and identify it by pointing, and say e.g., 'this is my property'. If absent, he must describe it and mention its value to identify it. [2] He said: 'that it...' etc.; it is connected to his statement 'something'; i.e., the claim is only valid by mentioning that the claimed item is in the possession of the defendant. So if he claimed that this house is in my possession and ownership, and the defendant denied that it is the plaintiff's property but acknowledged it is in the plaintiff's possession, this claim is not heard. al-Barjandi narrated it from 'al-Khulasa'. This condition is made in the claim because the defendant is not a party unless the property is in his possession. [1] He said: 'in the possession of the defendant'; possession is more general, whether actual or constructive, such that if he brought his [؟] and said, 'I took it from the hand of this person because it was my property', and provided evidence for that, the claim is accepted because the plaintiff acknowledged that the real possessor is actually the [؟]. So in 'al-Fusul al-Imadiyya'. [2] He said: 'and regarding movable...' etc.; i.e., if the claimed item is movable, the plaintiff adds to what was mentioned of genus and quantity, and that it is in the possession of the defendant. [3] He said: 'without right'; because it is possible that it is mortgaged in his possession or detained for the price, so it is not in his possession without right. Thus they said, and it is countered by what the author (may Allah have mercy on him) said in his statement: I say. [4] He said: 'I say'; its summary: The possibility that the property is mortgaged or detained, as it applies to movable property, also applies to immovable property. So there is no reason to restrict this ruling to movable property. It may be answered: The understanding of its reason depends on two accepted premises: One, that the claim for specific properties is only valid against the possessor. As mentioned in 'al-Hidayah', etc.: The defendant is only established as a party if it is in his possession. Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the conditions for a valid claim (da'wa) in Hanafi law. The author begins by distinguishing between a claim for a debt (dayn) and a claim for a specific property ('ayn). For a debt, the claimant must specify the genus (jins), quantity (qadr), quality (sifa), and cause (sabab) of the obligation. For a specific property, if it is present, simply pointing to it is sufficient; if absent, a description and valuation are required. The text then cites several authoritative works: al-Dhakhira emphasizes that for weighed and measured items, the type (e.g., early or late harvest) must be mentioned, along with quality and the cause of the obligation. Al-Fusul al-Imadiyya discusses brocade, noting that weight is required for a debt claim but not for a specific property. Al-Zahiriya mentions disagreement about specifying the mint for dirhams and dinars, while al-Khizana insists on weight due to regional variation.

Following this, the commentator (sharh) clarifies that the initial statement about claim conditions applies only to debts, not to specific property. He then explains that a valid claim requires the property to be in the defendant's possession. If the defendant acknowledges the property is with the plaintiff, the claim is not heard. The definition of possession (yad) is broad, including constructive possession, as illustrated by a hypothetical where a person brings his [؟] and claims he took it from someone else.

The text then addresses a specific condition for movable property: the claimant must state that the property is in the defendant's possession 'without right' (bighayri haqq). This is because the property might be held under a valid right, such as a mortgage or detention for price. The author of 'Umdat al-Ri'aya objects, arguing that this possibility equally applies to immovable property, so the distinction is arbitrary. The text proposes a possible answer relying on two premises, but notes that later scholars debated the issue, with some supporting the original distinction and others defending the objection. This intra-Hanafi disagreement shows the rigor of Hanafi legal analysis, where principles are tested against practical possibilities.

Key Terms
منقول (manqul): Movable property that can be physically transported.
بغير حق (bighayri haqq): Without a legal right; i.e., not under a lien, mortgage, or other valid encumbrance.
عقار ('aqar): Immovable property (real estate).

Key Takeaway
The intra-Hanafi dispute over the 'without right' condition for movable property illustrates how scholars test legal rules against their underlying rationale (ta'lil).

Proving Possession in Immovable Property: The Requirement of Evidence or Judge's Knowledge
Expansion on the requirement of evidence or judge's knowledge to prove possession in immovable property claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on proving possession in immovable property for claims, explain the reasoning based on shubha, and distinguish between absolute ownership claims and claims of purchase or usurpation where the rule does not apply.

Core Idea
In immovable property claims, possession must be proven by evidence or judge's knowledge due to the risk of collusion; movable property possession is self-evident.

This lesson expands on a key procedural rule in Hanafi qada: when a plaintiff claims absolute ownership of immovable property, the defendant's possession must be established through evidence (bayyina) or the judge's personal knowledge. The reasoning hinges on the concept of shubha (ambiguity) arising from the non-observability of immovable property. We will unpack the logic, the internal Hanafi dispute, and the important exception for claims based on a specific transaction like purchase or usurpation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Rule
State the general rule: possession in immovable property (عقار) is not established except by evidence (بينة) or the judge's knowledge (علم القاضي), unlike movable property (منقول) where possession is self-evident.
The rule is from al-Hidaya and is the mufta bihi position.
2
Understand the Reasoning (Talil)
Analyze the two premises: (1) shubha (ambiguity) must be removed, but shubhat al-shubha (ambiguity of ambiguity) is ignored. (2) Possession in عقار is not observable, creating a genuine shubha. Therefore, evidence is required to remove it. For منقول, possession is observable, so no shubha exists in its establishment.
This reasoning uses the concept of شبهة to justify the different treatment.
3
Recognize the Internal Dispute
Note that some Hanafi scholars (e.g., as reported by al-Halwani) held that admission suffices to establish possession in عقار. The majority view requires evidence to prevent collusion (تهمة المواضعة).
The majority view is considered الصحيح and the basis of fatwa.
4
Apply the Exception
When the plaintiff claims purchase from the possessor or usurpation, the requirement to prove possession by evidence is waived. This is because the claim is based on a legal act that can be asserted against anyone, not just the possessor.
If the defendant admits possession, that admission is sufficient; no external evidence is needed.
5
Practice with a Scenario
Given a hypothetical: A claims that B is occupying his land. A says he bought it from B. B admits the land is in his possession but denies the sale. Does A need to prove B's possession with witnesses?
No, because the claim is for purchase, not absolute ownership.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى/تعريفها وأحكامها
وفي العقار لا تثبت اليد إلا بحجة أو علم القاضي ( وفي العقار لا تثبت اليد إلا بحجة أو علم القاضي) والثانية : إن الشبهة معتبرة ويجب دفعها ، لا شبهة الشبهة، كما قالوا : إن شبهةً الربا ملحقة بالحقيقة لا شبهة الشبهة.
وإذا عرفت هاتين المقدمتين فنقول : إنَّ في ثبوتِ اليد على العقار شبهة ؛ لكونه غير مشاهد بخلاف المنقول، فإنّ ثبوت اليد فيه مشاهد ، فوجب دفعها في دعوى العقار، بإثباته بالبينة ؛ لتصح الدعوى ، وبعد ثبوتِهِ يكون احتمال كون اليد لغير المالك شبهةً الشبهة ، فلا تعتبر.
وأما اليد في المنقول فلا يحتاجُ إلى إثباتها لكونها شاهدة فيه، لكن فيه شبهةً كونِ اليد لغير المالك، فوجب دفعها ليصح الدعوى، وفيه بحث : إذ قد صرح بأنَّ المطالبة لا بد منها في العقار أيضاً ؛ ليزول احتمال كونه مرهوناً أو محبوساً بالثمن، ويعلم من هذا أنهم اعتبروا ذلك الاحتمال ، وأوجبوا دفعه في العقار أيضاً، وهذا ليس من الشبهة التي لم يعتبروها كما لا يخفى على من تدبّر. وقال البرْجَنْدِي : يمكن أن يقال : إنَّ إعارة المنقول وإيداعه أغلـب مـن إعارة العقار وإيداعه، وأيضاً الغصب متَّفق عليه في المنقول مختلف فيه في العقار، فلذلكَ خصص المنقول بهذا الحكم. هذا وقد نقل عن ظهيرِ الدِّين المَرْغِينَانِي : إنّه لا بُدَّ في دعوى العقار من معرفةِ القاضي كونه في يد المدعى عليه أنه في يده اليوم بغير حق. كذا في «الفصول العمادية»، وعلى هذه الرواية لا يحتاج إلى الفارق. انتهى.
[1] قوله : وفي العقار... الخ ؛ يعني لا يثبت يد المدعى عليه في الـعـقـار بــصـادق المدعي والمدعى عليه على أنه في يده ، بل تثبتُ اليد فيه ببينة، بأن يشهد الشهود أنهم عاينوا في يده، حتى لو قالوا : سمعنا ذلك، لم تقبل. كما صرحوا به. أو عَلِمَ القاضي أنه في يده بخلاف المنقول، فإنَّ اليد تثبتُ فيه بالتصادق.
والفرق بينهما أنّ اليد في العقار غيرُ شاهدة، ولعله في يد غيرهما، واتفقا على ذلك ؛ ليكون لهما ذريعة إلى أخذه بحكم الحاكم ، فشرط الحجة أو علم القاضي ؛ لتنتفي التهمة، واليد في المنقول معاينة ، فلا حاجة إلى اشتراط ذلك فيه، هذا ما صححه قال في «الهداية»: إنَّهُ لا تثبتُ اليدُ في العقار إلا بالبينة أو علم القاضي، الصحيح ؛ نفياً لتهمة المواضعة. في «الهداية»، وغيرها من المعتبرات. وقال بعضهم : إن اليد تصح بالإقرار، فلا حاجة إلى البينة ولا إلى العلم، وفي «البحر» : شهدوا أنه ملكه، ولم يقولوا : في يده بغير حق، يفتى بالقبول. قال الحَلْوَانِيُّ : اختلف فيه المشايخ ، والصَّحيح أنه لا يقبل ؛ لأنه إن لم يثبت أنه في يده بغير حق لا يمكنه المطالبة بالتسليم، وبه كان يفتي أكثر المشايخ. وقيل : يقضى في المنقول لا في العقار، حتى يقولوا : إنّه في يده بغير حق، فالصحيح الذي عليه الفتوى أنه يقبلُ في حق القضاء بالملك، لا في حق المطالبة بالتسليم. انتهى. وتمامه فيه، فارجع إليه إن شئت.
واعلم أنَّ ما ذكر من اشتراط ثبوتِ اليدِ في العقار بالبينة أو علم القاضي ليس مطلقاً في جميع الصور، بل إذا ادعى المدعي ملكاً مطلقاً في العقار، أما في دعوى الغصب والشراء من ذي اليد فلا يشترط ثبوت اليد. كما صرح في «البزازية» حيث قالَ بعد ذكر اشتراط ثبوتِ اليد في العقار : هذا لو ادعاه ملكاً مطلقاً، أما إذا ادعى الشراء من ذي اليد، وأقر بأنه في يدِهِ فأنكرَ الشّراء وأقر بكونه في يده ، لا يحتاج إلى إقامة البينة على كونه في يده.
والفرق أن دعوى الفعل كما تصح على ذي اليد تصح على غيره ، فإنّه يدعي عليه التمليك، والتمليك كما يتحقق من ذي اليد يتحقق من غيرِهِ أيضاً، فعدم ثبوتِ اليد بالإقرار لا يمنعُ صحة الدعوى ، أما دعوى الملكِ المطلق فدعوى تركِ التَّعرُّض بإزالة اليد، وطلب إزالتها لا يتصوَّرُ إلا من صاحب اليد، وبإقراره لا يثبت كونه ذا اليد ؛ لاحتمال المواضعة. انتهى بقدر الضرورة.
Book of Claims/Definition and Rulings. And regarding immovable property, possession is not established except by proof or the judge's knowledge. The second premise: that ambiguity (shubha) is considered and must be removed, not the ambiguity of ambiguity, as they said: the ambiguity of riba is attached to reality, not the ambiguity of ambiguity. And when you understand these two premises, we say: In establishing possession over immovable property there is ambiguity because it is not observable, unlike movable property, for establishing possession in it is observable. So it is necessary to remove it in a claim over immovable property by proving it with evidence, so that the claim becomes valid. And after its establishment, the possibility that the possession is of someone other than the owner is an ambiguity of ambiguity, so it is not considered. As for possession in movable property, there is no need to prove it because it is a witness in it, but there is an ambiguity of the possession being of someone other than the owner, so it must be removed for the claim to be valid. And there is a discussion: since they explicitly stated that the demand is necessary in immovable property as well, to remove the possibility of it being mortgaged or detained for the price, and it is known from this that they considered that possibility and obligated its removal in immovable property as well. And this is not from the ambiguity that they did not consider, as is clear to anyone who reflects. Al-Burjandi said: It can be said that lending and depositing movable property is more common than lending and depositing immovable property, and also, usurpation is agreed upon in movable property but disputed in immovable property; therefore, movable property was specifically given this ruling. And it is reported from Zahir al-Din al-Marghinani: In a claim over immovable property, it is necessary for the judge to know that it is in the hand of the defendant that he is in possession of it today without right. This is stated in al-Fusul al-Imadiyya, and according to this narration, there is no need for the distinction. End. [1] His statement: "And regarding immovable property..." etc., means: The possession of the defendant in immovable property is not established by the mutual admission of the plaintiff and defendant that it is in his hand, rather possession is established in it by evidence, by the witnesses testifying that they saw it in his hand, even if they said: We heard that, it is not accepted, as they explicitly stated. Or the judge knows that it is in his hand. Unlike movable property, for possession is established in it by mutual admission. And the difference between them is that possession in immovable property is not a witness, and perhaps it is in the hand of someone else, and they both agreed on that to have a means to take it by the judge's ruling, so proof or the judge's knowledge is stipulated to eliminate suspicion. And possession in movable property is observed, so there is no need to stipulate that in it. This is what is corrected in al-Hidaya: that possession is not established in immovable property except by evidence or the judge's knowledge, the correct ruling, to eliminate the suspicion of collusion. In al-Hidaya and other reliable works. And some said: Possession is valid by admission, so there is no need for evidence or knowledge. And in al-Bahr: They testified that it is his property but did not say: in his hand without right, a fatwa is given for acceptance. Al-Halwani said: The scholars differed, and the correct view is that it is not accepted, because if it is not established that it is in his hand without right, he cannot demand delivery. And this was the fatwa of most scholars. And it is said: Judgment is given in movable property but not in immovable property until they say: It is in his hand without right. So the correct view on which fatwa is given is that it is accepted regarding the judgment of ownership, but not regarding the demand for delivery. End. The rest is in it, so refer to it if you wish. And know that what is mentioned of stipulating the establishment of possession in immovable property by evidence or the judge's knowledge is not absolute in all cases. Rather, it is when the plaintiff claims absolute ownership in immovable property. However, in a claim of usurpation or purchase from the possessor, establishment of possession is not stipulated. As explicitly stated in al-Bazzaziyya, where after mentioning the stipulation of establishing possession in immovable property, it said: This is if he claims absolute ownership. But if he claims purchase from the possessor and admits that it is in his hand, then the other denies the purchase but admits it is in his hand, he does not need to produce evidence that it is in his hand. The difference is that a claim of an act is valid against the possessor as well as against others, because he claims transfer of ownership, and transfer of ownership can be realized from the possessor as well as from others. So the lack of establishing possession by admission does not prevent the validity of the claim. As for the claim of absolute ownership, it is a claim of refraining from interference by removing the possession, and demanding its removal is only conceivable from the possessor, and by his admission it does not establish that he is the possessor due to the possibility of collusion. End to the extent needed.
Source pages p. 93

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the rule that in claims regarding immovable property (عقار), possession (يد) is not established by the mutual admission of the parties alone, unlike movable property (منقول). The commentator (شارح) explains the reasoning based on the concept of shubha (دoubt/ambiguity). The key idea is that possession in immovable property is not directly observable, so there is a 'shubha' that must be removed by evidence (بينة) or the judge's knowledge (علم القاضي) to prevent collusion (مواضعة) between the parties. For movable property, possession is evident and thus establishes itself. The passage then discusses an intra-Hanafi dispute: some scholars held that admission suffices for immovable property, but the correct view (upon which fatwa is given) is that evidence is required, especially when the claim is for absolute ownership. However, an exception exists: when the plaintiff claims purchase from the possessor (شراء من ذي اليد) or usurpation (غصب), the requirement of proving possession by evidence is waived because the claim is based on an action that can be done by anyone, not just the possessor. The commentator relies on earlier authorities like al-Hidaya, al-Bahr al-Raiq, and al-Bazzaziyya.

Key Terms
يد (yad): Possession or control over property, often equivalent to 'hand' in legal contexts. In this passage, it refers to the de facto control of property that gives rise to a presumption of ownership.
عقار (aqar): Immovable property (real estate) such as land and buildings.
منقول (manqul): Movable property (chattels) that can be physically transferred.
شبهة (shubha): Ambiguity or doubt that affects the legal ruling. The text distinguishes between 'shubha' (a genuine doubt that must be removed) and 'shubhat al-shubha' (a mere remote possibility that is ignored).
بينة (bayyina): Evidence, typically the testimony of two male witnesses (or one male and two females). In this context, it is the means to prove possession in عقار.
دعوى (da'wa): A legal claim or lawsuit. This passage discusses the conditions for the validity of a claim regarding property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a claim over immovable property, when is it NOT necessary for the plaintiff to prove the defendant's possession by evidence or the judge's knowledge?
A) When the claim is for absolute ownership
B) When the claim is for purchase from the possessor and the defendant admits possession
C) When the property is movable
D) When the plaintiff admits the defendant's possession

Key Takeaway
Possession in immovable property for an absolute ownership claim must be proven by evidence or judge's knowledge to prevent collusion; this requirement is waived for claims of purchase or usurpation from the possessor.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate

Proof of Possession in Real Estate: Evidence vs Acknowledgment
Expounding the Hanafi rule on proof of possession in real estate and its exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule on proof of possession in real estate, explain the rationale of preventing collusion, and identify the exception for claims of usurpation or purchase.

Core Idea
Possession in real estate requires evidence or judge's knowledge due to risk of collusion; mere acknowledgment is insufficient.

In litigation over immovable property, establishing who holds possession is critical. This lesson examines a foundational Hanafi rule from Al-Hidayah: in real estate, possession cannot be proven by the defendant's acknowledgment alone; rather, clear evidence or the judge's personal knowledge is required. The commentary expands on the reasoning — the fear of collusion (muwada'a) between the parties — and clarifies exceptions when the claim concerns usurpation or a direct transaction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Majority Hanafi Rule
From Al-Hidayah: Possession in real estate is not established except by evidence (bayyinah) or the judge's knowledge (ilm al-qadi). This is the sahih (correct) view, adopted to negate suspicion of collusion.
The ruling applies when the plaintiff claims absolute ownership (mulk mutlaq) in real estate.
2
Recognize the Minority View
Some Hanafi mashayikh held that the defendant's acknowledgment (iqrar) of possession is sufficient, without needing separate evidence. They argued that acknowledgment itself is a form of proof.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf, not a cross-madhhab dispute.
3
Analyze the Rationale (Dalil Aqli)
The majority's reason is tuhmat al-muwada'a (suspicion of collusion). Real estate may be in the hands of a third party; the plaintiff and defendant might collude to falsely claim possession to enable a fraudulent judgment against the true owner.
For movables, the risk is lower because possession is observable.
4
Determine the Thamarah al-Khilaf (Practical Consequence)
Under the majority view, the plaintiff must first prove the defendant's possession. Under the minority, a simple acknowledgment suffices, making the lawsuit easier. The majority's precaution protects third-party rights.
The mufta bihi (operative fatwa) follows the majority.
5
Identify the Exception
If the claim is for usurpation (ghasb) or purchase from the possessor, proving possession is not required. The claim is based on an action (da'wa fi'l) that can be directed against anyone, not solely the possessor.
The exception is stated in Al-Bazzaziyyah and endorsed by the commentary.

Source Text

قال في «الهداية»: إنَّهُ لا تثبتُ اليدُ في العقار إلا بالبينة أو علم القاضي، هو الصحيح؛ نفياً لتهمة المواضعة (1) في «الهداية»، وغيرها من المعتبرات. وقال بعضهم : إن اليد تصح بالإقرار، فلا حاجة إلى البينة ولا إلى العلم، وفي «البحر»(۲): شهدوا أنه ملكه، ولم يقولوا : في يده بغير حق، يفتى بالقبول. قال الحَلْوَانِيُّ : اختلف فيه المشايخ ، والصَّحيح أنه لا يقبل ؛ لأنه إن لم يثبت أنه في يده بغير حق لا يمكنه المطالبة بالتسليم، وبه كان يفتي أكثر المشايخ. وقيل : يقضى في المنقول لا في العقار، حتى يقولوا : إنّه في يده بغير حق، فالصحيح الذي عليه الفتوى أنه يقبلُ في حق القضاء بالملك، لا في حق المطالبة بالتسليم. انتهى. وتمامه فيه، فارجع إليه إن شئت. واعلم أنَّ ما ذكر من اشتراط ثبوتِ اليدِ في العقار بالبينة أو علم القاضي ليس مطلقاً في جميع الصور، بل إذا ادعى المدعي ملكاً مطلقاً في العقار، أما في دعوى الغصب والشراء من ذي اليد فلا يشترط ثبوت اليد. كما صرح في «البزازية» حيث قالَ بعد ذكر اشتراط ثبوتِ اليد في العقار : هذا لو ادعاه ملكاً مطلقاً، أما إذا ادعى الشراء من ذي اليد، وأقر بأنه في يدِهِ فأنكرَ الشّراء وأقر بكونه في يده ، لا يحتاج إلى إقامة البينة على كونه في يده. والفرق أن دعوى الفعل كما تصح على ذي اليد تصح على غيره ، فإنّه يدعي عليه التمليك، والتمليك كما يتحقق من ذي اليد يتحقق من غيرِهِ أيضاً، فعدم ثبوتِ اليد بالإقرار لا يمنعُ صحة الدعوى ، أما دعوى الملكِ المطلق فدعوى تركِ التَّعرُّض بإزالة اليد، وطلب إزالتها لا يتصوَّرُ إلا من صاحب اليد، وبإقراره لا يثبت كونه ذا اليد ؛ لاحتمال المواضعة. انتهى بقدر الضرورة. [1] قوله : قال ... الخ ؛ لما كانت هذه المسألة مختلف فيها أراد الشارح له أن يبين إذ العقار عساه في يدِ غيرِهِما ، بخلاف المنقول ؛ فإنَّ اليد فيه مشاهدة . فتهمة المواضعة : أنَّ المدَّعي والمدعى عليه تواضعا على أن يقول المدعى عليه : إنَّ الدَّارَ في يدي ، والحالُ أنَّها في يد ثالث، فيقيم المدعي بينة، ويحكمُ القاضي بأنها ملك المدعي . وإنما قال في «الهداية»: هو الصحيح ؛ لأنَّ عند بعض المشايخ يكفي [۲] تصديق المدعى عليه أنَّها في يده ، ولا يحتاج إلى إقامة البينة. الاختلاف فيها، أو أن ما ذكر في «المتن» هو ما صحَّحه في «الهداية ، فقال : قال في «الهداية»... الخ. وعبارة «الهداية» هكذا : وفي العقار لا يكتفى بذكر المدعي وتصديق المدعى عليه أنه في يده (بل) لا تثبت اليد فيه إلا بالبينة أو علم القاضي هو الصحيح، نفياً لتهمة المواضعة، إذ العقار عساه في يدِ غيرهما ، بخلاف المنقول ؛ لأنَّ اليد فيه شاهدة. انتهى. [1] قوله : إذ العقار ... الخ ؛ حاصله : أنه يحتمل أنَّ المدعي والمدعى عليه تواضعا على أن يصدق المدعى عليه المدعي بأن العقار في يد المدعى عليه ليحكم القاضي باليد للمدعى عليه، حتى يتصرف المدعى عليه، فكان القضاء فيه قضاء بالتصرف في مال الغير، وذلك يفضي إلى نقض القضاء عند ظهوره في يد ثالث. كذا في «البناية). [٢] قوله : لأن عند...الخ ؛ حاصله : إنَّ عند بعض المشايخ يكتفى تصديق المدعى عليه أنَّ الدار مثلاً في يده ، ولا حاجة إلى إقامة البينة عليه ؛ لأنَّ تلك الدار إن كانت في يده فبعدما أثبت المدعي حقه يأخذها منه. وإن لم تكن في يده فلا يكون له ولاية الأخذ ؛ لأنَّ البينة قامت على غيره، مع أن تهمة المواضعة ثابتةٌ مع إقامة البينة أيضاً، بأن يكون في يد المدعى عليه أمانة ، فتواضعا على أن لا يُقر بالأمانة، فيقيم البيِّنَةَ على اليد، ثمَّ على أنها ملكه، فيقضى عليه.
He said in Al-Hidayah: 'Possession in real estate is not established except by evidence or the judge's knowledge; this is the correct view, to negate the accusation of collusion.' (1) In Al-Hidayah and other reliable works. And some said: Possession is valid by acknowledgment, so there is no need for evidence or knowledge. In Al-Bahr: They testified that it is his property, and they did not say: in his hand unlawfully; fatwa is given for acceptance. Al-Halwani said: The mashayikh differed on this; the correct view is that it is not accepted, because if it is not proven that it is in his hand unlawfully, he cannot claim delivery. This was the fatwa of most mashayikh. And it was said: Judgment is given in movables not in real estate, until they say: It is in his hand unlawfully. So the correct view, upon which fatwa is based, is that it is accepted for the purpose of judgment of ownership, not for the purpose of claiming delivery. End. Its completion is in it, so refer to it if you wish. And know that what is mentioned of the condition of establishing possession in real estate by evidence or the judge's knowledge is not absolute in all situations, but rather when the plaintiff claims absolute ownership in real estate. As for the claim of usurpation or purchase from the possessor, establishing possession is not required. As stated in Al-Bazzaziyyah where he said after mentioning the condition of establishing possession in real estate: This is when he claims absolute ownership. But if he claims purchase from the possessor, and he acknowledges that it is in his hand, and he denies the purchase and acknowledges that it is in his hand, he does not need to produce evidence that it is in his hand. The difference is that the claim of an action is valid against the possessor and against others, because he claims transfer of ownership from him, and transfer of ownership is realized from the possessor and from others as well, so the lack of establishing possession by acknowledgment does not prevent the validity of the claim. As for the claim of absolute ownership, it is a claim of refraining from interference by removing the hand, and the request for its removal can only be imagined from the possessor, and by his acknowledgment he is not established as the possessor due to the possibility of collusion. End to the extent necessary. [1] His statement: He said... etc.; Since this issue is disputed, the commentator intended to clarify it. Because real estate may be in the hand of someone other than the two of them, unlike movable property, because possession in it is visible. The accusation of collusion is that the plaintiff and defendant colluded that the defendant would say: The house is in my hand, while in reality it is in the hand of a third party, then the plaintiff brings evidence, and the judge rules that it is the plaintiff's property. And he said in Al-Hidayah: 'It is the correct view' because according to some mashayikh, the defendant's confirmation that it is in his hand is sufficient, and there is no need to produce evidence. [2] The difference, or what is mentioned in the Matn is what he corrected in Al-Hidayah, so he said: 'He said in Al-Hidayah... etc.' And the wording of Al-Hidayah is: In real estate, the plaintiff's mention and the defendant's confirmation that it is in his hand are not sufficient; rather, possession is not established except by evidence or the judge's knowledge; this is the correct view, to negate the accusation of collusion, because real estate may be in the hand of someone other than the two of them, unlike movable property because possession in it is evident. End. [1] His statement: 'Because real estate may be in the hand of someone other than the two of them' etc.; Its summary: It is possible that the plaintiff and defendant colluded that the defendant would confirm to the plaintiff's claim that the real estate is in the defendant's hand so that the judge rules possession for the defendant, so that the defendant may dispose of it, and this ruling would be a ruling for disposing of the property of a third party, which leads to nullification of the ruling when it appears in the hand of a third party. As in Al-Binayah. [2] His statement: 'Because according to some mashayikh' etc.; Its summary: According to some mashayikh, the defendant's confirmation that the house is in his hand is sufficient, and there is no need to produce evidence because if the house is in his hand, after the plaintiff proves his right, he takes it from him. If it is not in his hand, he has no authority to take it because the evidence was brought against someone else, even though the accusation of collusion also exists when evidence is brought, in that if the defendant holds it as a trust, they may collude not to acknowledge the trust, then he brings evidence of possession, then that it is his property, and judgment is given against him.
Source pages p. 94

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah is a commentary on a ruling from Al-Hidayah concerning the proof of possession (yad) in real estate (aqar) within litigation. The passage is an expansion and refinement of the Hidayah's text, typical of the sharh tradition. The core ruling states: possession in real estate is not established except by clear evidence (bayyinah) or the judge's personal knowledge (ilm al-qadi). The reason given is to negate the suspicion of collusion (tuhmat al-muwada'a) between the plaintiff and defendant. The commentary explains that this is the correct (sahih) view, though some mashayikh held that the defendant's acknowledgment (iqrar) suffices. The dispute is intra-Hanafi. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to the majority, as elaborated by Al-Halwani and others, is that the acknowledgment is not sufficient for the purpose of claiming delivery (taslim), but for the purpose of establishing ownership (qada bi al-mulk), it is accepted if the evidence explicitly states that the property is in his hand unlawfully. The commentary further clarifies that this condition of proving possession applies only when the plaintiff claims absolute ownership (mulk mutlaq). If the claim is for usurpation (ghasb) or purchase from the possessor, proving the defendant's possession is not required. The rationale is that a claim of an action (da'wa fi'l) is valid against anyone, whereas a claim of absolute ownership is essentially a request to remove the defendant's hand, which presupposes that the defendant is the possessor. The possibility of collusion makes reliance on mere acknowledgment risky for real estate, which may not be physically in the defendant's hand, unlike movables where possession is observable. The commentary also notes that even with evidence, the possibility of collusion remains, but the rule is precautionary.

Key Terms
العقار (al-ʿaqār): Immovable property such as land and buildings.
اليد (al-yad): Possession; physical control or legal possession of property.
المواضعة (al-muwāḍaʿah): Collusion between two parties to deceive the court.
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence, typically testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) in this passage, when is the plaintiff's evidence of possession accepted in a real estate case?
A) Only if the witnesses explicitly state that the property is in the defendant's hand unlawfully
B) Always, regardless of the wording
C) Only if the defendant does not acknowledge possession
D) Never; evidence is never required for real estate

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule requiring evidence or judge's knowledge to prove possession in real estate safeguards against collusion, with exceptions for claims of usurpation or direct transaction.

Possession Evidence in Immovable Property: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Explaining the Hidayah ruling on possession evidence to prevent collusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires evidence for possession in immovable property and evaluate the opposing position of some mashayikh.

Core Idea
Immovable property possession requires evidence because collusion can harm an absent third party.

This lesson examines a procedural rule from al-Hidayah that distinguishes immovable from movable property in claims. The core issue is whether the defendant's mere admission that he possesses the property is enough, or whether evidence is required to protect against collusion. We will explore the reasoning of both sides and the relied-upon position.

Guided Path
1
State the Hidayah ruling
Identify the ruling that for immovable property, possession is established only by evidence (bayyinah) or the judge's knowledge ('ilm al-qadi), not by the defendant's admission.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Identify the collusion concern
Understand the rationale: immovable property may be in the hand of a third party, so collusion between claimant and defendant can lead to a judgment that harms an absent person.
The term is tuhmat al-muwada'ah.
3
Understand the opposing view
Some Hanafi scholars (mashayikh) argue that admission suffices: if the defendant actually possesses, the claimant can take it after proving ownership; if not, no harm.
They also note collusion is possible even with evidence.
4
Trace the Hanafi rebuttal
Follow the response: the harm in admission is to an absent third party, and the judgment may be reversed (naqd al-qada'). In the evidence scenario, the defendant suffers harm, so collusion is less likely.
Al-Barjandi's analysis highlights the difference.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practically, under the mufta bihi position, a judge must not accept admission as proof of possession for immovable property; evidence is required.
This prevents potential collusion fraud.

إذ العقار عساه في يدِ غيرِهِما ، بخلاف المنقول ؛ فإنَّ اليد فيه مشاهدة . فتهمة المواضعة : أنَّ المدَّعي والمدعى عليه تواضعا على أن يقول المدعى عليه : إنَّ الدَّارَ في يدي ، والحالُ أنَّها في يد ثالث، فيقيم المدعي بينة، ويحكمُ القاضي بأنها ملك المدعي . وإنما قال في «الهداية»: هو الصحيح ؛ لأنَّ عند بعض المشايخ يكفي تصديق المدعى عليه أنَّها في يده ، ولا يحتاج إلى إقامة البينة. الاختلاف فيها، أو أن ما ذكر في «المتن» هو ما صحَّحه في «الهداية ، فقال : قال في «الهداية»... الخ. وعبارة «الهداية» هكذا : وفي العقار لا يكتفى بذكر المدعي وتصديق المدعى عليه أنه في يده (بل) لا تثبت اليد فيه إلا بالبينة أو علم القاضي هو الصحيح، نفياً لتهمة المواضعة، إذ العقار عساه في يدِ غيرهما ، بخلاف المنقول ؛ لأنَّ اليد فيه شاهدة. انتهى. (۱) «الهداية» (٣: ١٥٦).
(۲) «البناية (٧ : ٣٩٥).

فإِنَّهُ إن كان في يده ، وأقر بذلك، فالمدعي يأخذها منه إن ثبت ملكيته بالبينة أو بإقرار ذي اليد أو نكوله، وإن لم يكن في يده وأقر بذلك لا يكون للمدعي ولاية الأخذ من ذي اليد، وإن أقام المدَّعي البيِّنة ؛ لأنَّ البينة قامت على غير خصم ، فعُلِمَ أَنَّهُ إذا أقرَّ ذو اليد باليد ؛ فإنَّ الضرر لا يلحق إلا بذي اليد ، ولا يلحق إلى غيره فتهمة المواضعة مدفوعة، على أنَّ تهمة المواضعة إن كانت ثابتة هاهنا ففي صورة إقامة البينة ثابتةٌ أيضاً، فإنَّ الدَّارَ إذا كانت في يـد رجـل أمانةً فتواضع المدعي وذو اليد على أنَّ ذا اليد لا يقول أنها أمانة في يده ، حتى يقيم المدعي بيِّنَةً على أنها في يدِ ذي اليد، ثمَّ يقيمُ بيِّنةً على أنَّها ملك المدعي ، فيقضي القاضي ويأخذ المدعي الدار.
فالحاصل : إِنَّهُ إذا ظهرَ أَنَّهُ في يد ثالث، وذو اليد أقرَّ أَنَّهُ في يده ، لا يصيرُ الثالث محكوماً عليه ، وكذا إذا ظهر أنَّ يدَ ذي اليد يد أمانة لا يد خصومة . قال البرْجَنْدِي : وفيه إنَّ تهمة المواضعة في صورة الإقرار أكثر ، فإنه إذا لم تكن الدَّارُ في يد المقر وقد أقرَّ باليد، وحكم القاضى لا يلحقه ضرر، بخلاف ما إذا كانت في يده أمانة ، فإنّه إذا حكم عليه ، وأخرجت من يده يتضرر به، فهذه أبعد عن التهمة من الأولى. انتهى. 11 ] قوله : فإنه إن كان في يديه ... الخ ؛ ويرد عليه : إنه يحتمل أن يكون في يده أمانةٌ أو رهناً أو محبوساً ؛ لاستيفاء الثمن.
وأجيب عنه : بأن هذا الاحتمال ثابت بعد إقامة البينة، وفي المنقول أيضاً؛ فلذا لا يلتفت إليه مع أنه بعيد ؛ لأنَّ ضرر المدعى عليه بسبب دعوى صاحب الأمانةِ أو الرَّاهِنِ أو المشتري.
[٢) قوله : فإن الضرر... الخ ؛ وقد أورد عليه : إن محل الاحتياط ليس لزوم الضرر حتى يدفع بما ذكر، بل الفساد لزوم نقض القضاء عند ظهوره في يد ثالث. صرح به في شروح «الهداية»، وغيرها، وفيه ما فيه، فتأمل.
Book of Claims: Definition and Rulings. Since immovable property may be in the hand of someone other than the two parties, unlike movable property; indeed, the hand (possession) in it is visible. The collusion suspicion is that the claimant and defendant conspire that the defendant says: 'The house is in my hand,' while it is in the hand of a third party, so the claimant produces evidence, and the judge rules that the house is the claimant's property. He only said in al-Hidayah: 'It is the correct (view)' because according to some scholars, the defendant's affirmation that it is in his hand suffices, and there is no need to produce evidence. The difference of opinion concerning it, or that what is mentioned in the text is what he corrected in al-Hidayah, so he said: 'He said in al-Hidayah... etc.' The expression of al-Hidayah is like this: 'Regarding immovable property, the claimant's statement and the defendant's affirmation that it is in his hand are not sufficient; rather, possession is not established therein except by evidence or the judge's knowledge. This is correct, negating the collusion suspicion because immovable property may be in the hand of someone other than the two parties, unlike movable property because the hand is a witness in it.' End. [1] His saying, 'Since immovable property... etc.'; its summary is that it is possible that the claimant and defendant collude such that the defendant affirms to the claimant that the immovable property is in the defendant's hand so that the judge rules on possession for the defendant, then the defendant disposes of it. Thus the judgment becomes a judgment to dispose of the property of another, leading to the reversal of the judgment when it appears in the hand of a third party. So in al-Banayah. [2] His saying, 'Because according to... etc.'; its summary is that according to some scholars, the defendant's affirmation that the house, for example, is in his hand suffices, and there is no need to produce evidence because if that house is in his hand, after the claimant proves his right, he takes it from him. And if it is not in his hand, then he has no authority to take because the evidence was established against someone else, while the collusion suspicion also exists with the production of evidence, such as when it is a trust in the hand of the defendant, so they collude that he does not admit the trust, then he produces evidence on possession, then that it is his property, so judgment is given against him.
(1) Al-Hidayah (3:156).
(2) Al-Banayah (7:395).

So if it is in his hand and he admits that, the claimant takes it from him if his ownership is proven by evidence or the admission of the possessor or his refusal. And if it is not in his hand and he admits that, the claimant has no authority to take from the possessor, even if the claimant produces evidence, because the evidence was established against a non-opponent. So it is known that when the possessor admits possession, harm only befalls the possessor and does not befall anyone else, so the collusion suspicion is negated. But if the collusion suspicion is established here, then in the case of producing evidence it is also established. For if the house is in the hand of a man as a trust, and the claimant and the possessor collude such that the possessor does not say that it is a trust in his hand, until the claimant produces evidence that it is in the possessor's hand, then produces evidence that it is the claimant's property, then the judge rules and the claimant takes the house.
The conclusion: If it appears that it is in the hand of a third party and the possessor admitted that it is in his hand, the third party does not become subject to the judgment. Similarly, if it appears that the hand of the possessor is a trust hand, not a litigation hand. Al-Barjandi said: In it, the collusion suspicion in the case of admission is greater, because if the house is not in the hand of the admitter and he admitted possession, the judge's ruling does not cause him harm, unlike if it is in his hand as a trust; then if judgment is given against him and it is taken from him, he suffers harm. So this is farther from suspicion than the first. End. [11] His saying, 'For if it is in his hand... etc.'; It is objected to him that it is possible that it is in his hand as a trust, a pledge, or detained for the recovery of the price.
And it is answered that this possibility exists after producing evidence, and in movable property as well; therefore it is not considered, especially since it is unlikely because the harm to the defendant is due to the claim of the trustee, pledgee, or buyer.
[2] His saying, 'For harm... etc.'; It has been objected: The place of caution is not the occurrence of harm that is refuted by what was mentioned. Rather, the corruption is the occurrence of the reversal of the judgment when it appears in the hand of a third party. This is explicitly stated in the commentaries of al-Hidayah and others, and there is something in it, so reflect.

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the requirements for establishing possession (yad) in claims over immovable property (aqar) versus movable property (manqul). The text primarily expounds the ruling from al-Hidayah: for immovable property, mere admission (tasdiq) by the defendant that the property is in his hand is insufficient; rather, evidence (bayyinah) or the judge's personal knowledge ('ilm al-qadi) is required to establish possession. The reason given is to negate the suspicion of collusion (tuhmat al-muwada'ah) between the claimant and defendant, because immovable property may actually be in the hand of a third party, unlike movable property where the hand is observable. The author clarifies that some Hanafi scholars (certain mashayikh) hold that admission suffices, arguing that if the property is in the defendant's hand, the claimant can take it after proving ownership; if not, no harm is done. They also note that collusion can occur even with evidence—e.g., if the defendant holds the property as a trust (amanah) but colludes not to admit it, then the claimant brings evidence of possession and ownership. The text then rebuts this: the harm in the admission scenario is to a third party who may be the actual possessor, and the ruling could be overturned if the property is found in a third party's hand. Al-Barjandi adds that the collusion risk is higher in admission because the admitter may not actually possess the property, so the judge's ruling harms no one (if he admits falsely) whereas in the trust scenario, the defendant actually suffers harm. The passage concludes with further objections and responses, emphasizing that the real concern is not merely harm but the potential reversal of the judgment (naqd al-qada') when the property appears in a third party's hand. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is that of al-Hidayah, requiring evidence or judge's knowledge.

Key Terms
تهمة المواضعة (tuhmat al-muwāḍaʿah): The suspicion that the claimant and defendant have colluded (tawāḍaʿā) to fabricate a claim or admission to obtain a judgment that harms a third party.
العقار (al-ʿaqār): Immovable property (real estate), such as land and buildings.
المنقول (al-manqūl): Movable property, i.e., items that can be moved without damage.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does al-Hidayah require evidence or the judge's knowledge to establish possession in claims over immovable property, rather than relying on the defendant's admission?
A) Because immovable property cannot be physically moved to court.
B) To negate the suspicion of collusion between claimant and defendant, since immovable property may be in the hands of a third party.
C) Because movable property is more valuable and thus requires stricter proof.
D) Because the claimant's statement is always more trustworthy than the defendant's admission.

Key Takeaway
For immovable property, possession cannot be established by the defendant's admission alone; evidence or the judge's knowledge is required to prevent collusion and protect absent third parties.

Ruling When Defendant Admits Possession but Object is with Third Party
Explaining the effect of admission of possession when object is not in hand

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a judgment based on admission does not bind a third party when the object is not in the admitter's hand, and identify the legal reasoning behind this ruling.

Core Idea
A judgment binds only the parties before the court; a third party holding the object is not bound by the defendant's admission.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya discusses a nuanced scenario in qada: the defendant admits the disputed property is in his possession, but it is actually in the hand of a third party. The ruling clarifies that the plaintiff cannot seize it from the third party even with evidence, because the third party was not a litigant. This lesson will walk through the reasoning, including the handling of collusion suspicion and the objections raised by later commentators.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Determine what happens when the defendant admits possession but the object is with a third party. The text states the plaintiff cannot take it from the third party even with evidence.
This is the starting point: the third party is not bound.
2
Trace the key legal principle
Why is the third party not bound? Because the evidence is against a non-litigant (ghayr khasm). The judgment only affects the parties before the court.
This principle limits the effect of judgments to those who had a chance to defend.
3
Analyze the suspicion of collusion
The text addresses tuhmat al-muwada'a (collusion). It argues that if suspicion exists here, it also exists when evidence is produced, so it is not a distinguishing factor. Al-Birjandi adds that in the admission case, the suspicion is greater because the admitter suffers no harm.
Collusion is considered but does not automatically invalidate the ruling.
4
Evaluate the objections
Two objections are raised: (1) the object might be in the admitter's hand as a trust or pledge, and (2) the real issue is not harm but potential nullification of judgment. The answers dismiss these as far-fetched or already covered.
These objections show the depth of Hanafi internal critique.
5
Synthesize the final conclusion
Combine the above: the third party is not bound, but the plaintiff may have recourse against the admitter for damages based on his admission.
The ruling protects third parties while preserving the admitter's liability.

[٢) قوله : فإن الضرر... الخ ؛ وقد أورد عليه : إن محل الاحتياط ليس لزوم الضرر حتى يدفع بما ذكر، بل الفساد لزوم نقض القضاء عند ظهوره في يد ثالث. صرح به في شروح «الهداية»، وغيرها، وفيه ما فيه، فتأمل.
If it is in his hand and he admits that, then the plaintiff takes it from him if his ownership is established by evidence or by the admission of the possessor or his refusal to swear. But if it is not in his hand and he admits that, the plaintiff does not have the authority to take it from the possessor, even if the plaintiff produces evidence, because the evidence is against a non-litigant. So it is known that when the possessor admits possession, harm only befalls the possessor and does not befall others, so the suspicion of collusion is repelled. However, if the suspicion of collusion is established here, it is also established in the case of producing evidence, for if a house is in a person's hand as a trust, and the plaintiff and the possessor collude such that the possessor does not say it is a trust in his hand, until the plaintiff produces evidence that it is in the possessor's hand, then produces evidence that it is the plaintiff's property, then the judge rules and the plaintiff takes the house. The conclusion: if it appears that it is in the hand of a third party, and the possessor admitted it is in his hand, the third party does not become a party against whom judgment is made. Similarly, if it appears that the hand of the possessor is a hand of trust, not a hand of litigation. Al-Birjandi said: In it, the suspicion of collusion is greater in the case of admission, for if the house is not in the hand of the admitter and he admitted possession, the judge's judgment does not cause him harm. Unlike if it is in his hand as a trust, for if judgment is made against him and it is taken out of his hand, he is harmed. So this is further from suspicion than the first. And it is answered: This possibility exists also after producing evidence, and in what is transmitted as well; so it is not paid attention to, especially since it is far-fetched, because the harm to the defendant is due to the claim of the owner of the trust, the pledgor, or the buyer. Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of a ruling from the matn. It discusses a scenario where the defendant admits the disputed object is in his possession, but it is actually in the hand of a third party. The ruling is that the plaintiff cannot take it from the third party even with evidence, because the evidence is against a non-litigant. The harm of the judgment only affects the defendant. The suspicion of collusion (tuhmat al-muwada'a) is addressed by noting that in both admission and evidence scenarios, the suspicion exists. Al-Birjandi adds that in the admission scenario the suspicion is greater because the admitter suffers no harm if the object is not in his hand. An objection is raised that the object might be in his hand as a trust, pledge, or detained. The answer is that this possibility also exists after evidence and is far-fetched because the actual harm would be to the rightful owner. Another objection says the real issue is not harm but potential nullification of judgment if the object is found with a third party. This shows the depth of Hanafi juristic analysis.

Key Terms
يد (yad): Possession or control over an object, central to establishing the defendant's liability.
مواضعة (muwada'a): Collusion between plaintiff and defendant to deprive a rightful owner.
بينة (bayyina): Legal evidence, typically testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if the defendant admits the disputed object is in his possession, but it is actually in the hand of a third party?
A) The third party loses the object to the plaintiff.
B) The plaintiff can take the object from the third party with evidence.
C) The third party is not bound by the judgment and the plaintiff cannot take it.
D) The defendant must bring the object to court.

Key Takeaway
A defendant's admission of possession does not bind a third party who holds the object, as the third party is not a litigant before the court.

Three Conditions for a Valid Claim: Demand, Presentation, and Value
Defining three conditions for a valid claim with expansion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for a valid claim under Hanafi fiqh and explain the rationale behind each condition.

Core Idea
A claim must be demanded, presented (if possible), or valued (if absent) – clarity is the foundation of judicial justice.

In Hanafi judicial procedure, a valid claim (da'wa) requires more than just stating a right. The plaintiff must demand the object, present it in court if feasible, or specify its value if it is absent. This lesson unpacks each condition from the classical text of al-Wiqaya and its commentary, showing how they prevent uncertainty and enable enforcement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions in the matn
Read the matn: 'Wa al-mutalabatu bihi wa ihdaruhu in amkana, wa dhikru qimatihi in ta'adhara'. These are demand, presentation, and value.
They are listed conjunctively as conditions for validity.
2
Analyze the demand condition (mutalaba)
The commentary explains demand is needed because the object might be held as a pledge or for a price; without demand, the judge cannot compel. Also, judicial compulsion requires a specific request.
The reason is to remove the possibility of a non-demand situation where the claim is not contested.
3
Analyze the presentation condition (ihdar)
Presentation allows the plaintiff, witness, and oath-taker to point to the object. If impossible (heavy, costly), the judge may go to the object. The condition is limited to what is feasible.
The text gives examples: musk/saffron require presentation; a herd of sheep or millstone may not.
4
Analyze the value condition (dhikr al-qima)
When the object is destroyed or absent, its value must be stated so the claim becomes known. Value plus description substitutes for physical presence.
This ensures the claim is definite and prevents future disputes over quantum.
5
Synthesize the legal effect
All three conditions aim to make the claim determinate (ma'lum) and actionable by the judge. Any omission invalidates the claim unless an excuse applies.
The claim is invalid (ghayr sahih) if any condition is missing without a valid excuse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

(وذكر قيمته إن تعدر ٩٧ [١) قوله : عطف على قوله : وإنّه في يد المدعي عليه ؛ ويحتمل أن يكون عطفاً على قوله : ذكر شيء ؛ أي الدعوى إنّما تصح بمطالبة المدعي ما ادعاه من المدعى عليه، وإنما اشترط ذلك ؛ لاحتمال أن يكون مرهوناً في يده، وبالمطالبة يزولُ هذا الاحتمال. فإنه لو كان مرهوناً أو محبوساً بالثمن لا يطالب بالانتزاع من ذي اليد ؛ ولأنَّ فائدة الدعوى إجبار القاضى المدعى عليه على إيفاء حق المدعي، وذا لا يجوز للقاضي إلا إذا طالبه به فامتنع . [٢) قوله : وإحضاره ... الخ ؛ عطف على قوله : ذكر شيء ؛ يعني إِنَّ الدعوى إنّما تصح بإحضار المدعى عليه ما يدعيه المدعي مجلس الحكم إذا ثبت اليد، إن أمكن إحضاره بأن لا يكون له حمل ومؤنة، كالمسك والزّعفران، يشير إليه المدعي عند الدعوى، والشاهدُ عند أداء الشهادة، والحالف عند الحلف. وإن لم يمكن بأن يكون له حمل بأن يكون بحال لا يحمله إنسان إلى مجلس القاضي إلا بالأجرة، أو لا يمكن رفعه بيد واحدة، أو يختلف سعره في البلدان على الخلاف لم يجبر على الإحضار، فإن كان قطيعاً من الغنم، أو صبرة من الطعام، أو كرحى لا يجبر على الإحضار. بل للقاضي أن يحضر بنفسيه أو يبعث أميناً ليسمع الدعوى والبينة ويقضي، وإنما اشترط الإحضار إن أمكن ؛ لأنَّ الإعلام بأقصى شرط ، وذلك بالإشارة في المنقول ؛ لأنَّ النقل ممكن، والإشارة أبلغ في التعريف. [٣]قوله : وذكر قيمته إن تعذر ؛ يعني إنما تصحُ الدعوى بذكرِ قيمة ما يدعيه إن تعدرَ إحضاره بأن كان هالكاً أو غائباً ليصير ما يدعيه معلوماً بها ؛ لأنَّ الغائب لا يعرفُ إلا بالوصف والقيمة
And demanding it, and presenting it if possible, and mentioning its value if it cannot be presented. (And demanding it) is conjoined to his statement: 'and that it is in the hand of the defendant', and (presenting it if possible) so that the plaintiff, witness, and oath-taker may point to it. (And mentioning its value if it cannot be presented). [1] His statement: conjoined to his statement: 'and that it is in the hand of the defendant'; it is also possible it is conjoined to his statement: 'mention of something'; meaning the claim is only valid by the plaintiff demanding what he claims from the defendant. This condition is stipulated because of the possibility that it is pledged in his hand, and by demanding this possibility is removed. For if it were pledged or detained for the price, he would not demand its seizure from the possessor. And because the benefit of the claim is that the judge compels the defendant to fulfill the plaintiff's right, and the judge cannot do that unless the plaintiff demands it and he refuses. [2] His statement: 'and presenting it... etc.' conjoined to his statement: 'mention of something'; meaning the claim is only valid by the defendant presenting what the plaintiff claims in the court session if possession is established, if it is possible to present it, i.e., it does not have a burden or cost, like musk and saffron, so that the plaintiff points to it at the time of claim, the witness at the time of testimony, and the oath-taker at the time of oath. If it is not possible, i.e., it has a burden such that a person cannot carry it to the judge's court except for a fee, or cannot be lifted with one hand, or its price varies in different cities according to the disagreement, he is not compelled to present it. If it is a herd of sheep, a heap of food, or a millstone, he is not compelled to present it. Rather, the judge may go himself or send a trustee to hear the claim and evidence and give judgment. The condition of presenting if possible is stipulated because notification is the maximum condition, and that is by pointing in the case of movable property, because transportation is possible, and pointing is more effective in identification. [3] His statement: 'and mentioning its value if it cannot be presented'; meaning the claim is only valid by mentioning the value of what he claims if presenting it is not possible, i.e., it is destroyed or absent, so that what he claims becomes known by it; because the absent thing is only known by description and value.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is an expansion probe on the matn of al-Wiqaya, defining the conditions for a valid legal claim (da'wa). The matn lists three requirements: 1) demanding the object (mutalaba), 2) presenting it in court if possible (ihdar), and 3) stating its value if it cannot be presented (dhikr al-qima). The commentator explains each. Demand is required because the judge cannot compel the defendant without a specific demand, and it also clarifies that the object is not merely held as a pledge or security. Presentation is required for the plaintiff, witness, and oath-taker to point to the object. If the object is heavy or has transport cost, it need not be brought; instead the judge may go to the location. Stating the value is required when the object is lost or absent, so the claim becomes known by description and value. This ensures clarity and prevents injustice.

Key Terms
مطالبة (mutalaba): The plaintiff's formal demand for the claimed object from the defendant in court.
إحضار (ihdar): Presenting the claimed object in court so it can be pointed to by plaintiff, witness, and oath-taker.
قيمة (qima): The monetary value of the claimed object mentioned in the claim when the object is absent or destroyed.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is demand (mutalaba) required for a valid claim?
A) To prevent the claim from being a gift.
B) To remove the possibility that the object is held as a pledge or for a price, and to enable the judge to compel.
C) To allow the defendant to prepare a defense.
D) To ensure the plaintiff is sincere.

Key Takeaway
A valid claim requires demand, presentation (if feasible), or value (if absent) – ensuring the claim is clear and enforceable.
