Three Conditions for a Valid Claim (Daw'aa)
States and explains three prerequisites for a legal claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three conditions for a valid claim in Hanafi law and explain the reasoning behind each condition.

Core Idea
A claim is valid only when the claimant demands the item, produces it if possible, and states its value if it's absent.

In this passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya, the commentator explains the three prerequisites for a legal claim to be heard. These conditions ensure that the claim is specific, the defendant can respond, and the judge can adjudicate. Understanding them is essential for anyone involved in litigation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three conditions
Read the matn (base text): the claim requires demand (mutalaba), production (ihdar) if possible, and value statement (qima) if the item is absent.
These are conditions for the validity (sihha) of the claim, not mere recommendations.
2
Understand the rationale for demand
The commentator explains that demand removes the possibility that the item is held as collateral or for a price. Without demand, the judge cannot compel the defendant.
The demand distinguishes a claim from a mere statement; if the item is mortgaged, the demand removes the likelihood of alternative ownership.
3
Understand the rationale for production
Production (ihdar) allows the claimant, witness, and oath-taker to point to the item. If the item is heavy or costly to transport, the judge goes to the item instead.
Production is only required if possible (i.e., portable items like musk or saffron). For heavy items (e.g., flock of sheep, millstone), the judge inspects on site.
4
Understand the rationale for value
If the item is absent or destroyed, its value must be stated to make the claim specific. The value requirement also applies to witnesses.
There is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf: al-Bazdawi says the judge should ask for value but still hear the claim if not stated; some restrict value to theft claims to establish nisab.
5
Note exceptions and ikhtilaf
Production is excused if impossible; value may not be enforced if the claimant refuses after being asked. The relied-upon view is that all three conditions generally apply.
The commentary does not give a definitive tarjih but presents the matn broadly.

Source Text

The book of claims: its definition and rulings. And demanding it and bringing it if possible, and mentioning its value if it is difficult (to bring). (And demanding it): a conjunction to his statement: 'and that it is in the hand of the defendant'. And bringing it if possible) so that the claimant, witness, and oath-taker can point to it. (And mentioning its value if it is difficult) [1) His statement: a conjunction to his statement: 'and that it is in the hand of the defendant'; and it is possible that it is a conjunction to his statement: 'mentioning something'; i.e., the claim is only valid by the claimant demanding what he claims from the defendant. For if it were mortgaged or detained for the price, he is not required to demand its retrieval from the possessor; and because the benefit of the claim is to compel the judge to make the defendant fulfill the claimant's right, and that is not permissible for the judge except if he demanded it and he refused. [2) His statement: And bringing it... etc.; conjunction to his statement: 'mentioning something'; meaning the claim is only valid by the defendant bringing what the claimant claims to the court if possession is established, if bringing it is possible, i.e., it does not involve heavy burden and expense, like musk and saffron; the claimant points to it at the time of claim, the witness at the time of testimony, and the oath-taker at the time of oath. If it is not possible, meaning it is heavy such that a person cannot carry it to the judge except with payment, or cannot be lifted with one hand, or its price varies in different lands, he is not compelled to bring it. If it is a flock of sheep, a pile of grain, or a millstone, he is not compelled to bring it. Rather, the judge may go himself or send a trustee to hear the claim and evidence and give judgment. The condition of bringing it is imposed only if possible, because notification is the extreme condition, and that is by pointing in moveable property, because transport is possible, and pointing is more effective in identification. [3] His statement: And mentioning its value if it is difficult; meaning the claim is only valid by mentioning the value of what he claims if bringing it is difficult, i.e., it is destroyed or absent, so that what he claims becomes known thereby; because the absent thing is only known by description and value. (1) See: 'Majma' al-Anhur' (2:251). So if the requirement of stating the value is dropped for the claimant, it is also dropped for the witnesses, even more so because they are less familiar with it. And al-Allamah al-Bazdawi said: If the issue is disputed, the judge should order the claimant to state the value; if he orders him and he does not state it, his claim is still heard. And it is said: Mentioning the value is only required when claiming theft, to know that it was of nisab amount; otherwise, it is not required. Thus in 'al-Fusul' and others.
Source pages p. 97

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with explanation from the commentary on al-Wiqayah concerning the conditions for a valid claim (da'wa) in Hanafi law. The author first states the matn: a claim requires three things: (1) demanding the item from the defendant, (2) bringing it to court if possible, and (3) mentioning its value if it cannot be produced. The commentary then explains each condition. First, demanding the item is necessary because the defendant might be holding it as collateral or for a price; the demand removes this possibility, and the judge cannot compel the defendant without prior demand. Second, bringing the item is required only if it is easily transportable, like musk or saffron; if it is heavy or expensive to move, the judge goes himself or sends a deputy. The purpose is identification by pointing; for moveables, pointing suffices. Third, if the item is destroyed or absent, its value must be stated to make the claim known, as absent things are described by value. The commentary adds that if the claimant is excused from stating value, witnesses are excused even more. Al-Bazdawi suggests that in a disputed matter, the judge should ask for value but still hear the claim if not stated. Some say value is only required in theft claims to establish nisab.

Key Terms
المطالبة (al-mutalaba): Demanding the claimed item from the defendant; a condition for the validity of the claim.
الإحضار (al-ihdar): Bringing the claimed item to court if it is movable and easily transportable; for heavy items the judge goes instead.
القيمة (al-qima): The monetary value of the claimed item; must be stated if the item is absent or destroyed to specify the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the primary reason for requiring the claimant to demand the item from the defendant?
A) To ensure the defendant is aware of the claim
B) To remove the possibility that the item is mortgaged in the defendant's possession
C) To allow the judge to issue a ruling immediately
D) To determine the item's value

Key Takeaway
A claim is valid only when the claimant demands the item, produces it if possible, and states its value if it is absent.

Boundaries in Real Estate Claims: Abu Hanifa vs. Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on boundaries in real estate claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi requirement for mentioning boundaries in real estate claims and distinguish the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
A real estate claim requires boundaries even for famous houses because a house can change — but the Sahibayn disagree.

In Hanafi claim procedure, real estate claims have special requirements because land cannot be brought to court. The matn of al-Wiqayah specifies that the claimant must state the boundaries and the names of adjacent owners with their lineages. However, there is a well-known debate within the school: does this apply to a house everyone knows? This lesson explores that debate, its proofs, and its practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling from the matn: for real estate claims, the claimant must mention the four boundaries (or three) and the names and lineages of the adjacent owners. This is the position attributed to Abu Hanifa.
The matn requirement is unconditional.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) hold that boundaries are not required if the property is well-known (mashhura). Their view is a relaxation based on fame removing ambiguity.
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
The Sahibayn argue that a famous house is known by its fame, so boundaries are unnecessary. Fame provides sufficient identification.
The proof is based on the premise that ambiguity is removed.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response
Abu Hanifa responds that even a famous house may be enlarged or reduced over time, so boundaries are still needed for precise identification. Fame does not guarantee current dimensions.
The talil (illah) is the possibility of change.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if a claimant sues for a famous house, a judge following Abu Hanifa will require boundaries; a judge following the Sahibayn will accept the claim without boundaries.
The mufta bihi position: boundaries are generally required, but for well-known properties the Sahibayn's view may be followed.

Source Text

[1]قوله : والحدود الأربعة أو الثلاثةِ في العقار، وأسماء أصحابها ونسبتهم إلى الجد والحدود الأربعة أو الثلاثةِ في العقار، وأسماء أصحابها ونسبتهم إلى الجد) : ذكر الحدود يشترط في دعـوى الـدار عند أبي حنيفة له وإن كانت مشهورة ، وعندهما : لا يشترط إذا كانت مشهورة ، ثمَّ ذكرُ الحدودِ الثَّلاثةِ كاف عندنا وقال الفقيه أبو الليث الله : يشترط مع ذلك في الحيوان الذكورة والأنوثة في الدابة، وإن لم يبين القيمة ، فقال : غصب منّي عيناً كذا ، أو لا أدري أنه ذلك، أو قائم ولا أدري كم كانت قيمته. قال في «الكافي»: ذُكِرَ في عامة الكتب أنه يسمع دعواه ؛ لأنَّ الإنسان ربما لا يعرف قيمة ماله ، فلو كُلّف بيان القيمة لتضرّر به، وعزاه إلى القاضي فخر الدين، وإلى صاحب «الذخيرة». [1]قوله : والحدود... الخ، عطف على قوله : قيمته ؛ أي إنّما تصح الدعوى بذكرِ الحدود الأربعة أو الحدود الثلاثةِ إذا كان المدعى عقاراً، وبذكر أسماء أصحاب تلك الحدود ونسبتهم إلى الجد. أما اشتراط ذكر الحدود ؛ فلأنَّ العقار تعدَّرَ تعريفه بالإشارة ؛ ليتعدَّرَ نقله إلى مجلس الحكم، فلا بُدَّ من التَّحديد ، إذ العقار يعرفُ به، وذكر أسماء أصحاب الحدود ؛ لأنَّ التعريف يحصلُ بذلك ، وذكر أنسابهم ليتميّزوا عن غيرهم، ونسبتهم إلى الجد ؛ لأنَّ تمام التعريف يحصل به في الصحيح من مذهب الإمام، وهذا إذا لم يكن مشهوراً، وفي الرجل المشهور يكتفى بذكرِهِ لحصول المقصود به. عند [٢] قوله : ذكر الحدود... الخ ؛ يعني أنَّ ذكر الحدودِ يشترط في دعوى الدَّارِ الإمام، سواء كانت الدار مشهورة أو غير مشهورة ؛ لأنَّ الدَّارَ وإن كانت مشهورة قد تزاد وقد تنقص فتبقى مجهولةً ، فلا بُدَّ من ذكر حدودها ؛ لحصول التعريف، وعندهما: لا يشترط إذا كانت مشهورة.
[1] His statement: 'And the four boundaries or three in real estate, and the names of their owners and their lineage to the grandfather, and the four boundaries or three in real estate, and the names of their owners and their lineage to the grandfather': Mentioning boundaries is required in a claim for a house according to Abu Hanifa, even if it is well-known; according to the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is not required if it is well-known. Then, mentioning three boundaries is sufficient according to us. And al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: Additionally, for animals, mentioning the gender (male/female) of the animal is required, even if the value is not stated. He said: He said: 'He usurped from me such-and-such a thing', or 'I do not know whether it is that', or 'it exists and I do not know its value.' He said in al-Kafi: It is mentioned in most books that his claim is heared because a person may not know the value of his property; if he were asked to state the value, he would be harmed. And this is attributed to al-Qadi Fakhr al-Din and to the author of al-Dhakhira. [1] His statement: 'And the boundaries... etc.' is a conjunction to his statement 'its value'; i.e., the claim is only valid with mention of the four boundaries or the three boundaries if the claimed property is real estate, and with mention of the names of the owners of those boundaries and their lineage to the grandfather. As for the requirement of mentioning boundaries, it is because real estate is difficult to define by pointing, and difficult to transport to the courtroom, so specification (tahdid) is necessary, for real estate is known by it. And mentioning the names of the boundary owners because identification is achieved by that. And mentioning their lineages to distinguish them from others. And their lineage to the grandfather because complete identification is achieved by that in the correct view of the Imam. This is when the property is not well-known; for a well-known man (or property) it suffices to mention his name to achieve the purpose. [2] His statement: 'Mentioning boundaries... etc.'; meaning that mentioning boundaries is required in a claim for a house according to the Imam, whether the house is well-known or not, because the house, even if well-known, may be enlarged or reduced and thus remain unknown, so boundaries must be mentioned for identification. According to the two disciples, it is not required if it is well-known.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the conditions for a valid claim involving real estate (aqar). The matn states that for real estate, the claim must mention the four boundaries (or three) and the names and lineages of the adjacent owners. The author then notes a disagreement: Abu Hanifa requires boundaries even if the property is well-known, because a known house can change (be extended or reduced). The two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) do not require boundaries if the property is famous. All agree that three boundaries suffice. Additionally, a view from Abu al-Layth says that for animals, gender must be mentioned. The passage also discusses that if the claimant does not know the value, his claim should still be heard, as cited from al-Kafi. Then the commentary elaborates the rationale: real estate cannot be brought to court or pointed to, so boundaries and neighbor names are needed for identification. Lineage is needed to distinguish neighbors. The Imam's position is that boundaries are always required; the two disciples relax this for well-known properties.

Key Terms
الحدود (al-hudud): Boundaries of a property, typically north, south, east, west; three boundaries suffice.
العقار (al-'aqar): Immovable property, like land or buildings, which cannot be brought to court.
نسبة إلى الجد (nisba ila al-jadd): Tracing lineage to the grandfather to identify neighbors clearly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, why must boundaries be mentioned even for a well-known house?
A) Because the judge does not know the house
B) Because a well-known house may be enlarged or reduced, causing ambiguity
C) Because three boundaries are not sufficient
D) Because the value must be determined

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi real estate claims, boundaries are generally required for identification, but the Sahibayn waive this for well-known properties due to fame removing ambiguity.

Boundaries in Property Claims: The Intra-Hanafi Dispute
An intra-Hanafi dispute on boundary requirements in da'wa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa, the Sahibayn, and Zufar on boundary specification in da'wa for real estate and apply the relied-upon Hanafi ruling.

Core Idea
Three boundaries suffice; the fourth can be inferred as a straight line.

When claiming ownership of real estate in court, how precisely must the property be identified? The Hanafi school has a nuanced internal debate. While all agree that for non-famous property boundaries are required, the points of contention are whether fame dispenses with boundaries and how many boundaries are needed. This lesson walks through the positions of the leading Hanafi imams and the relied-upon ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core issue
The dispute centers on whether specification of boundaries is required for claimed real estate, especially if the property is well-known (mashhurah). Also, how many boundaries must be mentioned?
The passage also addresses lineage requirements for adjacent owners.
2
State the position of Abu Hanifa
Abu Hanifa holds that boundaries are always required, even for famous property, because fame may change. Moreover, three boundaries suffice because the fourth can be inferred.
He also requires lineage to the grandfather for owners.
3
State the positions of the Sahibayn and Zufar
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say no boundaries needed if the property is well-known. Zufar goes further: all four boundaries must be mentioned; three are insufficient.
The Sahibayn also differ on lineage: Abu Yusuf does not require grandfather.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response to Zufar
The author argues that three boundaries give enough identification; the fourth is a geometric inference. An error in the fourth would change the object, but omitting it does not cause error. The majority has the ruling of the whole.
Al-Khassaf provides a practical method to determine the fourth boundary.
5
Determine the mufta bihi (relied-upon ruling)
The commentator supports Abu Hanifa's view that three boundaries suffice and lineage to grandfather is required. This is the position for fatwa.
For famous property, the Sahibayn's view (no boundaries) is also considered; but the text seems to lean towards Abu Hanifa's caution.

And the four boundaries or three in real estate, and the names of their owners and their lineage to the grandfather: Mentioning boundaries is required in a claim of a house according to Abu Hanifa, even if it is well-known. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), it is not required if it is well-known. Then mentioning three boundaries is sufficient for us. And Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: additionally, in animals, the male and female of the beast must be stated. And if he does not state the value, he says: He usurped from me such an item, or I do not know if it is that, or it is present and I do not know its value. It is stated in 'al-Kafi' that in most books it is mentioned that his claim is accepted because a person may not know the value of his property, and if he were required to state the value he would be harmed. This is attributed to Judge Fakhr al-Din and the author of 'al-Dhakhira'. So if stating the value is waived for the claimant, it is also waived for the witnesses, indeed more so because they are further from practice. And Allama al-Bazdawi said: If the issue is disputed, the judge should require the claimant to state the value; if he requires him and he does not state it, his claim is heard. And it is said that stating the value is only required if he claims theft, to know that it was the nisab, and in other cases it is not required. This is in 'al-Fusul' and others. His statement: 'And the boundaries...' etc., is connected to his statement: 'its value'; i.e., the claim is valid only by mentioning the four boundaries or the three if the claimed property is real estate, and by mentioning the names of the owners of those boundaries and their lineage to the grandfather. As for the requirement to mention boundaries, it is because real estate cannot be defined by pointing due to the impossibility of transporting it to the court, so specification is necessary, as real estate is known by it. And mentioning the names of the owners is because definition is achieved by it, and mentioning their lineages to distinguish them from others, and their lineage to the grandfather because complete definition is achieved by it according to the correct opinion of the Imam. This is if it is not well-known, and for a well-known man, it is sufficient to mention him because the purpose is achieved. According to his statement: 'Mentioning boundaries...' etc., means that mentioning boundaries is required in a claim of a house according to the Imam, whether the house is well-known or not, because the house, even if well-known, may be increased or decreased, so it remains unknown; thus boundaries must be mentioned for definition. According to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), contrary to Zufar, because if he mentions three boundaries as in this case, the fourth boundary is another straight line. And lineage to the grandfather is the statement of Abu Hanifa, and if a man is well-known, it suffices to mention him. This is in claims of physical objects. Mentioning boundaries is not required if it is well-known, because fame suffices instead. So it is known that the difference is about the requirement of specification in a well-known house. As for specifying other than that, it is obligatory by agreement. When the author said: 'And the four boundaries or three in real estate', he ended. And real estate is every fixed property with a foundation, such as a house, palm trees, and other instances of real estate. The condition is absolute without denial, but the situation is not like that, so the commentator clarified what is disputed, and it appears that what is beyond that is agreed upon. His statement: 'Contrary to Zufar' because according to him it is not sufficient; rather, mentioning the four boundaries is necessary because the definition is not complete with mentioning three, just as it is not complete with mentioning two. For this reason, if we say he erred in the fourth boundary, it is not accepted, and that is the statement of the three. And our evidence: the majority has the ruling of the whole, unlike the case where he errs in the fourth boundary because the claimed object changes with it, but not by omitting it. Also, if he mentions three boundaries as in the image mentioned in the book, the fourth boundary can be known without mentioning it, so there is no need for it. And every answer you know in the claim applies also in testimony. And it is mentioned in 'al-Bahr' quoting from 'al-Multaqit': al-Khassaf said: If you judge with three boundaries, make the fourth boundary go alongside the third until it meets the first, meaning straight. End. His statement: 'And lineage to the grandfather is the statement of Abu Hanifa' matches what is mentioned in 'al-Hidayah' and other reliable sources. However, al-Birjandi said in 'Sharh al-Nuqayah': 'And their lineage to the grandfather: definition is only complete by mentioning the grandfather according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf.' End. And we have verified it previously, so remember.
Source pages p. 98

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the requirements for a valid claim (da'wa) in court for real estate. The author, from the commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah', explains the condition of specifying boundaries and the names/lineage of adjacent owners. The core issue: how much detail is needed to properly identify the claimed property?

Abu Hanifa requires mentioning all four boundaries for a house, even if it is well-known, because a well-known house may change over time (added to or reduced), so boundaries are necessary. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) do not require boundaries if the house is well-known, as fame suffices. Zufar differs, requiring all four boundaries—even three is insufficient because the fourth cannot be inferred with certainty; error in the fourth invalidates the claim. The Hanafi response: three boundaries are sufficient because the fourth can be inferred geometrically (a straight line), and the majority has the ruling of the whole. This is supported by practical guidance: al-Khassaf suggests drawing the fourth boundary as a straight line opposite the third.

Regarding lineage: Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require mentioning lineage up to the grandfather for complete identification, while Abu Yusuf does not. This applies when the owner is not famous; if famous, mere naming suffices.

The passage also briefly discusses the requirement to state value in claims: generally not required unless it's a theft claim to verify the nisab. The judge may optionally request it if the issue is disputed, but failure to state does not invalidate the claim.

The commentary emphasizes that all these details are for da'wa (plaintiff's claim); the same rules apply for shahada (testimony).

Key Terms
دعوى (da'wa): A legal claim or lawsuit brought before a judge, asserting a right over property.
حدود (hudud): Boundaries or limits of a property that define its extent and location.
عقار (aqar): Immovable property, such as land, houses, and palm trees that are fixed and have a foundation.
نسبة إلى الجد (nisba ila al-jadd): Lineage traced to the grandfather, required for complete identification of adjacent property owners.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, how many boundaries must be specified in a claim for real estate that is not well-known?
A) Four boundaries
B) Three boundaries
C) Two boundaries
D) No boundaries needed

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi property claims, three boundaries suffice; the fourth is inferred, and lineage to grandfather is required for identification.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
6 min
Requirement of Boundaries in Property Claims
Hanafi ruling on mentioning boundaries in property claims with Zufar disagreement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the number of boundaries required in a property claim and explain the rationale for three sufficing, as well as identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement on mentioning the grandfather's name.

Core Idea
Three boundaries suffice; the fourth can be inferred. Fame dispenses with specification entirely.

When claiming ownership of real property, a plaintiff must identify the property clearly to avoid ambiguity. The Hanafi school requires boundaries only for unknown properties, and even then, three boundaries are sufficient because the fourth can be deduced from the other three. This differs from Zufar's view that all four must be explicitly stated. Additionally, when identifying a person, the grandfather's name may be needed according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, but not according to Abu Yusuf.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on boundaries
Identify the basic ruling: for well-known property, mentioning boundaries is not required (fame dispenses). For unknown property, boundaries are wajib. The Hanafis (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad) agree that three boundaries suffice.
Zufar's view is the exception within the Hanafi school on this issue.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam Zufar requires all four boundaries because definition is not complete with three. He argues that a mistake in the fourth would invalidate the claim.
Zufar is an early Hanafi scholar but here differs from the three imams.
3
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi proof (li-na) is twofold: (1) The majority (three boundaries) has the ruling of the whole; (2) If three are mentioned, the fourth can be inferred by drawing a straight line opposite the third, so explicit mention is unnecessary. Omission is not the same as error.
This reasoning is based on qiyas and practicality in judicial proceedings.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on lineage
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require mentioning the grandfather's name to identify a person when the name is common; Abu Yusuf does not require it. The relied-upon position is that of Abu Hanifa.
This dispute is separate from boundaries but arises in the same context of specification in claims.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
In practice, a claimant needs only mention three boundaries; the judge can infer the fourth. According to Zufar, all four must be explicitly stated, otherwise the claim is invalid. The Hanafi position facilitates lawsuits and reduces procedural hurdles.
The practical impact is significant: fewer claims rejected for technicality.

Source Text

خلافاً لزفر ؛ فإنَّهُ إذا ذكر ثلاثة حدودٍ كما في هذهِ الصُّورةِ فالحدُّ الرَّابِعُ خطّ مستقيم آخر ، والنِّسبة إلى الجد قول أبي حنيفة" وإن كان رجلاً مشهوراً يكتفي بذكره، هذا في دعوى الأعيان

لا يشترط ذكرُ الحدودِ إذا كانت مشهورة ؛ لاستغناء الشهرة عنه.

فعُلِمَ أنَّ الاختلاف في اشتراط التحديد في الدَّارِ المشتهرة، وأمَّا تحديد غيرِهـا فواجب اتفاقاً، ولما قال المصنف : والحدود الأربعة أو الثلاثة في العقار. انتهى. والعقار كل ملك ثابت له أصل كالدار والنخل وغيرهما من أفراد العقار شرط مطلق بلا نكير، والحال ليس كذلك ، فبين الشارح له ما اختلف فيه، وظهر منه أنَّ ما عداه فهو متفق عليه.

[1] قوله : خلافاً لزفر ؛ فإنَّ عنده غير كافي، بل لا بد من ذكر الحدود الأربعة ؛ لأنَّ التَّعريف لم يتم بذكرِ الثلاثة كما لا يتم بذكر الاثنين ؛ ولهذا لو قلنا : غلط في الحد الرابع لا تقبل، وبه قالت الثلاثة.

ولنا : إن للأكثر حكم الكلِّ ، بخلافِ ما لو غَلِطَ في الحد الرابع ؛ لأنه يختلف به المدعى، ولا كذلك تركها، وأيضاً إذا ذكر ثلاثة حدودٍ كما في الصورة المذكورة في الكتاب، فيمكن أن يعلم الحد الرابع بلا ذكره ، فلا حاجة إليه، وكلُّ جواب عرفته في الدعوى فهو في الشهادة أيضاً.

وذكر في «البحر»(۱) نقلاً عن «الملتقط»: قال الخصاف له : إذا قضيت بثلاثة حدود اجعل الحد الرابع يمضي بإزاء الحد الثالث، حتى يحاذي الحد الأول، يعني على الاستقامة. انتهى.

[٢] قوله : والنسبة إلى الجد قول أبي حنيفة ؛ هذا مطابق لما ذكره في «الهداية»(۲)، وغيرها من المعتبرات، لكن قال البرْجَندِيُّ في «شرح النُّقاية»: ونسبتهم إلى الجد، فإنَّ التعريف إنَّما يتم بذكرِ الجد عند أبي حنيفة ومحمد الله خلافاً لأبي يوسف . انتهى. وقد حققناه سابقاً فتذكر.
Contrary to Zufar, for when he mentions three boundaries as in this case, the fourth boundary is another straight line. The attribution to the grandfather is the statement of Abu Hanifa. And if a man is well-known, his mere mention suffices. This is in claims of real property (a'yan).

The mention of boundaries is not required if the property is well-known, because its fame dispenses with it.

So it is understood that the dispute is about the requirement of specification in a well-known house. As for specifying other than that, it is obligatory by agreement. And when the author said: "The four boundaries or three in real estate" – end. Real estate is every fixed property that has a foundation, like a house, palm trees, and other types of real estate – an absolute condition without denial, but the situation is not so. So the commentator explained the point of difference, and it became clear that what is other than that is agreed upon.

[1] His statement: "Contrary to Zufar; for according to him it is not sufficient, rather he must mention all four boundaries because the definition is not completed by mentioning three just as it is not completed by mentioning two. For this reason, if we say: he made a mistake in the fourth boundary, it is not accepted. And the three (imams) held this view.

Our proof: The majority has the ruling of the whole, unlike the case if he makes a mistake in the fourth boundary because the claimed property differs by it, and not so by omitting it. Also, if he mentions three boundaries as in the picture mentioned in the book, the fourth boundary can be known without mentioning it, so there is no need for it. And every answer you learn in claim (da'wa) applies also to testimony.

And it is mentioned in "Al-Bahr" quoting from "Al-Multaqit": Al-Khassaf said to him: When you judge by three boundaries, make the fourth boundary opposite the third boundary, aligned with the first boundary, meaning in a straight line. End.

[2] His statement: "And the attribution to the grandfather is the statement of Abu Hanifa"; this corresponds to what he mentioned in "Al-Hidayah" and other reliable works. But al-Barjandi said in "Sharh al-Nuqayah": Their attribution to the grandfather, for indeed the definition is only completed by mentioning the grandfather according to Abu Hanifa and Muhammad, contrary to Abu Yusuf. End. And we have verified this earlier, so remember.
Source pages p. 99

Commentary
This passage deals with the requirement of specifying boundaries in lawsuits concerning real property (da'wa 'ayn). The author clarifies that for well-known property, mentioning boundaries is not necessary because fame substitutes for specification. However, for unknown property, boundaries must be mentioned. The main dispute is with Imam Zufar, who insists on all four boundaries, reasoning that definition requires completion. The Hanafi response (li-na) is twofold: first, the majority of boundaries (three) take the ruling of the whole; second, if three are given, the fourth can be inferred, making express mention unnecessary. An additional intra-madhhab dispute among Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad is noted regarding the necessity of specifying the grandfather's name (al-jadd) in lineage-based identification. Abu Hanifa and Muhammad require it, while Abu Yusuf deems it unnecessary. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) follows Abu Hanifa's view as stated in Al-Hidayah.

Key Terms
حدود (hudud): Boundaries of a property that define its extent, e.g., north, south, east, west.
دعوى الأعيان (da'wa al-a'yan): Lawsuit concerning specific physical property (real estate) as opposed to debts.
الجد (al-jadd): Grandfather (paternal) in lineage, used to identify a person when name is common.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, how many boundaries must a claimant mention for an unknown piece of property?
A) All four boundaries.
B) Three boundaries; the fourth can be inferred.
C) Two boundaries are enough.
D) No boundaries are needed for any property.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts three boundaries as sufficient in property claims; the fourth is inferred, and fame dispenses with boundaries entirely.

Judge’s Duty Upon a Valid Claim: Questioning the Defendant
Explains the judge's duty to question the defendant upon a valid claim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the judge's duty upon a valid claim, distinguish between iqrar and bayyinah, and apply the Hanafi ruling on a defendant's ambiguous response.

Core Idea
A valid claim obligates the judge to question the defendant; admission binds without judgment, while evidence requires judicial pronouncement.

When a valid lawsuit is filed, the Hanafi judge has a clear duty: to ask the defendant about the claim. This simple step ensures the dispute is properly framed. More importantly, the outcome depends on whether the defendant admits, denies, or remains silent, and each triggers a different legal effect. This lesson unpacks the judge's procedure, the distinction between admission and evidence, and the Hanafi position on ambiguous responses like 'I neither admit nor deny.'

Guided Path
1
Identify the judge's duty upon a valid claim
The judge must ask the defendant about the claim; if the claim is invalid, no question is required.
2
Distinguish the legal nature of admission (iqrar) vs. evidence (bayyinah)
Admission (iqrar) is binding proof by itself; the judge's pronouncement is merely metaphorical. Evidence (bayyinah) only becomes binding through the judge's ruling, which removes the possibility of falsehood.
3
Classify the defendant's possible responses
If the defendant admits, the judge orders compliance. If he denies, the judge asks the plaintiff for evidence. If he says 'I neither admit nor deny,' this is considered denial.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on silence
Abu Yusuf holds that a silent defendant is imprisoned until he answers; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad do not require imprisonment. The fatwa follows Abu Yusuf in judicial matters.
5
Trace the proof from the Prophet's practice
The judge asks for evidence after denial, following the hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr reported by al-Bukhari and Muslim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى
وإذا صحت سأل القاضي الخصم عنها
قوله : سأل القاضي الخصم عنها ؛ أي عن دعواه ؛ ليتضح وجه حكمه ؛ لأنَّ القضاء بالبينة يخالف القضاء بالإقرار ؛ وهذا لأنَّ الإقرار حجة ملزمة بنفسه، ولا يحتاجُ فيه إلى القضاء، وإطلاق اسم القضاء فيه مجاز. وإنما هو أمر بالخروج عما لزمه بالإقرار بخلاف البينة، فإنَّها ليست بحجة إلا إذا اتصل بها القضاء، فيسقط احتمالُ الكذب بالقضاء في حق العمل، فتصير حجَّةً يجب العمل به كسائر الحجج الشرعية. ومعنى سؤاله، أن يقول : خصمُكَ ادَّعى عليك كذا وكذا، فماذا تقول؟ فإن لم تصدر الدعوى من المدعي صحيحة، لا يسألُ القاضي المدعى عليه لعدم وجوب الجواب عليه لها بخلاف الدعوى الصحيحة ؛ فإنه يجب عليه جوابها، وظاهر الكلام يدل على أن القاضى يسألُ الخصم عنها، وإن لم يطلمبه المدعي.
وفي «الفتاوى السراجيّة» : إذا حضر الخصمان لا بأس أن يقول : ما لكما ؟ وإن يبتدأه بالكلام، وإذا تكلّم المدَّعي سكت الآخر، ويسمع مقالته، فإذا فرغ يقول للمدعى عليه : بطلب المدعي ماذا تقول؟ وقيل : إنَّ المدعي إن كان جاهلاً فإنَّ القاضي يسألُ المدعى عليه بدون طلب المدعي. انتهى.
وقال البرْجَندِيُّ في «شرح النُّقاية»: وفي هذا الكلام إشارة خفية إلى أنَّ القاضي يختار السكوت بعد إتيان الخصمين إليه حتى يبتدئ المدعي بالكلام، والأظهر أنه مخير إن شاء تركهما حتى يتكلما ، وإن شاء ابتدأهما بالكلام. وقال : ما لكما ؟ لكن لا يكلمهما بشيءٍ آخر، وتصريح بأنه إذا تم دعواه استنطق الآخر، وإن لم يلتمس المدعي ذلك من القاضي، واختار بعض القضاة أن لا يستنطقه إلا إذا قال المدعي للقاضي سله عن جوابه، فحينئذ يستنطقه، والأول أصح. كذا في «المبسوط». انتهى.
قوله : حكم ؛ على الخصم : يعني أنَّ القاضي يحكم بالخروج عن موجب ما أقرَّ به ؛ لأنَّ الإقرار حجة بنفسه فلا يتوقف في صدقِهِ على الحكم من القاضي، فكان الأولى أن يقول : فإن أقر فيها فلفظ الحكم في الإقرارِ مجاز للزومِه بإقراره فلا حاجة إلى القضاء لكونه حجة بنفسه لا يتوقف على القضاء بخلاف البينة ، فإنَّ الشهادة غيرُ حجة ، وإنما تصير حجة بالقضاء، وسقط احتمال الكذب.
قوله : أو أنكر الخصم إنكاراً صريحاً أو غير صريح، كما إذا قال : لا أقرّ ولا أنكر ، فإنّه إنكار عندهم، وما روي أنه إقرارٌ غيرُ ظاهر، فيحبس حتى يقر فغلط على ما أشير إليه في «المنية». كذا في «جامع الرموز».
وقال السرخسي : وعند أبي يوسف الله يحبس إلى أن يجيب، وقال في «البحر» : والفتوى على قول أبي يوسف له فيما يتعلق بالقضاء، كما في «القنية»، و«البَزَّازيّة» ؛ فلذا أفتيت بأنه يحبس إلى أن يجيب. انتهى.
قوله : وسأل... الخ ؛ يعني أنّ القاضي يسأل المدعي البينة على مدعاة، وإنما يبدأ بالسؤال عن البينة ؛ لأنها الأصل في قطع الخصومة، وبه بدأ رسولُ الله ، كما أخرج الشيخان عن وائل بن ... الحديث.
Book of Claims. When the claim is valid, the judge asks the opponent about it. He said: 'The judge asks the opponent about it' i.e., about his claim, so that the basis of his judgment becomes clear, because judgment based on evidence differs from judgment based on admission. This is because admission is a binding proof by itself and does not require judgment, and the use of the word 'judgment' for it is metaphorical. It is merely an order to relinquish what he has admitted, unlike evidence, which is not proof unless judgment is connected to it, thus the possibility of falsehood is removed by judgment in practical terms, so it becomes a proof that must be acted upon like other legal proofs. The meaning of his question is that he says: 'Your opponent has claimed such-and-such from you, what do you say?' If the claim made by the plaintiff is not valid, the judge does not ask the defendant because answering it is not obligatory, unlike a valid claim, for which answering is obligatory. The apparent wording indicates that the judge asks the opponent about it even if the plaintiff does not request it. In al-Fatawa al-Sirajiyya: When the two opponents are present, it is permissible for him to say: 'What is between you?' and he may initiate speech, and when the plaintiff speaks, the other remains silent, and he listens to his statement, then when he finishes, he says to the defendant: 'The plaintiff asks: What do you say?' It is said: If the plaintiff is ignorant, the judge asks the defendant without the plaintiff's request. End. Al-Birjandi said in Sharh al-Niqaya: In this speech there is a subtle indication that the judge chooses silence after the two opponents come to him until the plaintiff begins to speak, but the apparent view is that he has a choice: if he wishes, he leaves them until they speak, and if he wishes, he initiates speech with them. He said: 'What is between you?' but he should not speak to them about anything else, and it clarifies that when his claim is complete, he makes the other speak, even if the plaintiff does not request that from the judge. Some judges chose not to make him speak unless the plaintiff says to the judge: 'Ask him for his answer,' then he makes him speak, and the first view is more correct. Thus in al-Mabsut. End. He said: 'He judges' against the opponent: meaning that the judge judges that he must relinquish what he has admitted, because admission is a proof by itself and its truth does not depend on the judge's judgment, so it would have been better to say: 'If he admits, then...' because the word 'judgment' regarding admission is metaphorical due to its necessity by his admission, so there is no need for judgment since it is a proof by itself not dependent on judgment, unlike evidence, for testimony is not proof and only becomes proof through judgment, eliminating the possibility of falsehood. He said: 'Or the opponent denies, explicitly or implicitly, such as when he says: I neither admit nor deny,' for it is considered denial according to them, and what is narrated that it is an implicit admission, so he is imprisoned until he admits, is a mistake, as referred to in al-Munya. Thus in Jami' al-Rumuz. Al-Sarakhsi said: According to Abu Yusuf, he is imprisoned until he answers. It is said in al-Bahr: The fatwa is on the saying of Abu Yusuf in matters related to judgment, as in al-Qunyah and al-Bazzaziyyah; therefore I have given fatwa that he is imprisoned until he answers. End. He said: 'And he asks...' meaning that the judge asks the plaintiff for evidence on his claim, and he begins by asking for evidence because it is the basis for ending the dispute, and the Messenger of Allah ﷺ began with it, as al-Bukhari and Muslim reported from Wa'il ibn... the hadith.
Source pages p. 101

Commentary
This passage from the Hanafi fiqh commentary 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the judge's procedure when a valid claim (da'wa sahihah) is presented. It begins by stating the core ruling: when a claim is valid, the judge must ask the defendant about it. The commentator elaborates that this question is to clarify the basis of the judgment, highlighting a key distinction between judgment based on admission (iqrar) and judgment based on evidence (bayyinah). Admission is a binding proof by itself—the judge merely orders compliance, so using the term 'judgment' is metaphorical. In contrast, evidence becomes proof only through the judge's pronouncement, which removes the possibility of falsehood for practical purposes. The judge's question takes the form: 'Your opponent claims such-and-such from you; what do you say?' If the claim is not valid, no question is required, as the defendant is not obligated to answer. Notably, the text indicates the judge asks even if the plaintiff does not request it. The passage then discusses the judge's optional behavior: he may remain silent until the plaintiff speaks or initiate by asking 'What is between you?' The preferred view is that the judge is not obligated to initiate but may do so. Some judges require the plaintiff to explicitly request the defendant's answer; however, the first view (that the judge asks without request) is deemed more correct, as stated in al-Mabsut. The commentary then turns to the three possible responses from the defendant: admission, denial, or silence. If the defendant admits, the judge 'judges' metaphorically, since the admission itself is binding. If he denies explicitly or implicitly (e.g., saying 'I neither admit nor deny'), that is considered denial. A narration that such a statement is implicit admission and warrants imprisonment until admission is a mistake; the correct view is that it is denial. However, according to Abu Yusuf, the defendant who remains silent or refuses to answer is imprisoned until he answers, and this is the fatwa in practice. Finally, the passage notes that after denial, the judge asks the plaintiff for evidence (bayyinah), as evidence is the fundamental means to settle disputes, following the practice of the Prophet ﷺ. This passage combines a clear ruling with intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on the permissibility of initiating questioning and on imprisoning a silent defendant, with the mukhtar (preferred) position being that the judge may ask without request and that Abu Yusuf's view on imprisonment is acted upon.

Key Terms
دعوى (da'wa): A legal claim or lawsuit brought by a plaintiff against a defendant before a judge.
إقرار (iqrar): Admission or confession by a party against himself, which is a binding proof without need for judgment.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence, primarily consisting of witnesses or documents, that requires the judge's ruling to become effective.
إنكار (inkar): Denial of the claim by the defendant, either explicitly or implicitly.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, if a defendant says 'لا أقر ولا أنكر' (I neither admit nor deny), how is this statement treated?
A) It is considered an implicit admission and he must pay.
B) It is considered a denial, and the plaintiff must provide evidence.
C) It is considered a refusal to answer, and he is immediately imprisoned.
D) It is considered a waiver of defense.

Key Takeaway
Upon a valid claim, the judge must question the defendant; admission is self-binding, while denial shifts the burden to the plaintiff to provide evidence, and ambiguous responses are treated as denial.

Procedure of the Judge upon Acknowledgment, Denial, Evidence, and Oath
Explains judge's procedure with proof from hadith

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi judge's procedural steps in a lawsuit and apply the rule that the oath is the claimant's right.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment binds directly; evidence requires a decree; the oath is the claimant's right, only administered upon request.

In a court case, the judge's actions depend on whether the defendant acknowledges, denies, or gives an ambiguous answer. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi procedure rooted in the Sunnah of the Prophet (peace be upon him). You will learn the sequence: ask for evidence, judge if proven, and administer the oath only if the claimant requests it – because the oath belongs to the claimant, not the judge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defendant's response
Determine whether the defendant acknowledges, denies explicitly, or gives an ambiguous answer. Each triggers a different judicial response.
Ambiguous denial (e.g., 'I do not acknowledge nor deny') is treated as denial, and according to Abu Yusuf, the defendant is imprisoned until a clear answer.
2
Apply the rule on acknowledgment
If the defendant acknowledges, the judge issues a declaratory judgment because acknowledgment is conclusive proof in itself – no further investigation needed.
Acknowledgment is a hujja (proof) by itself; the judge's ruling is simply formal.
3
Ask the claimant for evidence
Upon denial, the judge must ask the claimant to produce evidence (bayyinah). This order follows the Prophet's practice in the hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr.
Evidence is the primary means to settle disputes. The judge should not skip this step.
4
Administer the oath upon request
If the claimant has no evidence, the judge administers an oath to the defendant, but only if the claimant requests it. The oath is the claimant's right, as indicated by the phrase 'faka yaminuhu' (you have his oath) with the letter lam (specification).
The oath must be administered by the judge personally; a private oath is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن أقر بها حكم أو أنكر، وسأل المدعى البينة. فأقام قضى عليه، وإن لم يقم حلقه إن طلبه خصمه. [1] قوله : حكم على الخصم : يعني أن القاضي يحكم بالخروج عن موجب ما أقر به لأن الإقرار حجة بنفسه فلا يتوقف في صدقه على الحكم من القاضي، فكان الأولى أن يقول : فإن أقر فيها فلفظ الحكم في الإقرار مجاز للزومه بإقراره فلا حاجة إلى القضاء لكونه حجة بنفسه لا يتوقف على القضاء بخلاف البينة، فإن الشهادة غير حجة، وإنما تصير حجة بالقضاء، وسقط احتمال الكذب. [2] قوله : أو أنكر الخصم إنكاراً صريحاً أو غير صريح، كما إذا قال : لا أقر ولا أنكر، فإنه إنكار عندهم، وما روي أنه إقرار غير ظاهر، فيحبس حتى يقر فغلط على ما أشير إليه في «المنية». كذا في «جامع الرموز». وقال السرخسي : وعند أبي يوسف يحبس إلى أن يجيب، وقال في «البحر» : والفتوى على قول أبي يوسف له فيما يتعلق بالقضاء، كما في «القنية»، و«البزازية»؛ فلذا أفتيت بأنه يحبس إلى أن يجيب. انتهى. [3] قوله : وسأل... الخ ؛ يعني أن القاضي يسأل المدعي البينة على مدعاه، وإنما يبدأ بالسؤال عن البينة لأنها الأصل في قطع الخصومة، وبه بدأ رسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم، كما أخرج الشيخان عن وائل بن حجر قال : «جاء رجل من حضرموت، ورجل من كندة إلى النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم... فقال الحضري : يا رسول الله إن هذا غلبني على أرض كانت لأبي، فقال الكندي : هي أرضي في يدي أزرعها ليس له فيها حق، فقال صلى الله عليه وسلم للحضرمي : ألك بينة ؟ قال : لا، قال : فلك يمينه ؟ قال : يا رسول الله، الرجل فاجر لا يبالي على ما حلف عليه، وليس يتورع عن شيء، فقال : ليس لك منه إلا ذلك، فانطلق ليحلف... الحديث، فرسول الله صلى الله عليه وسلم سأل ورتب اليمين على فقد البينة، فلا بد من السؤال ليمكن القاضي الاستحلاف. [1] قوله : قضى عليه ؛ أي قضى القاضي على الخصم بالدعوى لانتفاء التهمة عن الدعوى لترجح جانب الصدق على الكذب بالبينة. وفي هذا الكلام إشعار بأن إقامة البينة ينبغي أن تكون بعد الإنكار والاستشهاد من المدعي، حتى لو شهدوا بعد الدعوى والإنكار بدون طلب المدعي الشهادة لا يسمع، وهذا عند الطحاوي، وعند غيره يسمع. كذا في «الفصول العمادية». [2] قوله : وإن لم يقم المدعي البينة على دعواه حلفه ؛ أي حلف القاضي الخصم إن طلبه : أي إن طلب المدعي تحليف الخصم لما روينا من حديث وائل بن حجر، وإنما اشترط طلب المدعي استحلاف خصمه لأن اليمين حقه، وإنما صار اليمين حقاً له لأنه أضاف اليمين إلى المدعي في قوله: «لك يمينه»، بحرف اللام المقتضية للاختصاص، فهذا تنصيص على أن اليمين حق المدعي. والسر فيه : إن المنكر قصد إتواء حقله على زعمه بالإنكار، فمكنه الشارع من إتواء نفسه باليمين الكاذبة، وهي الغموس إن كان كاذباً كما يزعم وهو أعظم من إتواء المال، وإلا يحصل للحالف الثواب بذكر اسم الله تعالى، وهو صادق على وجه التعظيم. وقيد بتحليف القاضي لأن الخصم لو حلف بطلب المدعي يمينه بين يدي القاضي من غير استحلاف القاضي فهذا ليس بتحليف لأن التحليف حق القاضي، ولو اصطلحا على أن يحلف عند غير القاضي ويكون بريئاً فهو باطل، فلو برهن عليه
If he acknowledges in it, he judges; or if he denies, and the claimant asks for evidence. If he produces evidence, he gives judgment against him; and if he does not produce, he administers an oath to him if his opponent requests it. [1] His statement: 'he judges' against the opponent: means that the judge judges by exiting from the consequence of what he acknowledged, because acknowledgment is a proof in itself, so it does not depend in its truth on the judgment of the judge. Therefore, it would have been better to say: 'if he acknowledges in it,' for the word 'judgment' with respect to acknowledgment is metaphorical because it is binding by his acknowledgment, so there is no need for judgment because it is a proof in itself not depending on judgment, unlike evidence, for testimony is not a proof, but becomes a proof by judgment, and the possibility of lying is eliminated. [2] His statement: 'or if the opponent denies' explicitly or not explicitly, as when he says: 'I do not acknowledge nor deny,' for it is denial according to them. And what is narrated that it is an unclear acknowledgment, so he is imprisoned until he acknowledges, is a mistake, as indicated in al-Munya. Thus in Jami' al-Rumuz. Al-Sarakhsi said: According to Abu Yusuf, he is imprisoned until he answers. And in al-Bahr: The fatwa is on Abu Yusuf's statement regarding matters related to judgment, as in al-Qunyah and al-Bazzaziyyah; therefore I gave a fatwa that he is imprisoned until he answers. End. [3] His statement: 'and asks...' etc.: means that the judge asks the claimant for evidence on his claim, and he begins by asking about evidence because it is the basis for ending the dispute, and the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, began with it, as narrated by the two Shaykhs from Wa'il ibn Hujr: 'A man from Hadramawt and a man from Kindah came to the Prophet...' The Hadrami said: 'O Messenger of Allah, this man overpowered me on land that belonged to my father.' The Kindi said: 'It is my land in my possession, I cultivate it, he has no right in it.' The Prophet said to the Hadrami: 'Do you have evidence?' He said: 'No.' He said: 'Then you have his oath?' He said: 'O Messenger of Allah, the man is wicked, he does not care what he swears, and he does not refrain from anything.' He said: 'You have nothing but that.' So he went to swear... the hadith. So the Messenger of Allah asked and arranged the oath upon the lack of evidence, so the question is necessary for the judge to administer the oath. [1] His statement: 'he gives judgment against him'; i.e., the judge gives judgment against the opponent because of the absence of suspicion about the claim because the side of truth outweighs falsehood by evidence. And in this speech there is an indication that producing evidence should be after denial and the claimant's request for testimony, so that if they testify after the claim and denial without the claimant's request for testimony, it is not heard. This is according to al-Tahawi, and according to others it is heard. Thus in al-Fusul al-Imadiyyah. [2] His statement: 'and if the claimant does not produce evidence on his claim, he administers an oath to him'; i.e., the judge administers an oath to the opponent if he requests it: i.e., if the claimant requests the opponent's oath, because of what we narrated from the hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr. And the condition of the claimant's request for the opponent's oath is because the oath is his right, and the oath became his right because he attributed the oath to the claimant in his statement: 'you have his oath', with the letter 'lam' indicating specification, so this is a text that the oath is the right of the claimant. And the inner meaning is that the denier intended to usurp his right by his denial, so the Lawgiver enabled him to usurp himself by a false oath, which is al-ghamus if he is lying as he claims, and that is greater than usurping wealth; otherwise, the one swearing obtains reward by mentioning the name of Allah, the Exalted, while being truthful in a manner of glorification. And it is restricted to the judge's administration of the oath because if the opponent swears at the claimant's request in front of the judge without the judge administering the oath, this is not an administration of oath because administering oath is the right of the judge. And if they agree that he swears before someone other than the judge and he becomes free, it is invalid. So if he presents evidence upon it...
Source pages p. 102

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the ruling of the judge's procedure in a lawsuit. It explains that if the defendant acknowledges the claim, the judge gives judgment based on that acknowledgment because acknowledgment is itself conclusive proof (hujja bi nafsihi) and does not require a judicial decree to become binding; the judge's ruling is merely declaratory. If the defendant denies, the judge asks the claimant for evidence. The commentary emphasizes that the judge should begin by asking for evidence, following the practice of the Prophet (peace be upon him) as shown in the hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr. If the claimant produces evidence, judgment is given in his favor. If he does not produce evidence, the judge administers an oath to the defendant, but only if the claimant requests it, because the oath is the claimant's right. The commentary also discusses nuances: the nature of denial (explicit or implicit), the necessity of the claimant's request for testimony, and the fact that the oath must be administered by the judge personally. It mentions a difference among later Hanafi scholars regarding whether a judge should ask the defendant to respond without the claimant's request, but the preferred view (as in al-Mabsut) is that the judge may ask them to speak. The overall procedure is rooted in the hadith, where the Prophet asked for evidence first and then offered the oath.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): Acknowledgment by the defendant of the claim; it is conclusive proof in itself and does not require a judicial decree.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence presented by the claimant to prove his claim; it becomes a proof only after the judge's ruling.
يمين (yamin): Oath that the judge administers to the defendant upon the claimant's request when the claimant has no evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi procedure, when should the judge administer the oath to the defendant?
A) Immediately after the defendant denies the claim.
B) After the claimant produces evidence, regardless of request.
C) Only after the claimant fails to produce evidence and requests the oath.
D) Only if the judge suspects the defendant is lying.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi judge must first ask for evidence, then administer the oath only upon the claimant's request when evidence is lacking – a procedure rooted in the Sunnah and reflecting the claimant's right to demand an oath.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate
12 min
Evidence and Oath in Legal Claims: The Hanafi Procedure
Ruling on evidence and oath in claims, with hadith proof and details on nukul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the administration of the oath in legal claims, identify the hadith proof, explain why the plaintiff's request is required, and distinguish the Hanafi position on nukul from that of al-Shafi'i.

Core Idea
The oath is the plaintiff's right, triggered only by his request—except in four cases by Abu Yusuf.

This lesson unpacks the foundational procedure for evidence and oath in Islamic litigation. The Hanafi school, based on the Prophet's hadith, establishes that a plaintiff must first present evidence (bayyinah); if he fails, he may request that the defendant take an oath. This request is a necessary condition for the judge to administer the oath. The lesson also explores Abu Yusuf's exceptions where the oath is administered without request, the nature of nukul (refusal to swear), and the Shafi'i counter-position.

Guided Path
1
Extract the Base Ruling
Identify the two-part ruling: (1) if the plaintiff brings evidence, judge rules in his favor; (2) if no evidence, judge administers an oath to the defendant—but only if the plaintiff requests it. This sets the stage for the hadith proof.
The ruling is immediate from the matn: 'fa in aqama qada 'alayh, wa in lam yuqim hallafahu in talabahu khasmuhu.'
2
Analyze the Hadith Proof
Examine the hadith of al-Hadrami and al-Kindi. Note the Prophet's questions: 'Do you have evidence?' and 'Then you have his oath?' The phrase 'laka yaminuhu' (you have his oath) with the letter lam indicates that the oath belongs to the plaintiff as a right. This is the core textual evidence for the Hanafi position.
The commentator expands on the 'secret' (al-sirr) behind the oath: the defendant, by denying, aims to take the plaintiff's right, so the Lawgiver gives him a chance to destroy himself via a false oath, which is a greater sin.
3
Understand the Condition of Plaintiff's Request
The judge administers the oath only 'in talabahu' (if the plaintiff requests it). This is derived from the hadith: the oath is 'his right,' and a right is only exercised by its owner's demand. The commentator stresses this condition, contrasting with Abu Yusuf's exceptions.
The judge's administration is also a right of the judge; a private oath outside court is invalid.
4
Examine Abu Yusuf's Four Exceptions
Abu Yusuf holds that in four specific cases, the judge administers the oath without the plaintiff's request: (1) buyer returning defective goods, (2) pre-emptor, (3) wife seeking maintenance from absent husband, (4) rightful claimant in a sale dispute. These are accepted as mufta bihi.
The majority (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) require the request in all cases, but the text presents Abu Yusuf's view without rejecting it, implying it is operative.
5
Define Nukul and Contrast with Shafi'i
Nukul is the defendant's refusal to take the oath. Hanafis rule: if the defendant refuses (explicitly or by silent incapacitated silence), the judge may rule against him. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing the judge should return the oath to the plaintiff; if the plaintiff swears, judgment is for him; if he refuses, the case ends. The Hanafi response: the hadith of Ali is a specific instance, not a general rule.
The practical difference: Hanafi judges can end the case with a ruling based on nukul; Shafi'i judges must offer the oath back to the plaintiff.

Chapter of Da'wa: Its definition and rulings. So if he brings evidence, the judge rules against him; and if he does not bring evidence, he administers the oath if his opponent requests it. So if he brings evidence, the judge rules against him; and if he does not bring evidence, he administers the oath if his opponent requests it. Al-Hadrami said: O Messenger of Allah, this man has overpowered me regarding land that belonged to my father. Al-Kindi said: It is my land in my possession, I cultivate it; he has no right in it. The Prophet said to al-Hadrami: Do you have evidence? He said: No. He said: Then you have his oath? He said: O Messenger of Allah, the man is a sinner, he does not care about what he swears upon, and he does not abstain from anything. He said: You have nothing except that. So he went to swear... the hadith. The Messenger of Allah asked and made the oath conditional upon the lack of evidence, so the question is necessary for the judge to administer the oath. [1] His statement: "he rules against him" means the judge rules against the opponent due to the claim, because the claim is free from suspicion, as the side of truth outweighs falsehood with evidence. In this speech there is indication that presenting evidence should be after the denial and the plaintiff's request for testimony, such that if they testify after the claim and denial without the plaintiff's request for testimony, it is not heard. This is according to al-Tahawi, and according to others it is heard. So in al-Fusul al-Imadiyya. [2] His statement: "And if the plaintiff does not bring evidence on his claim, he administers the oath to him" meaning the judge administers the oath to the opponent if he (the plaintiff) requests it, i.e., if the plaintiff requests the taking of an oath from his opponent, as we narrated from the hadith of Wa'il ibn Hujr. The request of the plaintiff for the oath of his opponent is only stipulated because the oath is his right. The oath became his right only because He (the Prophet) attributed the oath to the plaintiff in his statement: "You have his oath" with the letter lam which indicates exclusivity, so this is a specification that the oath is the right of the plaintiff. The secret in it is that the defendant intended to eliminate his right according to his claim by denial, so the Lawgiver enabled him to eliminate himself by the false oath, which is al-ghamus if he is lying as he claims, and it is greater than eliminating [the plaintiff's right]. Otherwise, the one swearing obtains reward by mentioning Allah's name with truthful magnification. And it is restricted to the judge's administration of the oath, because if the opponent swears at the plaintiff's request before the judge without the judge administering it, that is not considered administration; because administration is the right of the judge. And if they mutually agree to swear before someone other than the judge and he is free of liability, it is invalid. So if he proves (evidence), it is accepted; otherwise he will swear a second time. And he is not made to swear before his request in all claims according to the two sides (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad). According to Abu Yusuf, the judge administers the oath without request in four situations: 1. In the return due to defect: the buyer swears "By Allah, I did not accept the defect." 2. The pre-emptor: "By Allah, I did not nullify your pre-emption." 3. The woman, when she requests the fixing of maintenance from her absent husband, swears "By Allah, your husband left nothing for you nor gave you maintenance." 4. The rightful claimant swears "By Allah, I did not sell." And they all agreed that whoever claims a debt against the deceased, the judge makes him swear without the request of the executor or heir: "By Allah, you have not received it from the debtor nor from anyone who paid it to you, nor has any collector collected it for you at your command, nor have you discharged him from it, nor anything from it, nor have you assigned anyone to any part of that, nor do you have a pledge for it or anything from it." So in al-Bazzaziyya and others. [1] His statement: "nukul"; he said in al-Misbah: "nakaltu 'an al-aduww nukulan" from the category of qa'ada, this is the dialect of Hijaz; and "nakila nakalan" from the category of ta'iba is a dialect; and "nakala 'an al-yamin" means he refrained from it; and "nakala minhu yankulu" from the category of qatala. End of the summary to the extent needed. [2] His statement: "or was silent..." Know that nukul may be literal, like his saying "I do not swear" and may be legal, like remaining silent, and its ruling is the ruling of the first if it is known that there is no incapacity of muteness or deafness. This is the correct view. So in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj. The author only explicitly mentioned silence because there is disagreement; according to some shaykhs it is not the ruling of nukul, and the correct view is what has passed. [3] His statement: "is valid" i.e., that judgment. Al-Shafi'i said: The judge should not judge based on nukul, but rather he returns the oath to the plaintiff, and if he (the plaintiff) refuses, the dispute ends; and if he swears, he judges accordingly, as it was reported from Ali that he made the plaintiff swear after the defendant's nukul.
Source pages p. 103

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the chapter of da'wa (claims), explaining the initial ruling of evidence and oath. The base text states: if the plaintiff produces evidence (bayyinah), the judge rules in his favor; if not, the judge administers an oath to the defendant, provided the plaintiff requests it. The commentator supports this with the famous hadith of al-Hadrami and al-Kindi. In that hadith, the Prophet asked the plaintiff for evidence, and when he had none, he offered him the defendant's oath. The commentator emphasizes that the judge must ask for evidence first, and that the oath is the plaintiff's right, as indicated by the Prophet's phrase 'you have his oath.' The letter lam in 'laka yaminuhu' signifies exclusivity, making the oath a right of the plaintiff. The passage then explains the wisdom: the defendant's denial aims to take the plaintiff's right, so the Lawgiver gives the defendant the chance to destroy himself by a false oath, which is a greater sin than taking the plaintiff's property. The oath must be administered by the judge; a private oath is invalid. The commentator then discusses the condition that the plaintiff must request the oath; unless the defendant swears voluntarily, he is not compelled without request. However, Abu Yusuf lists four exceptions where the judge administers the oath without request: (1) the buyer returning a defective item swears he did not accept the defect; (2) the pre-emptor swears he did not nullify his right; (3) a wife seeking maintenance from an absent husband swears he left nothing; (4) a rightful claimant swears he did not sell the property. Additionally, there is consensus that in claims against a deceased person, the claimant must swear a detailed oath without being asked by the executor or heir. The passage then defines nukul (refusal to swear) linguistically. Nukul can be explicit (saying 'I will not swear') or legal (remaining silent without incapacity). The correct view is that silence without incapacitating condition (like muteness or deafness) is treated as nukul. Finally, the commentator notes the intra-madhhab dispute on nukul: the Hanafi position is that the judge can rule against the defendant based on his nukul. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, arguing the judge should instead return the oath to the plaintiff; if the plaintiff swears, the judge rules in his favor, and if he refuses, the dispute ends. The Hanafi school relies on the hadith and the principle that nukul is a form of admission.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence that proves a claim in court, typically testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females.
يمين (yamin): An oath sworn by a party in a lawsuit, typically by Allah, to support their claim or denial.
نكول (nukul): Refusal of a defendant to take the oath after it is offered by the judge, which can lead to a ruling against him.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, when does the judge administer an oath to the defendant in a claim?
A) Automatically whenever the plaintiff fails to bring evidence
B) Only if the plaintiff requests it
C) Only if the defendant requests it
D) Never; the judge rules immediately without oath

Key Takeaway
The oath is the plaintiff's right; without his request, the judge does not administer it, except in Abu Yusuf's four cases.

Qada — Judiciary
intermediate

Judgment by Nukul: Hanafi vs. Shafi'i
Inter-madhab dispute on nukul with sub-rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on nukul, explain the Shafi'i counter-argument, and identify the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf) between the two schools.

Core Idea
Nukul alone is a valid basis for judgment; the oath is not returned to the plaintiff.

This lesson examines a core procedural dispute: what happens when the defendant refuses to take an oath in court? The Hanafi school holds that the judge can rule against the defendant immediately after nukul, while Imam al-Shafi'i insists the oath be returned to the plaintiff. Understanding this ikhtilaf reveals how each school weighs different proofs and practical consequences.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify that judgment by nukul is valid — the judge rules against the defendant who refuses to swear, without requiring the plaintiff to swear.
Nukul can be literal or constructive (silence without disability).
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that the judge must return the oath to the plaintiff. If the plaintiff swears, judgment goes for him; if he also refuses, the case ends.
Shafi'i's main proof: a narration from Ali that he made the plaintiff swear after nukul.
3
Follow the opponent's proof
Examine the Shafi'i rational arguments: nukul may be due to confusion, scrupulousness, or pride, so it is not definitive; returning the oath to the plaintiff is more just because the apparent evidence shifts to the plaintiff after nukul.
Also, the oath was initially on the denier because apparent support was with him; after nukul, support shifts.
4
Analyse the Hanafi response
The Hanafi response cites ijma' al-sahaba (consensus of Companions) and specific incidents (Shurayh judged by nukul in Ali's presence and Ali approved; Umar's judgment; Ibn Abbas). Rationale: the strong presumption is that a Muslim performs obligations and avoids falsehood, so nukul indicates concession or admission, overriding other possibilities.
If nukul were due to confusion, the defendant would ask for time. The principle 'the oath is upon the denier' is not overridden.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: Hanafi judges rule against the defendant immediately upon nukul (after three offers), while Shafi'i judges ask the plaintiff to swear and only then rule.
This directly affects court procedure and the burden of proof.

If he refuses [the oath] once, or remains silent without a disability, and the judge passes judgment based on the refusal, it is valid, and the oath is offered three times. If he refuses once – meaning he says 'I will not swear' or remains silent without a disability – and the judge passes judgment based on the refusal, it is valid, and the oath is offered three times. It is accepted, otherwise he will be asked to swear a second time. He is not made to swear without his request in all lawsuits according to both [Imam Abu Hanifa and Muhammad]. According to Abu Yusuf, the judge administers an oath without request in four places: 1. In the case of return due to defect, the buyer swears: 'By Allah, I was not satisfied with the defect.' And the preemptor: 'By Allah, I did not invalidate your preemption.' 3. The woman, when she requests the fixing of maintenance from her absent husband, swears: 'By Allah, your husband left nothing for you, nor gave you maintenance.' 4. The rightful claimant swears: 'By Allah, I did not sell.' And they have agreed that whoever claims a debt against a deceased, the judge makes him swear without the request of the executor or heir: 'By Allah, you have not received it from the debtor, nor from anyone who paid it to you, nor has any receiver received it for you by your command, nor have you released him from it, nor any part of it, nor have you transferred any of that to anyone, nor do you have a pledge for it or any part of it.' Thus in al-Bazzaziyyah and others.
