Nukul as Settlement or Admission: The Hanafi Intra-Madhhab Dispute
Analyzing Abu Hanifa's and Sahibayn's views on nukul in lawsuits

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the difference between Abu Hanifa's and the Sahibayn's views on nukul and identify the mufta bihi position in marriage cases.

Core Idea
Nukul is generally settlement, not admission — but in marriage, the Sahibayn's view that it is admission prevails for fatwa.

In this passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah, we encounter a classic intra-Hanafi dispute: when a defendant refuses to take a sworn oath (nukul), does that count as settling the dispute (badhl) or as admitting the claim (iqrar)? The answer has significant practical consequences, especially in personal status matters like marriage. Let's unpack the arguments of Abu Hanifa and his two disciples and see which position is relied upon for fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Define nukul and its legal context
Nukul is the defendant's refusal to swear a required oath. It ends the litigation but its legal characterization determines consequences.
The judge asks the defendant to swear; refusal triggers nukul.
2
State Abu Hanifa's position: nukul is badhl (settlement)
Abu Hanifa holds that nukul is a settlement: the defendant refrains from prohibiting the claim, ending the dispute without admission. This avoids making the defendant a liar in his previous denial.
Supported by Quran 59:9 and rational arguments.
3
Examine the proofs: rational and textual
The passage gives examples: if nukul were admission, it would be valid without a judge, but it requires a judge's court; also, in a guarantor case, nukul does not oblige the guarantor, which would not be so if it were admission.
These practical tests show nukul operates as settlement.
4
Present the Sahibayn's position: nukul is iqrar (admission) where badhl is impermissible
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani argue that in matters where settlement is prohibited (freedom, lineage, marriage, hudud, li'an), nukul must be admission, as it cannot be settlement.
Settlement is not allowed in these seven matters.
5
Explore the thamarah al-khilaf and determine the mufta bihi
The practical difference: in marriage, if a woman denies marriage and refuses the oath, under Sahibayn she is deemed to have admitted the marriage. The fatwa is on the Sahibayn's view in marriage due to general need (umum al-balwa).
For financial matters, Abu Hanifa's view (settlement) is followed.

Chapter of Lawsuit: Its Definition and Rulings

And for Abu Hanifa (may Allah have mercy on him): Indeed, a person often avoids truthful oath, so he gives something and does not swear. And if it is possible to interpret it as settlement, admission is not established due to doubt, so it is interpreted as settlement. And settlement does not apply in these matters.
So it is apparent that he refrained from them out of fear of destruction, opposition to his desire, miserliness of his soul, and preference for returning to the truth, for that is better than persisting in falsehood, as Allah said: 'And whoever is saved from the stinginess of his soul, it is those who are the successful' (59:9). Thus it is necessarily admission, and admission applies in these matters. And Allah knows what is in the breasts.

(1) Surah al-Hashr: 9.
(2) See: al-Tabyin (4:297).

[1] His saying: 'And for Abu Hanifa...' etc.; its explanation: Indeed, nukul is settlement and permissibility, i.e., ending the litigation by paying what the opponent claims; because the oath is no longer obligatory with nukul, and whatever is so is either settlement or admission, but being settlement is more appropriate so that he does not become a liar in his previous denial. And these rights do not allow settlement and permissibility.
Do you not see that if a man says: 'I am free, and I give myself to this person to cover me,' or says: 'I am the son of so-and-so and I allow this person to claim my lineage,' or a woman says: 'I am not a wife to this man, and I permit myself to him,' it is not valid to judge against him by nukul, like qisas for life, hudud, and li'an; for this reason it is only permitted in the judge's court and by his judgment, whereas if it were admission it would be permitted absolutely and without judgment.
Similarly, if a man guarantees for another for what a third person will admit to him, then the guarantee-holder claims a debt from the third person, requests an oath, and he refuses (nukul), nothing is incumbent upon the guarantor; whereas if it were admission, it would be incumbent. Similarly, if one buys half a slave then buys the remaining half, finds a defect, and contests regarding the first half, requests an oath, and he refuses (nukul), judgment is given against him for return. Then if he wants to return the other half, he needs a new contest and request for oath if he denies; whereas if it were admission, he would not be requested for oath a second time, rather the whole would be incumbent upon him by the first nukul. This is because the oath becomes obligatory when requested to end the litigation, and with settlement there is no litigation or request, so it is not obligatory.

(1) Surah al-Hashr: 9.
(2) See: al-Tabyin (4:297).

(1)
Zubdat al-Nihayah li-Umdat al-Ri'ayah

It can be said: Since settlement is not permitted in these matters, nukul is not considered settlement, so it is interpreted as admission. And in the Fatawa of Qadi Khan (may Allah have mercy on him) the fatwa is according to their opinion in marriage. And it is only permitted from the mukatib, slave, and authorized minor because there is necessity, so it falls under permission for trade, just as slight hospitality and slight gifts fall under it, since trade necessarily requires that. And it is only permitted in debt based on the plaintiff's claim. The meaning of settlement is refraining from prohibition, and refraining from prohibition is permissible in wealth because the matter of wealth is easy, unlike these seven matters. And the judge must judge by nukul by virtue of the law because the plaintiff had the claimed thing apparently, and the defendant nullified it by dispute, and the law nullifies his dispute up to the oath. So when he refrains from the oath, the original state returns by law, and it is valid to impose it as a liability from the beginning based on the plaintiff's claim that he is truthful, and that the meaning of settlement is refraining from prohibition. And even if it is settlement in reality, wealth is obligatory as a liability from the beginning like guaranty and transfer of debt.

And it is mentioned in Sharh al-Jami' li-Qadi Khan, al-Waqi'at, and al-Fusul: The fatwa is according to their opinion, and it is the choice of Fakhr al-Islam (may Allah have mercy on him). And it is said it is the choice of the later scholars. And it is said: The judge should consider the condition of the plaintiff: if he sees him as vexatious, he takes by their opinion and makes him swear; if he sees him as oppressed, he does not make him swear. This is similar to what Shams al-A'immah chose regarding authorization for litigation without the opponent's consent: the judge, if he sees vexation and intent to harm from the opponent, permits authorization without consent, otherwise not.

Then know that if the defendant is not sworn before Imam Abu Hanifa regarding lineage, is the plaintiff's evidence accepted? Consider: if it is lineage that is established by admission and his evidence is accepted, like child and parent; if it is not established by his admission, his evidence is not accepted, like grandfather, grandchild, uncles, brothers, and their children, because it involves attributing lineage to someone else, unlike the claim of the higher or lower patron where it is accepted, and if he claims that he is the freed slave of his grandfather, etc. The detail is in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and elsewhere. (3) [1] His saying: 'It can be said...' etc.; Ya'qub Pasha said: There is discussion, and that is the difference...
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah discusses the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the legal nature of nukul (refusal to take an oath) in litigation. The central issue is whether nukul constitutes a settlement (badhl) — i.e., the defendant gives up the claim to end the dispute — or an admission (iqrar) of the claim. The text begins by presenting Imam Abu Hanifa's position: nukul is to be interpreted as settlement because people often avoid truthful oaths by paying something, and it is better to assume settlement to avoid making the defendant a liar in his previous denial. Abu Hanifa supports this with a Quranic verse about stinginess (59:9). The commentator then explains that settlement applies to financial matters, not to personal status cases like claims of freedom, lineage, or marriage, nor to hudud or li'an. If nukul were admission, it would be valid anywhere; but since these matters require a judge's court and judgment, it shows nukul is settlement. The commentator provides examples: a man claiming freedom, a woman denying marriage, a guarantor case, and a partial slave purchase case — all illustrating that if nukul were admission, the consequences would differ. After presenting Abu Hanifa's view, the text presents the opinion of the two disciples (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) that in certain matters where settlement is impermissible (like lineage, marriage, etc.), nukul should be considered admission. The fatwa according to Qadi Khan and later authorities is on the opinion of the Sahibayn in matters of marriage due to general need. The passage also mentions the judge's discretion: if the plaintiff is vexatious, the judge may follow the Sahibayn and still administer an oath; if the plaintiff appears oppressed, the judge may not require an oath. Finally, the text discusses the acceptance of evidence in lineage cases when the defendant has not been sworn, distinguishing between lineages established by admission (like parent-child) versus those not (like grandparent). This is a detailed intra-madhhab analysis showing the practical implications of the theoretical disagreement. The relied-upon position for fatwa (mufta bihi) is the Sahibayn's view in marriage and similar matters, as chosen by Fakhr al-Islam and later scholars.

Key Terms
النكول (al-nukul): Refusal of a defendant to take a sworn oath when requested by the plaintiff; this refusal triggers a legal consequence.
البذل (al-badhl): Settlement: the defendant refrains from prohibiting the claim, i.e., gives up contesting the claim, ending the litigation without admitting the claim.
الإقرار (al-iqrar): Admission: the defendant acknowledges the truth of the plaintiff's claim, which has full legal effect even outside court.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (mufta bihi) mentioned in the passage, in which type of case is nukul considered admission (iqrar) rather than settlement (badhl)?
A) Financial debt cases
B) Marriage (nikah) cases
C) Homicide (qisas) cases
D) Cases involving penalties (hudud)

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that nukul is generally settlement, but in personal status matters like marriage, the Sahibayn's view (admission) is relied upon for fatwa due to general need.

Nukul's Effect: Financial vs. Personal Status Claims
Explains why nukul is bazi in money but not in marriage, lineage, etc.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on nukul in financial and personal status claims, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn.

Core Idea
Nukul is treated as admission in financial claims but not in marriage or lineage.

This passage addresses the critical distinction in Hanafi fiqh between nukul (refusal to swear) in property disputes versus personal status matters like marriage and lineage. While in financial cases the Sahibayn's view that nukul constitutes admission (bazi) prevails, in marriage the fatwa follows their position that nukul is not admission. The reasoning hinges on the concept of 'ease' in financial matters versus the need for caution in personal affairs.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
State clearly when nukul is considered admission (bazi) and when it is not, according to the mufta bihi position.
Financial claims: nukul is bazi. Marriage, lineage, etc.: nukul is not bazi.
2
Distinguish the two Hanafi views
Understand Imam Abu Hanifa's position (nukul is never bazi) versus the Sahibayn's view (nukul is bazi in financial claims only).
This is an intra-madhhab ikhtilaf.
3
Understand the rationale
Recognize that in financial matters, the principle 'amr al-mali hain' (the matter of wealth is easy) applies, so leaving off prohibition (tark al-man') is permissible, making nukul tantamount to admission. In personal status matters, caution requires otherwise.
This is a dalil aqli (rational proof) based on the nature of the claims.
4
Note the fatwa
Learn that according to Fatawa Qadi Khan and other later authorities, the fatwa in marriage is on the view of the Sahibayn (nukul is not bazi).
The later scholars' choice (ikhtiyar al-muta'akhkhirin) also supports this.
5
Apply the distinction
Recognize the practical consequence: a judge faced with nukul in a financial claim rules against the defendant; in a marriage claim, the judge does not rule based on nukul alone.
The judge may also consider the plaintiff's character, as discussed in the passage.

ويمكن أن يقال : لما لم يجز البذلُ في هذه الأشياء، لا يجعل النكول بذلاً، فيحمل على الإقرار، وفي فتاوى قاضي خان له أن الفتوى على قولهما في النكاح . وإنما جاز من المكاتب والعبدِ والصبي المأذون لهما ؛ لأنَّ [فيه] ضرورةً، فيدخلُ تحت الإذن في التجارة، كما تدخل الضيافة اليسيرة والهدية اليسيرة، إذ لا بُدَّ للتجارة من ذلك، وإنما جاز في الدين بناء على دعوى المدعي. ومعنى البذل : ترك المنع ، وترك المنع جائز في المال ؛ لأنَّ أمرَ المالِ هين، بخلافِ هذه الأشياء السبعة، وإنّما وجب على القاضي أن يقضي بالنكول بحكم الشرع لِمَا أنَّ المدعي كان له الشيء المدعى ظاهراً، وأبطله المنكر بالنزاع، والشرع أبطل نزاعَهُ إلى اليمين . فإذا امتنع اليمين عادَ الأصلُ بحكم الشرع، وإنما صح إيجابهُ بالذمَّةِ ابتداءً بناءً على زعم المدعي أنه مُحِقِّ ، وأنَّ معنى البذلِ تركُ المنع ، ولئن كان بذلاً حقيقةً، فالمالُ يجب فيه في الذمة ابتداءً كالكفالة والحوالة. وذكر في «شرح الجامع لقاضي خان ، و«الواقعات»، و«الفصول»: إنَّ الفتوى على قولهما، وهو اختيار فخرِ الإسلام الله ، وقيل : هو اختيار المتأخرين. وقيل : ينبغي للقاضي أن ينظر في حالِ المدَّعي فإن رآه مُتَعَنتاً يأخذ بقولهما ويحلفه، وإن رآه مظلوماً لا يحلّفه، وهو نظير ما اختاره شمس الأئمةِ له في التوكيل بالخصومة لغير رضاء الخصم : إنَّ القاضي إن رأى من الخصم التعنت وقصد الإضرار بالآخر قبل التوكيل بغير رضاه، وإلا فلا. ثم اعلم أنه إذا لم يستحلف المنكر عند الإمام في النسب هل تقبلُ بينةُ المدعي؟ ينظر : فإن كان نسباً يثبت بالإقرار وتقبلُ بينته مثلُ : الولد، والوالد، وإن لم يثبت بإقرارِهِ لا تقبل بينته مثلُ : الجد، وولد الولد والأعمام ، والأخوة، وأولادهم؛ لأنَّ فيه حمل النسب على الغير بخلاف دعوى المولى الأعلى أو الأسفل حيث تقبل، وإن ادعى أنه مُعْتَقَ جَده ، ونحو ذلك ، والتفصيلُ في «السراج الوهاج» وغيره
And it can be said: Since bazi (offering) is not permissible in these things, nukul (refusal) is not made bazi, so it is interpreted as iqrar (admission). And in the Fatawa of Qadi Khan, he said that the fatwa is on their two statements in marriage. And it is only permitted from the mukatab, the slave, and the authorized boy because there is necessity, so it enters under the permission for trade, just as minor hospitality and minor gifts enter, since trade cannot do without that. And it is only permitted in debt based on the plaintiff's claim. And the meaning of bazi is leaving off prohibition, and leaving off prohibition is permissible in property because the matter of property is easy, unlike these seven things. And it was only obligatory upon the judge to judge by nukul by virtue of the shari'a because the plaintiff had the claimed thing apparently, and the defendant invalidated it by dispute, and the shari'a invalidated his dispute up to the oath. When he refrains from the oath, the original returns by virtue of the shari'a. And it is only valid to impose it upon the dhimmah (liability) from the beginning based on the plaintiff's claim that he is truthful, and that the meaning of bazi is leaving off prohibition. And even if it is bazi in reality, property is obligatory therein upon the dhimmah from the beginning like kafalah and hawalah. And it is mentioned in Sharh al-Jami' li Qadi Khan, al-Waqi'at, and al-Fusul: The fatwa is on their two statements (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), and it is the choice of Fakhr al-Islam (al-Bazdawi?), and some said it is the choice of the later scholars. And some said: It is appropriate for the judge to look at the plaintiff's condition: if he sees him as obstinate, he takes by their two statements and makes him swear; if he sees him as oppressed, he does not make him swear. And this is similar to what Shams al-A'imma chose regarding delegation of litigation without the opponent's satisfaction: The judge, if he sees obstinacy from the opponent and intent to harm the other, accepts the delegation without his satisfaction, otherwise not. Then know that if the defendant is not made to swear according to Imam (Abu Hanifa) in lineage, is the plaintiff's evidence accepted? Consider: if it is a lineage that is established by admission and its evidence is accepted, like child, parent; and if it is not established by his admission, his evidence is not accepted, like grandfather, grandchild, uncles, brothers, and their children; because that involves attributing lineage to another unlike the claim of a higher or lower master where it is accepted, and if he claims that he is the freed slave of his grandfather, and so on. And the detail is in al-Siraj al-Wahhaj and others.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the effect of nukul (refusal to take oath) in different types of claims. The author explains why nukul is treated as bazi (offering/admission) in financial claims but not in seven specific things (including marriage, lineage). The key reasoning is that in financial matters, the matter is easy (amr al-mali hain), so leaving off prohibition (tark al-man') is permissible and thus nukul can be considered bazi. However, in personal status matters like marriage and lineage, such a concession is not given. The author then states that according to Qadi Khan's Fatawa, the fatwa is on the view of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) in marriage, meaning nukul is not treated as admission there. This is also the choice of Fakhr al-Islam and later scholars. Additionally, the author discusses a nuance: the judge may consider the plaintiff's character—if the plaintiff is obstinate, the judge may follow the Sahibayn and administer the oath to the defendant; if the plaintiff is oppressed, the judge may follow Imam Abu Hanifa and not swear the defendant. This parallels a ruling on delegation of litigation. The passage then extends to lineage claims: if the defendant refuses the oath, the plaintiff's evidence may be accepted only for lineages that can be established by admission (like direct parent-child). For relationships that cannot be established by admission alone (like uncles, brothers), the evidence is not accepted because that would require attributing lineage to another person without proper proof.

Key Terms
النكول (al-nukūl): Refusal of the defendant to take the oath after being required by the judge
البذل (al-badhl): Offering or yielding; here, the act of the defendant giving up his right to contest, interpreted as admission
الإقرار (al-iqrār): Admission or confession by the defendant; here, nukul is interpreted as iqrar in certain claims
أمر المال هين (amr al-mālī hayyin): The matter of wealth is easy; a principle used to justify leniency in financial transactions

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school, what is the ruling on nukul in a claim of marriage (nikah)?
A) Nukul is treated as admission and the judge rules against the defendant
B) Nukul is not treated as admission; the judge may imprison the defendant
C) Nukul is not treated as admission; the judge does not rule against the defendant
D) Nukul leads to the plaintiff being required to swear

Key Takeaway
Nukul is treated as admission in financial claims (mufta bihi) but not in personal status claims like marriage or lineage.

Nukul in Hadd, Li'an, and Theft
States unanimous rule: no oath in hadd or li'an; thief swears or pays.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule against administering oaths in hadd and li'an, explain the exception for personal rights, and apply the rule for the thief where nukul leads to property liability but not cutting.

Core Idea
No oath in hadd or li'an because they are Allah's rights; thief's nukul only incurs property, not cutting.

This passage clarifies a fundamental procedural rule in Hanafi fiqh: oaths cannot be used to establish hudud (prescribed punishments) or li'an (mutual imprecation). The rule is based on consensus and the nature of these punishments as pure or predominant rights of Allah. However, when property is involved, such as in theft, an oath may be administered, and refusal to swear (nukul) results in financial liability but never in the hadd punishment.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
State the general rule: no oath is taken in hadd or li'an by agreement (consensus). The act is the administration of an oath for establishing these punishments, and the ruling is that it is prohibited.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is consensus (ijma), explicitly stated in the text: 'لا يستحلف بالإجماع' – he is not made to swear by consensus. This is a qat'i (definitive) proof.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The phrase indicates unanimity that oaths are not taken. The reasoning is that hadd is a pure right of Allah (haqq Allah) or predominantly so, and li'an stands in place of hadd. Nukul is treated as admission with doubt, and hadd cannot be established by doubtful evidence.
4
Note the exception
If hadd involves a personal right, such as a master suspending emancipation on the condition of zina, then an oath can be administered. Nukul then establishes the personal right (emancipation) but not the hadd.
5
Apply to theft
When a thief is accused and the plaintiff claims the stolen property, the thief is made to swear that he does not owe the property. If he refuses (nukul), he is liable for the property value but is not subjected to the hadd of cutting. The judge should guide the plaintiff to claim the property specifically, not the hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحـد ولـعـان) : أي كما إذا ادعى رجلٌ على آخر أنَّك قذفتني بالزنا ، وعليك الحد لا يستحلفُ بالإجماع ، وكذا إذا ادعت المرأة على الزوج أنَّك قذفتني بالزنا وعليك اللعان. (وحلف السَّارقِ ، وضَمِنَ إن نكل ، ولم يقطع) ؛ لأنَّ المال يلزم بالنكول لا القطع. ثم ذكر تعليلاً: ثابت بين قصد البذل وعدم جواز شرعاً ، فلا يمكن القولُ المذكور إلا أن يدَّعي أنه لا يقصد به البذلُ إلا بعد الجريان، لكنه بعيد كما لا يخفي. انتهى. (1) قوله : وحد ولعان ؛ أي ولا يحلف في حد ولا في لعان اتفاقاً، أما الحد فهو خالص حق الله تعالى : كحد الزنا والشرب والسرقة، أو حقه تعالى فيه غالب: كحد القذف، فإن حق العبد فيه مغلوب، وأما اللعان فهو قائم مقام حد الزنا في جانب الزوج ، فلا يثبت بالنكول الذي هو إقرار مع شبهة. وذكرَ الزَّيْلَعِيُّ نقلاً. عن الصدر الشهيد له : إنَّ الحدود لا يستحلف فيها بالإجماع إلا إذا تضمَّنَ حقاً بأن علق عتق عبدِهِ بالزنا، وقال : إن زنيت فأنت حرّ، فادَّعى العبد أنه قد زنى ولا بينة له عليه يستحلف المولى، حتى إذا نكل ثبت العتق دونَ الزنا. انتهى. [٢) قوله : وحلف السارق... الخ ؛ يعني إنّ السارق يحلف بالاتفاق عند إرادة أخذِ المال، ويقول فيه : بالله تعالى ما له عليك هذا المال. وعن محمد له : إن القاضي يقول للمدعي : ماذا تريد؟ فإن قال : أريد القطع ، يقولُ في جوابه : إنَّ الحدود لا يستحلف فيها، وإن أريد المال يقول له : دع دعوى السرقة وادَّع المال. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر (۲)، و«التبيين (۳) ، فإن نكل عن الحلفِ ضَمِنَ المال ولا يقطع.
And hadd and li'an: It means as when a man claims against another: 'You slandered me with zina, and upon you is the hadd' – he is not made to swear by consensus. Likewise when a woman claims against her husband: 'You slandered me with zina, and upon you is the li'an.' (And the thief is made to swear, and he is liable if he refuses, and he is not cut) – because property is incurred by nukul, not cutting. Then an explanation: established between the intention of badhl and its being impermissible legally, so the mentioned saying is not possible unless he claims that he does not intend badhl by it except after the crime, but it is improbable, as is not hidden. (And hadd and li'an): i.e. no oath is taken in hadd or li'an by agreement. As for hadd, it is a pure right of Allah: like the hadd of zina, drinking, and theft, or the right of Allah is predominant in it: like the hadd of slander, for the right of the servant is overcome. As for li'an, it stands in place of the hadd of zina on the husband's side, so it is not established by nukul, which is an admission with doubt. And al-Zayla'i narrated from al-Sadr al-Shahid: Hadds are not sworn in by consensus except when they involve a right, such as when he attaches the emancipation of his slave to zina, and says: 'If you commit zina, you are free,' then the slave claims that he has committed zina and has no evidence against him – the master is made to swear, so that if he refuses, the emancipation is established but not the zina. (The thief is made to swear): It means that the thief swears by agreement when intending to take the property, and says in it: 'By Allah, this property is not owed to you.' And from Muhammad: The judge says to the plaintiff: 'What do you want?' If he says: 'I want the cutting,' he says in response: 'Hadds are not sworn in.' If he wants the property, he says to him: 'Leave the claim of theft and claim the property.' This is in Majma' al-Anhur and al-Tabyin. So if he refuses the oath, he is liable for the property and is not cut.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof regarding the application of nukul in hadd and li'an cases, and the specific case of the thief. It begins by stating unanimously that no oath is administered in hadd (punishments like zina, drinking, theft, false accusation) or in li'an (mutual imprecation between spouses when husband accuses wife of zina). The reason is that hadd is a pure right of Allah (haqq Allah) or predominantly so, and li'an stands in place of hadd for the husband. Since these are not purely personal rights, nukul (refusal to swear) cannot establish them because nukul is treated as admission with doubt, and hadd cannot be established by doubtful evidence. The text then provides an exception: if hadd involves a personal right, such as a master suspending emancipation on the condition of zina, then the master is sworn. The second part deals with the thief. When a person is accused of theft and the victim claims the stolen property, the thief is made to swear that he does not owe the property. By consensus, the thief swears if he wants to avoid paying. If he refuses (nukul), he must pay the value of the stolen property but is not subjected to the hadd of cutting. This is because nukul only incurs financial liability, not corporal punishment. The text adds a subtle point: the intention behind swearing is to avoid property loss, not to admit the theft that would lead to cutting. The judge should guide the plaintiff to claim the property specifically, not the hadd, for the oath to be administered.

Key Terms
الحد (al-ḥadd): Corporal punishment prescribed by Shari'ah for specific crimes like zina, theft, etc.
اللعان (al-liʿān): Mutual imprecation between spouses when husband accuses wife of zina without witnesses
النكول (al-nukūl): Refusal to take the oath; in theft, it leads to property liability but not hadd

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The thief is not liable for anything

Key Takeaway
Oaths are not used to establish hudud or li'an because they are rights of Allah; nukul in theft only creates a financial obligation, not a criminal punishment.

The Limited Effect of Nukul in Marriage Claims
Ruling with proof: two cases of nukul in marriage claims, establishing financial liability only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that nukul (refusal to swear) establishes only financial liability, not personal status, and apply it to cases of claimed divorce and marriage.

Core Idea
Nukul proves money, not marriage: refusal to swear establishes financial liability only, never the underlying marriage or divorce.

In Hanafi litigation, when a defendant refuses to take an oath (nukul), the plaintiff's claim is established – but only if it is a financial claim. This lesson examines two marriage-related cases where this principle is applied: a woman claiming divorce before consummation, and a woman claiming dower. The key distinction is between financial rights (which can be proven by nukul) and personal status (which cannot). Understanding this limitation is crucial for correctly applying the rules of evidence in marriage disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two cases
Read the two scenarios: (1) woman claims divorce before consummation, (2) woman claims marriage and dower. What is the husband's denial? In each, the judge requires the husband to swear. Note the consequence of his refusal.
The first case is explicitly about half dower; the second about dower or maintenance.
2
Understand the consensus proof
The text states that oath-taking in divorce is established by consensus (ijma). The principle is that oath-taking applies to financial claims by consensus. This shows that the rule is based on universal agreement, providing certainty.
Consensus provides certainty (qat'i) to the ruling.
3
Distinguish financial vs. status claims
Abu Hanifa's key point: nukul establishes financial liability (daman) but not the underlying personal status (halal). For example, if the husband refuses to swear, he must pay half the dower, but this does not prove that the divorce occurred. The marriage remains intact.
This distinction applies to other areas: hudud and qisas cannot be proven by nukul.
4
Apply the rule to the two cases
In case 1: nukul → half dower owed, but divorce not proven. In case 2: nukul → dower owed, but marriage not proven. This shows that nukul only fixes the financial consequence, not the status.
The practical implication: the woman cannot remarry based on nukul alone; she would need other evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذا الزوج إذا ادعت المرأة طلاقاً قبل الدخول، وكذا في النكاح إذا ادعت هي مهرها
(وكذا الزوج إذا ادعت المرأة طلاقاً قبل الدخول) ؛ لأنَّه يَحْلِفُ في الطلاق إجماعاً ، فإن نكل ضمن نصف مهرها.
(وكذا في النكاح إذا ادعت هي مهرها : أي إذا ادعت المرأة النكاح وطلبت المال كالمهر أو النفقة ، فأنكر الزوج يحلف، فإن نكل يلزم المال، ولا يثبت الحل عند أبي حنيفة له ؛ لأنَّ المال يثبت بالبذل لا الحلّ.
الضمان: وهو يجب مع الشبهة فيجب بالنكول. والقطع : وهو لا يجب مع الشبهة، فلا يجب بالنكول، فصار كما إذا شهد عليها رجل وامرأتين أو ثبتت بالشهادة على الشهادة، أو بكتاب القاضي إلى القاضي ، فإنَّ ضمان المال يجب فيها دون القطع. قوله : قبل الدخول ؛ وكذا بعد الدخول. كذا في «النتائج»، وإنّما وضع المسألة في الطلاق قبل الدخول ؛ لأنه لو أطلق ينصرفُ إلى الطلاق الذي يلزم منه المهر تاماً، ويبقى أمر الطلاقِ الذي يلزم منه نصفُ المهر مستوراً، فكشفه أولى مع أنَّ لزوم الحلف في الطلاق بعد الدخول بالطريق الأولى، فإنَّه إذا استحلفه قبل تأكد المهر، فبعده أولى. كذا في «مجمع الأنهر». قوله : لأنه يحلف بالطلاق إجماعاً ؛ لأنَّ فيه دعوى المال، والاستحلاف يجري في المال إجماعاً.
And likewise the husband if the woman claims divorce before consummation – because he takes an oath in divorce by consensus, and if he refuses, he is liable for half her dower. And likewise in marriage if she claims her dower, i.e., if the woman claims marriage and demands money like dower or maintenance, and the husband denies, he takes an oath; if he refuses, the money is obligated, but the marital relationship (halal) is not established according to Abu Hanifa, because money is established by the [defendant's] refusal, not the marital relationship. [Discussion of guarantee and capital punishment:] Guarantee (daman) is obligated with ambiguity, so it is obligated by nukul. Capital punishment (qat') is not obligated with ambiguity, so it is not obligated by nukul. Thus it is like when a man and two women testify against him, or testimony upon testimony, or a judge's letter to a judge: guarantee of money is obligated therein but not capital punishment. [Commentary on 'before consummation':] And likewise after consummation. This is in 'al-Nata'ij'. The issue was placed in divorce before consummation because if it were absolute, it would refer to the divorce that entails the full dower, and the matter of divorce entailing half the dower remains hidden, so uncovering it is better, even though the obligation of the oath after consummation is a fortiori: if he is sworn before the dower is confirmed, then after it is even more so. This is in 'Majma' al-Anhar'. [Commentary on 'because he takes an oath in divorce by consensus':] Because it involves a claim of money, and the administration of oaths applies to money by consensus.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents two rulings from the chapter on litigation (da'wa), both centering on the effect of nukul (refusal to take an oath) in marriage-related claims. The first case: if a woman claims that her husband divorced her before consummation, and the husband denies it, the judge requires the husband to take an oath. This is established by consensus (ijma). If the husband refuses (nukul), he becomes liable for half the dower (mahr). The second case: if a woman claims that a valid marriage exists and demands her dower or maintenance, the husband is similarly required to take an oath. If he refuses, he must pay the claimed amount, but—and this is the key point—the marriage itself (halal) is not proven. This is the position of Abu Hanifa, stated explicitly in the text. The reasoning is that financial rights can be established by the defendant's refusal (bazl, i.e., nukul), but personal status matters (like the validity of marriage) cannot. The commentary further distinguishes between claims involving financial rights (daman) and those involving corporal or capital penalties (qat'). In financial claims, nukul establishes liability; in hudud or qisas, it does not. The author notes that the ruling applies whether divorce was before or after consummation, though the case is stated for before consummation to clarify the half-dower issue. The consensus for oath-taking in financial claims is affirmed.

Key Terms
نكول (nukul): Refusal by the defendant to take the required oath; this establishes the plaintiff's claim only for financial rights in Hanafi law.
استحلاف (istihlaf): The act of requiring the defendant to swear an oath in litigation.
يمين (yamin): Oath; in this context, the defendant's oath denying the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Only the divorce, not the dower
D) Neither the divorce nor the dower

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the defendant's refusal to take an oath (nukul) establishes only financial liability, never the underlying personal status of marriage or divorce.

Financial Rights from Nukul in Lineage Claims
Expanding the rule that nukul in lineage claims proves financial rights only.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that nukul in lineage claims establishes only the financial right, not the lineage itself, and cite the consensus proof.

Core Idea
Nukul proves the purse, not the pedigree — financial rights are settled without establishing lineage.

This passage addresses a procedural nuance in claims of lineage (nasab). When a plaintiff asserts a family relationship solely to secure a financial or custodial benefit (such as inheritance, maintenance, guardianship of a foundling, or prevention of gift revocation), the Hanafi school permits oath-taking and, upon refusal (nukul), rules in favor of the financial claim. However, the lineage itself remains unproven. The passage provides four practical examples and grounds the ruling in scholarly consensus (ijma).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the defendant's oath-taking in a lineage claim involving a financial right. The ruling is that the oath is wajib by consensus, and nukul leads to judgment for the financial right, not the lineage.
Consensus is the proof.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is ijma (consensus) that the defendant swears on lineage in such claims.
The passage uses the phrase 'إجماعاً' for each example.

Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Consensus implies that nukul (refusal to swear) results in judgment for the financial claim, but not for lineage. The wording 'يقضي بالمال دون النسب' makes the distinction explicit.
The consensus is qati' al-thubut and qati' al-dalala.
4
Examine the four examples
Each example (inheritance, maintenance, custody of a foundling, prohibition of gift revocation) illustrates the rule: nukul establishes the financial right, not the lineage.
Note that in custody, the right is custodial (financial in nature).

And likewise in lineage if he claims a right like inheritance and maintenance and others: i.e., he takes an oath in a claim of lineage if the claimant claims money, and by nukul the money is proven, not the lineage, according to Abu Hanifa. And others: like custody of a foundling, and prohibition of revocation in gift. [Commentary:] And likewise he takes an oath in lineage if a right is claimed, because the objective is this right and proving money, and the administration of oaths applies to such, so upon nukul money is proven, not lineage. [Commentary on 'inheritance':] That Zayd claims against 'Amr that he is the brother of the defendant, and their father died leaving property in the defendant's hands; he takes an oath on lineage by consensus; if he swears, he is free; if he refuses, judgment is given for the money, not lineage. [Commentary on 'maintenance':] That a disabled person claims against a wealthy person that he is his brother and his maintenance is upon him; the defendant denies the brotherhood and takes an oath by consensus; if he refuses, judgment is given for maintenance, not lineage. [Commentary on 'custody of a foundling':] That there is a boy who cannot express himself in the hands of a finder; a free woman claims his brotherhood to take him from the finder by right of custody; she wants to have him swear, and he refuses; custody is proven for her, not lineage. Commentary
This passage expands the same principle to claims of lineage (nasab). The rule is that when the plaintiff asserts a family relationship for the purpose of obtaining a financial right (inheritance, maintenance, custody, or prevention of gift-revocation), the defendant is required to take an oath. This is by consensus (ijma). If the defendant refuses (nukul), the judge rules in favor of the plaintiff for the financial right, but the lineage itself is not proven as a matter of legal status. This is the position of Abu Hanifa. The commentary provides concrete examples: a brother claiming inheritance, a disabled person claiming maintenance from a wealthy brother, a woman claiming custody of a foundling as a sister, and a donee preventing gift-revocation by claiming brotherhood. In each case, nukul results in the financial or custodial right being established, but the lineage remains unproven. The logic is that oath-taking is a tool for settling financial disputes (due to consensus), and its effect is limited to the financial claim, not the underlying personal status. This is consistent with the Hanafi distinction between financial matters (where nukul is effective) and personal status matters (where it is not).

Key Terms
نسب (nasab): Lineage or family relationship; in this context, a claim of brotherhood or other familial tie asserted for a financial benefit.
حجر في اللقيط (hajr fi al-laqit): Custody or guardianship of a foundling; in the example, a woman claims sisterhood to obtain custody.

Oath-Taking in Retaliation Claims: Murder and Lesser Injuries
Ruling on nukul in qisas claims with ijma proof

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the consequence of nukul in retaliation claims for murder versus lesser injuries and identify the proof (ijma) for the initial oath requirement.

Core Idea
In retaliation claims, nukul in murder leads to imprisonment, not immediate qisas — due to the gravity of the claim.

This lesson examines a concise but important rule in Hanafi judicial procedure: how a defendant's refusal to take an oath (nukul) affects retaliation claims. The passage distinguishes between capital and non-capital claims, showing that the law treats murder with extra caution. Understanding this distinction clarifies a key difference between qisas for life versus limb.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The act is the defendant taking an oath in a qisas claim. The text states: 'he takes an oath by consensus' – meaning the oath is wajib (obligatory).
Consensus (ijma) is the proof.
2
Locate the dalil
The phrase 'يحلف إجماعاً' directly cites ijma as the evidence. Ijma is a qat'i (certain) source of law.
The proof is explicit in the text.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Ijma establishes that the oath is an integral part of qisas litigation. The consensus of scholars makes it binding.
Ijma as proof cannot be overridden by istihsan here.
4
Distinguish consequences of nukul
If the defendant refuses the oath (nukul): in a murder claim, he is imprisoned until he confesses or takes the oath; in lesser injury claims, qisas is directly enforced.
The severity of the crime influences the procedure.
5
Note the practical difference
Imprisonment in murder ensures additional certainty before capital punishment, while for lesser injuries, refusal establishes the claim with less caution.
This is a unique Hanafi safeguard.

Nukul in Lineage Claims: Financial Rights Proven, Lineage Not
Ruling with commentary on nukul in lineage claims

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that nukul (refusal of oath) in a lineage-based financial claim proves the financial right but not the lineage, and apply it to examples of inheritance, maintenance, custody, and gift revocation.

Core Idea
Nukul proves the financial claim but not the underlying lineage.

When a plaintiff bases a financial claim on an alleged familial relationship, the defendant is required to swear an oath denying the lineage. If the defendant refuses to swear (nukul), the financial right is established, but the lineage itself remains unproven. This lesson examines the rationale, examples, and an important objection and response that clarifies the separation between proof in reality and proof in court.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core ruling
Read the passage: 'through nukul the property is proven, not the lineage, according to Abu Hanifa.' State this as the central rule.
The rule applies when a financial right is claimed based on lineage.
2
Examine the examples
Work through each example provided: inheritance, maintenance, custody of a foundling, and prohibition of gift revocation. For each, identify the financial right and why nukul proves it without lineage.
The financial right is the primary purpose; the oath is a tool to resolve it.
3
Analyze the objection and response
A hypothetical objection: proving dower without marriage seems inconsistent. The response distinguishes between reality and judicial proof. Understand this logical distinction.
Judicial proof can be partial; evidence may suffice for one element but not for another.
4
Apply the rule to a new scenario
Using the rule, decide the outcome in a novel case (e.g., a claim for back rent based on alleged tenancy). Determine what nukul proves.
The rule is specific to lineage-based claims; for other relationships, different rules may apply.
5
Compare with the rule in qisas
Note the exception: in claims for life (qisas), nukul does not lead to immediate judgment; the defendant is imprisoned until confession or oath. Understand the rationale.
Life cases involve severe consequences; hence a stricter procedure.

(Likewise in lineage if he claims a right like inheritance and maintenance): meaning he swears in a claim of lineage if the plaintiff claims property, so through nukul the property is proven, not the lineage, according to Abu Hanifa. (And others): like the guardianship of a foundling, and the prohibition of returning a gift. We say: Proof of dower in reality necessitates proof of marriage in reality, but its proof before the judge does not necessitate proof of marriage before him because it is possible that evidence is established for the first without the second, and the necessary from the issue is proof of dower before the judge without proof of marriage before him, and there is no harm in it because of lack of necessity. He said: 'Likewise... etc.': meaning likewise he swears in lineage if he claims a right, because the purpose is this right and proving property, and the oath is applied in similar cases, so upon nukul, property is proven without lineage. He said: 'And maintenance': such as a chronically ill person claiming against a wealthy person that he is his brother and his maintenance is upon him, so the defendant denies the brotherhood, he swears by consensus, if he refuses judgment is given for maintenance without lineage. He said: 'Like guardianship of a foundling': such as a child who cannot express himself being in the hand of a finder, and a free woman claims his brotherhood intending to take the foundling by right of custody, and she wants to have him swear, and he refuses, so guardianship is proven for her without lineage. He said: 'And prohibition of returning a gift': such as the donor wants to return, so the donee says: I am your brother so you cannot return, so the donor is made to swear, if he refuses the prohibition of return is proven but the lineage is not proven.

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with commentary on the consequences of nukul (refusal to swear an oath) in claims of lineage when a financial right is involved. The core ruling is that when a plaintiff claims a financial right based on an alleged lineage (e.g., brotherhood), the defendant is required to swear an oath denying the lineage. If the defendant refuses to swear (nukul), the financial right is proven, but the lineage itself is not established. This is the position of Imam Abu Hanifa. The passage provides several examples: inheritance (where a person claims brotherhood to inherit), maintenance (where a disabled person claims brotherhood for support), guardianship of a foundling (where a woman claims brotherhood to obtain custody), and prohibition of returning a gift (where the donee claims brotherhood to prevent revocation). In each case, the defendant is sworn, and upon nukul, the financial claim is upheld, but the lineage is not confirmed. The passage also includes an objection and response regarding the logical consistency of proving a dower without proving the marriage, which is analogous to proving financial rights without lineage. The response clarifies that while in reality the financial right and the underlying relationship are connected, in court they can be separated because evidence may establish one without the other. The commentary further explains that the purpose of the oath in such cases is to protect the financial right, not to establish lineage, which is why only the financial aspect is affected by nukul.

Key Terms
النكول (al-nukul): Refusal to take an oath after being required to do so in a legal claim.
الاستحلاف (al-istihlaf): The act of requiring the defendant to swear an oath to deny the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Nukul (refusal of oath) in a lineage-based financial claim proves the financial right but not the lineage; the two are separated in judicial proof.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute on Nukul in Retaliation Claims
Intra-Hanafi dispute on nukul in qisas for life vs. limb

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn differ on the effect of nukul in qisas claims and identify the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Nukul in limb retaliation leads to qisas for Abu Hanifa but financial compensation for the Sahibayn — a dispute rooted in the analogy of limbs to wealth.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya explores a sharp intra-Hanafi disagreement over what happens when a defendant refuses to swear in a retaliation lawsuit. The core question: does nukul allow immediate qisas or only financial liability? The answer depends on whether limbs are treated like wealth or like life.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa)
Identify what Abu Hanifa rules for nukul in a life claim versus a limb claim. Note the reasoning: limbs are like wealth.
2
State the Sahibayn's position
Identify the uniform rule of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad: nukul always leads to financial compensation (diyah/arsh) because it is an admission with shubha.
3
Follow the proof of each side
For Abu Hanifa, the qiyas (analogy) comparing limbs to wealth; for the Sahibayn, the principle that shubha prevents qisas. Locate these arguments in the passage.
4
Identify the thamarah al-khilaf
State the practical difference: in a limb claim, Abu Hanifa orders qisas (cutting the limb), while the Sahibayn order arsh (financial compensation).
5
Understand the Hanafi tarjih
The commentary defends Abu Hanifa's view against the objection about theft, implying it is the relied-upon position.
The objection relates to hadd theft, which is a pure right of Allah and thus requires certainty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله: وكذا منكر القود؛ يعني وكذا يحلف من ادعى قصاصاً على غيره فإن الأطراف بمنزلة الأموال، فيجري فيها البذل بخلاف النفس هذا عند أبي حنيفة، وأما عندهما يلزم الأرش في النفس وما دونها، فإن النكول إقرار فيه شبهة، فلا يثبت به القصاص، بل يلزم المال. فجحده سواء كان ذلك القصاص قصاصاً في النفس أو في الطرف، فإن نكل في دعوى النفس لم يقتص منه بل حبس، حتى يقر فيقتص منه، أو يحلف فيطلق عن الحبس، وإلا يحبس أبداً وإن نكل فيما دون النفس يقتص منه.
تقريره: إن الأطراف خلقت وقاية للنفس كالأموال، فلذا يسلك بها مسلك الأموال حتى أبيح قطعها للحاجة، ولا يجب على القاطع الضمان إذا قطعها بأمره بخلاف النفس، فإنه لو قتله بأمره يجب عليه القصاص في رواية، والدية في أخرى. وإذا سلك بها مسلك الأموال يجري فيها البذل كالأموال، إلا أنه لا يجوز قطعها بلا فائدة، وهذا البذل مفيد لدفع الخصومة، فصار قطع اليد للأكلة، وقلع السن للوجع، وإذا امتنع القصاص امتناعه في النفس، واليمين حق مستحق عليه يحبس فيه، كما في القسامة. وما أورده أبو المكارم من أنه يتوجه عليه لزوم قطع يد السارق بالنكول، وقد مر أنه لا يقطع، ليس بوارد؛ لأن فود الطرف حق العبد، فيثبت بالشبهة كالأموال، بخلاف القطع في السرقة، فإنه خالص حق الله، وهو لا يثبت بالشبهة، فظهر الفرق. فتدبر.
قوله: يلزم الأرش... الخ؛ تقريره: إن عندهما يلزمه الدية في النفس والطرف كليهما، فإن النكول إقرار فيه شبهة عندهما؛ لأنه إن امتنع عن اليمين تورعاً عن اليمين الصادقة لا يكون إقراراً، بل يكون بذلاً، فلما كان إقراراً فيه شبهة فلا يثبت به القصاص، ويجب به المال خصوصاً إذا كان امتناع القصاص لمعنى من جهة من يجب عليه.
He said: 'And likewise the denier of retaliation': meaning likewise swears the one who claims retaliation against another, for limbs are in the position of wealth, so compensation applies to them unlike the life. This according to Abu Hanifa. As for according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the financial compensation is necessary in both life and what is less than it, because nukul is an admission with ambiguity, so retaliation is not proven by it, rather financial liability is necessary. So his denial, whether that retaliation is in life or limb, if he refuses in a life claim, he is not retaliated against but is imprisoned until he confesses so retaliation is carried out, or he swears so he is released from prison, otherwise he is imprisoned forever. And if he refuses in what is less than life, retaliation is carried out against him. Its explanation: The limbs were created as a protection for life, like wealth, so they are treated as wealth to the extent that cutting them is permitted for necessity, and the cutter is not liable if he cuts by his order, unlike life, for if he kills him by his order, in one narration retaliation is obligatory, and in another, blood money. And when they are treated as wealth, compensation applies to them like wealth, except that cutting them is not permissible without benefit, and this compensation is beneficial to repel litigation, so cutting the hand for food, and pulling the tooth for pain, and when retaliation is prevented, it is prevented in life, and the oath is a right incumbent upon him, he is imprisoned for it, as in qasama. And what Abu al-Makarim objected, that the cutting of the thief's hand by nukul would be necessary, and it has passed that it is not cut, is not valid; because retaliation for a limb is a right of the servant, so it is proven by ambiguity like wealth, unlike cutting in theft, for it is a pure right of Allah, and it is not proven by ambiguity, so the difference is clear. So reflect. He said: 'Financial compensation is necessary... etc.': Its explanation: According to the two, blood money is necessary for both life and limb, because nukul is an admission with ambiguity according to them; for if he refrains from the oath out of caution from a truthful oath, it is not an admission, rather it is a compensation, so since it is an admission with ambiguity, retaliation is not proven by it, and financial liability is obligatory, especially when the prevention of retaliation is due to a reason from the side of the one upon whom it is obligatory.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This passage presents an intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani) regarding the effect of nukul (refusal to swear) in claims of retaliation (qisas). The context is that when a plaintiff claims qisas for a killing or injury, the defendant is required to swear an oath denying the act. According to Abu Hanifa, if the defendant refuses to swear in a claim for life, he is not immediately executed; instead he is imprisoned until he either confesses (leading to qisas) or swears (leading to release). If he refuses in a claim for a limb (below life), then qisas is carried out. The rationale is that limbs are akin to wealth (amwal) because they are created for protection of life, and compensation can be accepted for them, similar to financial transactions. In contrast, life is more sacred and cannot be taken by mere nukul. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold that in both life and limb cases, nukul leads to financial compensation (diyah or arsh), not qisas. Their reasoning is that nukul is an admission with ambiguity (shubha) because the defendant might refuse out of caution against swearing falsely, not necessarily because he admits the claim. Such an ambiguous admission cannot establish qisas, which requires certainty; instead, financial liability is imposed. The passage then addresses an objection: if limbs are treated like wealth, then cutting the hand of a thief could be established by nukul, but it is known that the hand is not cut based on nukul in theft. The response distinguishes: the right to qisas for a limb is a private right of the victim (haqq al-'abd), which can be proven by ambiguous evidence, whereas the hadd punishment for theft is a pure right of Allah (haqq Allah), which requires absolute certainty and cannot be established by shubha. The passage concludes with a reflective note.

Key Terms
القود (al-qawad): Retaliation or qisas, either for life (naps) or for limbs (ma duna al-naps).
الأرش (al-arsh): Financial compensation for injuries short of life, analogous to diyah but for limbs and wounds.
الشبهة (al-shubhah): Ambiguity or doubt that prevents the application of a severe punishment or retaliation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what happens if a defendant refuses to swear in a claim for retaliation for a lost hand (limb)?
A) He is imprisoned until he confesses.
B) He is subjected to qisas for the hand.
C) He must pay arsh (financial compensation).
D) The case is dismissed.

Key Takeaway
Nukul in a retaliation claim leads to different outcomes depending on whether the claim is for life or limb, and on whether one follows Abu Hanifa or the Sahibayn.

Swearing the Defendant When Plaintiff Claims Witnesses Are Present: Abu Hanifa vs. Abu Yusuf
Intra-Hanafi dispute on istihlaf with witness claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the disagreement between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf regarding the defendant's oath when the plaintiff claims to have witnesses present, and identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf).

Core Idea
The right to demand an oath is contingent on inability to produce witnesses — claiming witnesses present blocks the oath.

In judicial procedure, a plaintiff may demand the defendant's oath if he cannot produce witnesses. But what if the plaintiff claims to have witnesses in the city? Can he still demand the oath, or must he first bring his witnesses? This text presents a classic intra-Hanafi dispute between Imam Abu Hanifa and Imam Abu Yusuf, with Muhammad al-Shaybani's position varying. Understanding this ikhtilaf sharpens our appreciation of how the Hanafi school derives procedural rulings from a single hadith.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core question
The issue is whether a defendant can be sworn when the plaintiff claims to have witnesses present in the city (al-misr) but not necessarily in the court session.
Presence in the city is different from presence in the court (al-majlis).
2
State Abu Hanifa's position and his proof
Abu Hanifa holds that the defendant is not sworn. His proof: the well-known hadith where the Prophet (sallallahu alayhi wa sallam) said, 'Do you have witnesses?' and when the plaintiff said 'No,' the Prophet said 'You have his oath.' This shows the oath is only when witnesses are absent. Also, swearing before witnesses causes humiliation if they appear later.
The hadith is cited in the text: 'ألك بينة فقال : لا ، فقال : لك يمينه'.
3
State Abu Yusuf's position and his proof
Abu Yusuf holds that the defendant is sworn because the oath is the plaintiff's right based on the same hadith, interpreted broadly. The plaintiff has valid purposes: avoiding travel expenses, immediate resolution (by admission or nukul). Moreover, the witnesses might not be accepted.
Abu Yusuf analogizes to when witnesses are outside the city, where the oath is given by agreement.
4
Examine Muhammad al-Shaybani's dual narrations
Muhammad has two reported positions: according to al-Khassaf, he agrees with Abu Yusuf; according to al-Tahawi, he agrees with Abu Hanifa. The more relied-upon view (a'zam al-mu'tabirat) is that he follows Abu Hanifa.
This illustrates that later authorities settled the dispute in favor of Abu Hanifa.
5
Identify the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
According to Abu Hanifa, the judge does not administer the oath; the plaintiff must bring witnesses. According to Abu Yusuf, the judge administers the oath, giving the plaintiff a chance to win by nukul (defendant's refusal to swear).
The difference affects case outcomes: victory by witness vs. victory by oath.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
[1] قوله فإن قال ... الخ ؛ أي إن قال المدعي لي بينة حاضرة في المصر، وطلب يمين خصمه لم يحلف، وهذا عند الإمام ، وقال أبو يوسف : يستحلف ؛ لأن اليمين حقه بالحديث المعروف، فإذا طالبه به يجيبه ، وله غرض صحيح في الاستحلاف، وهو أن يدفع به مؤنة المسافة ويتوسل به إلى حقه في الحال بإقراره أو نكوله، وفي البينة احتمال فلعلها لا تقبل فيجيبه إذا طلبه، كما إذا كانت خارج المصر. وللإمام له : إن ثبوت الحقِّ في اليمين مرتَّب على العجز عن إقامة البينة، فلا يكون حقه دونه، كما إذا كانت البينة حاضرة في المجلس بخلاف ما إذا كانت خارج المصر؛ لأنه قد يتعدَّرُ عليه الجمع بين خصمه وشهوده، فكان عاجزاً ؛ ولأن في استحلافه مع حضور الشهود هتك المسلم إذا أقام البينة بعد [ما] حلف فيجب أن يتوقاه. وفي الاستدلال بالحديث المعروف وهو قوله الله للمدعي : «ألك بينة فقال : لا ، فقال : لك يمينه ، فقالك يحلف ولا يبالي ، فقال : ليس لك إلا شاهداك أو يمينه)، كما تقدم ذكره ، نظراً لأنه و إنما جعل له اليمين عند فقده البينة ومحمد مع أبي يوسف له فيما ذكره الخصاف ، ومع الإمام فيما ذكره الطحاوي له كما في أكثر المعتبرات (۳). (۱) هذا إذا كانت البينة حاضرة في المصر غائبة عن مجلس الحكم حتى كانت غائبة عن المصر يحلف، أو كانت في مجلس الحكم لا يحلف . ينظر : فتح باب العناية»(٣: ١٦٧). (۲) سبق تخريجه. (۳) ينظر: تبيين الحقائق (٤ : ٣٠٠).
[1] His statement: 'If he says: I have witnesses present...' etc.; its meaning: If the plaintiff says, 'I have witnesses present in the city,' and asks for the oath of his opponent, he (the opponent) is not sworn. This according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). Abu Yusuf said: He is sworn, because the oath is his right according to the well-known hadith. So if he demands it of him, he must respond, and he has a valid purpose in seeking the oath: to avoid the expense of travel and to obtain his right immediately through the opponent's admission or nukul. And there is a possibility that the witnesses may not be accepted, so he should respond if he asks, as when the witnesses are outside the city. For Imam (Abu Hanifa): The right to the oath is contingent upon inability to present witnesses, so it is not his right when witnesses are present, as when the witnesses are present in the court. Rather, if they are outside the city, he may be unable to bring them together with his opponent, so he is considered unable. Also, swearing him when witnesses are present would be an affront to the Muslim if he brings witnesses after swearing, so it must be avoided. And in the deduction from the well-known hadith — the Prophet said to the plaintiff: 'Do you have witnesses? He said: No. He said: You have his oath. He said: [?He swears and does not care?] He said: You have no [right] except your two witnesses or his oath' — as previously mentioned, because the oath is only given to him when witnesses are absent. Muhammad is with Abu Yusuf in what al-Khassaf mentioned, and with Imam in what al-Tahawi mentioned, as in most reliable works. (1) This is when the witnesses are present in the city but absent from the court; if they are absent from the city, he is sworn; if they are present in the court, he is not sworn. See Fath Bab al-Inaya. (2) Previously cited. (3) See Tabyin al-Haqa'iq.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage discusses a dispute between Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf (with Muhammad having dual narrations) regarding the procedure when the plaintiff claims to have witnesses present in the city but not in the court. The issue is whether the judge should administer an oath to the defendant if the plaintiff requests it. According to Abu Hanifa (the Imam), the judge should not administer the oath because the plaintiff's right to demand an oath is contingent upon his inability to present witnesses. Since he claims to have witnesses present, he is not unable. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that the hadith grants the plaintiff the right to demand an oath regardless of the presence of witnesses, and there are practical benefits: avoiding travel expenses and immediate resolution. Abu Hanifa responds that swearing the defendant when witnesses are present risks embarrassing the Muslim if he later brings witnesses after swearing. The commentary notes that Muhammad al-Shaybani's position varies: according to al-Khassaf, he agrees with Abu Yusuf; according to al-Tahawi, he agrees with Abu Hanifa. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) is not explicitly stated here but is found in other sources. The practical consequence: if witnesses are in the city but not in the court, the defendant is not sworn (Abu Hanifa) or is sworn (Abu Yusuf). If they are outside the city, the defendant is sworn by agreement. If they are in the court, the defendant is not sworn.

Key Terms
بينة حاضرة (bayyinah hadirah): Witnesses present in the city (al-misr) but not necessarily in the court session.
استحلاف (istihlaf): The act of the judge administering an oath to the defendant upon the plaintiff's demand.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Administer the oath to the defendant immediately.
B) Deny the request and require the plaintiff to bring his witnesses first.
C) Imprison the plaintiff for making a false claim.
D) Dismiss the case altogether.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the defendant is not sworn if the plaintiff claims to have witnesses present in the city, because the right to the oath is contingent on inability to produce witnesses.

Oath and Surety When Plaintiff Has a Present Witness
Intra-Hanafi dispute on oath and surety

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions on oath and surety when a plaintiff claims a present witness, and explain the role of istihsan in overriding qiyas.

Core Idea
The plaintiff's right to an oath is contingent on inability to produce evidence; a present witness bars the oath and triggers surety by istihsan.

When a plaintiff claims to have a witness present in the city but demands the defendant's oath, a key intra-Hanafi dispute arises. Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the oath is not given, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad offer different views. This lesson unpacks the rulings on the oath and the subsequent surety requirement, showing how istihsan overrides strict analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
The plaintiff says 'I have a present witness in the city' and requests the defendant's oath. Determine whether the oath is administered and what happens if the defendant refuses to swear.
The witness is in the city (miṣr) but not necessarily in the court session.
2
Analyze the oath dispute
Compare the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa (no oath) and Abu Yusuf (oath is a right). Understand the underlying dalil: the hadith about oath when no evidence, and the principle that the right to oath is contingent on inability to produce evidence.
Muhammad is reported to side with both, but the relied-upon position is Imam's.
3
Examine the surety dispute
When the defendant refuses to swear, the court requires a surety (kafil) for three days (Imam). Compare with Abu Yusuf's view (until next court session) and Muhammad's exception (if defendant is well-known or claim trivial). Understand the role of istihsan in overriding qiyas.
Shafi'i rejects surety before evidence; Hanafi istihsan protects the plaintiff's right.
4
Apply the rule to practical scenarios
Consider concrete cases: plaintiff claims a present witness but demands oath; defendant is well-known and unlikely to abscond; defendant is a stranger. Determine whether oath is given and/or surety required.
The key is the balance between protecting the plaintiff and not harming the defendant unnecessarily.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب الدعوى تعريفها وأحكامها
فإن قال : لي بينة حاضرة
(فإن قال : لي بينةٌ حاضرة)(۱)
القصاص، كما إذا أقرَّ مَن يجب عليه القصاص بالخطأ، والولي يدعي العمد فيحنئذ يجب المال، وفيما نحن فيه كذلك ؛ لأنه لم يصرح بالإقرار فأشبه الخطأ.
أما إذا كان من جهةِ مَن له القصاص كما إذا أقام على ذلك رجلاً وامرأتين لا يقضي عليه بشيء، وكذا بالشهادة على الشهادة لا يقضي بشيء ؛ لأن القصاص سقط فيه لمعنى من جهةِ مَن له، فلا يجب عليه شيء.
وفي النكول لمعنى من جهة من عليه فيصارَ إلى الأرش لا محالة ؛ لأن المال فيه أصل ثم يتعدى إلى الحد، فإذا اقتصر يبقى الأصل على حاله، وهاهنا الأصلُ القصاص ثم يتعدى إلى المال إذا وجد شرطه. فتدبر فيه.
قوله فإن قال ... الخ ؛ أي إن قال المدعي لي بينة حاضرة في المصر، وطلب يمين خصمه لم يحلف، وهذا عند الإمام ، وقال أبو يوسف : يستحلف ؛ لأن اليمين حقه بالحديث المعروف، فإذا طالبه به يجيبه ، وله غرض صحيح في الاستحلاف، وهو أن يدفع به مؤنة المسافة ويتوسل به إلى حقه في الحال بإقراره أو نكوله، وفي البينة احتمال فلعلها لا تقبل فيجيبه إذا طلبه، كما إذا كانت خارج المصر.
وللإمام له : إن ثبوت الحقِّ في اليمين مرتَّب على العجز عن إقامة البينة، فلا يكون حقه دونه، كما إذا كانت البينة حاضرة في المجلس بخلاف ما إذا كانت خارج المصر؛ لأنه قد يتعدَّرُ عليه الجمع بين خصمه وشهوده، فكان عاجزاً ؛ ولأن في استحلافه مع حضور الشهود هتك المسلم إذا أقام البينة بعد [ما] حلف فيجب أن يتوقاه. وفي الاستدلال بالحديث المعروف وهو قوله الله للمدعي : «ألك بينة فقال : لا ، فقال : لك يمينه ، فقالك يحلف ولا يبالي ، فقال : ليس لك إلا شاهداك أو يمينه)، كما تقدم ذكره ، نظراً لأنه و إنما جعل له اليمين عند فقده البينة ومحمد مع أبي يوسف له فيما ذكره الخصاف ، ومع الإمام فيما ذكره الطحاوي له كما في أكثر المعتبرات.
وطلب حلف الخصم لا يحلف ويكفل بنفسه ثلاثة أيام، فإن أبي لازمه أي في المصر، حتّى لو قال : لا بينةَ لي ، أو شهودي غُيَّب، يحلف ولا يكفل،
وطلب حلف الخصم لا يحلف ويكفل بنفسه ثلاثة أيام، فإن أبي لازمه قوله : أي في المصر ؛ قيد الشارح له بقوله في المصر ؛ لأن البينة لو كانت في مجلس الحكم لا يحلف بالاتفاق، وان كانت خارج المصر يحلف بالاتفاق، فلعله أشار إلى ما اختلف فيه وإلى ما اختاره الفقهاء.
قوله : يحلف ولا يكفل ؛ لأنه لا فائدة في التكفيل، فإنَّ الفائدة هو الحضور عند حضور الشهود وذلك في الهالك محال.
قوله : ويكفل ؛ كيلا يُغيِّب نفسه فيضيعُ حقُّ المدعي، والقياس : أن لا يؤخذ كفيل قبل إقامة البينة لعدم تعلق حق المدعي حينئذ فإن مجرد الدعوى ليس سبباً للاستحقاق كيف وقد عارضه الخصم بالإنكار. فينبغي أن لا يجب عليه إعطاء الكفيل، وهو مذهب الشافعي له، لكن استحساناً فيه ؛ لأن في أخذ الكفيل نظراً للمدعي ، وليس فيه كثير ضرر بالمدعى عليه ؛ لأنه إن لم يكن من قصده الاختفاء لا يتضرر، وإن كان من قصده كان ظالماً فلا ينظر له، فيكفل احتياطاً، ثمّ يجب أن يكون الكفيل ثقةً معروفاً بين الناس لا يتوهم اختفاؤه حتى يحصل به فائدة التكفيل. قوله : ثلاثة أيام ؛ هذا التقدير مروي عن الإمام، وهو الصحيح. كما في «الهداية»، و«الكافي»، وعن أبي يوسف له : إنه مقدر بما بين مجلس القاضي حتى إذا كان يجلس في كل يوم يكفل إلى اليوم الثاني، وإن كان يجلس في كل عشرة أيام يوما يكفل إلى عشرة أيام وصححه في «الخانية»، ولا فرق في الظاهر بين الخامل والوجيه والحقير من المال والخطير. كذا في «الهداية». وعن محمد : إنه إن كان معروفاً ، والظاهرُ من حاله لا يخفي نفسه بذلك القدر من المال لا يجبر على إعطاء الكفيل، وكذا لو كان المدعي حقيراً لا يخفي الإنسانُ نفسه لأجله لا يجبر على التكفيل، لكن إذا أعطى هو باختياره يؤخذ منه. كذا في «التبيين».
The book of judicial claims: its definition and rulings. So if he says: I have a present witness... (If he says: I have a present witness) 117... Retaliation, just as if the one upon whom retaliation is due acknowledges by mistake, and the guardian claims intent, then payment is due. And in our case it is likewise because he did not explicitly acknowledge, so it resembles mistake. As for when it comes from the one entitled to retaliation, as if he brings a man and two women regarding that, nothing is adjudged against him. Likewise, by testimony upon testimony nothing is adjudged, because retaliation is dropped due to a meaning from the one entitled, so nothing is due from him. And in refusal (nukul) due to a meaning from the one against whom it is claimed, recourse to compensation is inevitable, because payment therein is an original that then extends to hadd. So when it is limited, the original remains in its state. And here the original is retaliation, then it extends to payment when its condition exists. So ponder it. His words 'if he says' etc.: i.e., if the plaintiff says 'I have a present witness in the city' and requests the oath of his opponent, he is not sworn in. This according to Imam. Abu Yusuf said: he is sworn in, because the oath is his right by the well-known hadith. So when he demands it, he must fulfill it. And he has a valid purpose in the swearing, which is to remove the burden of travel and to attain his right immediately by his acknowledgment or refusal. And there is possibility regarding the witness, perhaps it will not be accepted, so he must respond when he requests, as if the witness were outside the city. And for Imam: the establishment of right regarding the oath is contingent upon inability to produce the evidence, so his right does not exist without that, as when the evidence is present in the session, contrary to when outside the city, because it may be difficult for him to bring together his opponent and his witnesses, so he is unable. And because swearing him when witnesses are present disgraces the Muslim if he produces evidence after he has sworn, so it must be avoided. And in deducing by the well-known hadith, namely that the judge said to the plaintiff: 'Do you have evidence? He said: No. He said: You have his oath.' He said: 'He swears and doesn't care.' He said: 'You have only your two witnesses or his oath,' as previously mentioned. This is because the oath is only given to him when he lacks evidence. And Muhammad is with Abu Yusuf according to what al-Khassaf mentioned, and with Imam according to what al-Tahawi mentioned, as in most reliable sources. And if he requests the oath of the opponent, he is not sworn in, and he provides a surety for himself for three days. If he refuses, he is incarcerated (i.e., in the city), so that even if he says 'I have no evidence' or 'my witnesses are absent', he is sworn in and does not provide a surety. And if he requests the oath of the opponent, he is not sworn in and he provides a surety for himself for three days. If he refuses, he is incarcerated. His words: i.e., in the city; the commentator restricted it to in the city because if the evidence were in the session, he would not be sworn in by agreement, and if outside the city he would be sworn in by agreement. Perhaps he indicated what is disputed and what the jurists selected. His words: he is sworn in and does not provide a surety, because there is no benefit in the surety, as the benefit is his presence when the witnesses are present, and that is impossible in the perishing [owner?]. His words: and he provides a surety, so that he does not hide himself and thus waste the right of the plaintiff. The analogy is that a surety should not be taken before establishing evidence because the right of the plaintiff is not yet established; mere claim is not a cause for entitlement, especially since the opponent opposes with denial. So he should not be obliged to give a surety, and that is the madhhab of al-Shafi'i. However, it is istihsan here, because taking the surety is a consideration for the plaintiff, and there is no great harm to the defendant because if he does not intend to hide, he is not harmed; if he intends to hide, he is oppressor, so no consideration for him. So he provides a surety as a precaution. Then the surety must be trustworthy and well-known among people so that his disappearance is not expected, so that the benefit of the surety is achieved. His words: three days; this estimation is narrated from Imam, and it is correct, as in al-Hidayah and al-Kafi. From Abu Yusuf: it is estimated by the interval between court sessions, so that if he sits every day, he sureties until the second day; if he sits once every ten days, he sureties for ten days. And it is corrected in al-Khaniyyah. And there is no difference apparently between the humble and the prominent and the insignificant in wealth and the important. As in al-Hidayah. And from Muhammad: if he is well-known and his apparent state indicates that he will not hide himself for that amount of wealth, he is not forced to provide a surety. Likewise, if the plaintiff is insignificant, so that a person would not hide himself for his sake, he is not forced to suretyship. But if he gives voluntarily, it is taken from him. As in al-Tabyin.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the ruling when a plaintiff claims to have a witness present in the city and demands that the defendant take an oath. The text first presents the scenario: if the plaintiff says 'I have a present witness' and asks for the defendant's oath, the defendant is not sworn in according to Imam Abu Hanifa. Abu Yusuf disagrees, arguing that the oath is a right of the plaintiff based on the ḥadīth 'Your oath is upon him' (or similar), and he has a valid purpose in requesting it: to avoid the burden of bringing witnesses and to potentially secure his right immediately through confession or refusal (nukūl). Imam counters that the right to an oath is contingent upon inability to produce evidence; if the plaintiff has a witness present, he is not considered unable. Moreover, swearing the defendant when witnesses are present could lead to disgrace if the plaintiff later produces evidence after the oath, which must be avoided. The ḥadīth cited applies only when the plaintiff lacks evidence. Muhammad al-Shaybānī is reported to side with Abu Yusuf in some narrations (by al-Khaṣṣāf) and with Imam in others (by al-Ṭaḥāwī), but the relied-upon position is that of Imam. The second part discusses the surety (kafālah). If the defendant refuses to swear, he is not forced to swear but must provide a surety for three days to ensure he does not abscond. If the plaintiff says he has no evidence or that his witnesses are absent, the defendant is sworn in and no surety is required. The reason for the surety is to protect the plaintiff's right; analogical reasoning (qiyās) would not require a surety at this stage, but istiḥsān (juristic preference) demands it to avoid harm to the plaintiff. Imām Shāfiʿī disagreed. The duration of the surety is three days according to Imam, while Abu Yusuf ties it to the interval between court sessions. From Muhammad, if the defendant is well-known or the claim is trivial, no surety is forced. The commentary explains the practical applications and variations among the three imams.

Key Terms
بينة حاضرة (bayyina ḥāḍira): A witness who is present in the city (miṣr) but not necessarily in the court session.
استحلاف (istiḥlāf): The act of demanding or administering an oath to the defendant.
تكفيل (takfīl): Providing a surety (kafil) to guarantee the defendant's presence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, why is the defendant not sworn in when the plaintiff has a present witness in the city?
A) Because the defendant has a right to avoid oaths.
B) Because the right to an oath is contingent on inability to produce evidence.
C) Because the plaintiff must first bring the witness to court.
D) Because the defendant is presumed innocent until proven guilty.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a present witness eliminates the right to an oath but triggers a surety by istihsan to protect the plaintiff's right, with variations among the three imams on duration and exceptions.
