The Inter-Madhhab Dispute on Surety Before Evidence
Inter-madhhab dispute on surety before evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school requires a defendant to provide a surety before evidence and evaluate the Shafi'i counter-argument based on qiyas.

Core Idea
Istihsan overrides qiyas: protecting the claimant's right precedes the defendant's inconvenience in Hanafi judicial procedure.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya addresses a key procedural difference between the Hanafi and Shafi'i schools: whether a defendant can be compelled to provide a surety (kafil) before the claimant has presented any evidence. The Hanafi school employs istihsan (juristic preference) to depart from the strict qiyas that would otherwise prevent such a requirement.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core dispute
Determine the precise question at issue: can a judge order the defendant to provide a surety before the claimant has produced evidence?
The dispute is about the timing of the surety, not its existence.
2
State the Shafi'i position and its proof
Imam al-Shafi'i holds that no surety can be required before evidence, because a mere claim does not establish a right. His evidence is qiyas: the asl is the general rule that no obligation arises from an unproven claim.
The opposing school's dalil is qiyas jali.
3
State the Hanafi position and its proof
The Hanafi school, citing istihsan, requires the surety to protect the claimant's potential right and prevent the defendant from absconding. The harm to a dishonest defendant is disregarded.
Istihsan is a stronger consideration in Hanafi usul.
4
Analyze the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: In Hanafi courts, the defendant may be forced to provide a surety or be surveilled before any evidence. In Shafi'i courts, no such measure can be taken until evidence is presented.
This affects the claimant's security.
5
Note the Hanafi tarjih
The Hanafi position is the mufta bihi within the Hanafi school, as confirmed by al-Marghinani in al-Hidayah and al-Kafi.
Tarjih authority: al-Marghinani.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وطلب حلف الخصم لا يحلف ويكفل بنفسه ثلاثة أيام، فإن أبي لازمه أي في المصر، حتّى لو قال : لا بينةَ لي ، أو شهودي غُيَّب، يحلف ولا يكفل،
وطلب حلف الخصم لا يحلف ويكفل بنفسه ثلاثة أيام، فإن أبي لازمه) [ ١ ] قوله : أي في المصر ؛ قيد الشارح الله له بقوله في المصر ؛ لأن البينة لو كانت في مجلس الحكم لا يحلف بالاتفاق، وان كانت خارج المصر يحلف بالاتفاق، فلعله أشار إلى ما اختلف فيه وإلى ما اختاره الفقهاء.
[٢]قوله : يحلف ولا يكفل ؛ لأنه لا فائدة في التكفيل، فإنَّ الفائدة هو الحضور عند حضور الشهود وذلك في الهالك محال.
[٣] قوله : ويكفل ؛ كيلا يُغيِّب نفسه فيضيعُ حقُّ المدعي، والقياس : أن لا يؤخذ كفيل قبل إقامة البينة لعدم تعلق حق المدعي حينئذ فإن مجرد الدعوى ليس سبباً للاستحقاق كيف وقد عارضه الخصم بالإنكار. فينبغي أن لا يجب عليه إعطاء الكفيل، وهو مذهب الشافعي له، لكن استحساناً فيه ؛ لأن في أخذ الكفيل نظراً للمدعي ، وليس فيه كثير ضرر بالمدعى عليه ؛ لأنه إن لم يكن من قصده الاختفاء لا يتضرر، وإن كان من قصده كان ظالماً فلا ينظر له، فيكفل احتياطاً، ثمّ يجب أن يكون الكفيل ثقةً معروفاً بين الناس لا يتوهم اختفاؤه حتى يحصل به فائدة التكفيل. [٤] قوله : ثلاثة أيام ؛ هذا التقدير مروي عن الإمام، وهو الصحيح. كما في «الهداية»(۱) ، و«الكافي»، وعن أبي يوسف له : إنه مقدر بما بين مجلس القاضي حتى إذا كان يجلس في كل يوم يكفل إلى اليوم الثاني، وإن كان يجلس في كل عشرة أيام يوما يكفل إلى عشرة أيام وصححه في «الخانية»، ولا فرق في الظاهر بين الخامل والوجيه والحقير من المال والخطير. كذا في «الهداية»(۳). وعن محمد : إنه إن كان معروفاً ، والظاهرُ من حاله لا يخفي نفسه بذلك القدر من المال لا يجبر على إعطاء الكفيل، وكذا لو كان المدعي حقيراً لا يخفي الإنسانُ نفسه لأجله لا يجبر على التكفيل، لكن إذا أعطى هو باختياره يؤخذ منه كذا في «التبيين (۳)
And the request for the opponent's oath: he does not take an oath but provides a surety himself for three days; if he refuses, he is required (to stay) within the city, even if he says 'I have no evidence' or 'my witnesses are absent', he takes an oath and does not provide a surety. [Commentary: The statement 'in the city' qualifies because if the evidence was in the court, he does not take an oath by agreement; if outside the city, he takes an oath by agreement. The statement 'he takes an oath and does not provide a surety' because there is no benefit in the surety; the benefit is appearance when witnesses are present, and in the case of the absent person this is impossible. The statement 'he provides a surety' is to prevent him from hiding and causing the claimant's right to be lost; the qiyas (analogy) is that no surety should be taken before evidence is produced, because the claimant's right is not yet established; mere claim is not a cause for entitlement especially when opposed by denial. Therefore, he should not be obliged to give a surety, and this is the madhhab of al-Shafi'i, but it is istihsan (juristic preference) to require it, because taking the surety considers the claimant and does not harm the defendant much; if he does not intend to hide, he is not harmed, and if he intends to hide, he is a wrongdoer and is not considered. So he provides a surety as a precaution. Then the surety must be trustworthy and well-known among the people so that his disappearance is not suspected, so that the benefit of the surety is achieved. The statement 'three days': this estimation is narrated from al-Imam (Abu Hanifa) and it is correct, as in al-Hidayah and al-Kafi. From Abu Yusuf: it is estimated by the period between court sessions; if the judge sits daily, he provides a surety until the next day; if every ten days, he provides a surety for ten days, and this is corrected in al-Khaniyah. There is no apparent difference between the obscure and the prominent, or between small and large amounts of money. Thus in al-Hidayah. From Muhammad: if he is well-known and apparently will not hide himself for that amount of money, he is not compelled to give a surety; similarly if the claimant is insignificant, one would not hide for his sake, he is not compelled to give a surety. But if he gives voluntarily, it is taken from him. Thus in al-Tabyin.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab and intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the defendant's obligation to provide a surety (kafil) or submit to surveillance in court. The matn states the basic rule: when the plaintiff demands an oath from the defendant, the defendant does not take an oath but instead provides a surety for three days; if he refuses, he is required to remain in the city. However, if the defendant declares he has no evidence or his witnesses are absent, he simply takes an oath and no surety is required. The commentary clarifies that the qualification 'in the city' is important: if the evidence is present in the court, the defendant does not take an oath by consensus; if the evidence is outside the city, he takes an oath. The reason the defendant is not given an oath when asked is that the benefit of the surety is to ensure his presence when witnesses come; for an absent witness, this is impossible. The core of the dispute is whether a surety can be demanded before the claimant produces evidence. The qiyas (analogy) of al-Shafi'i holds that since the claim alone does not establish a right, no surety should be taken. The Hanafi school, however, applies istihsan (juristic preference) to require the surety, because it protects the claimant's right and does not harm an honest defendant. The surety must be trustworthy. The duration of the surety/surveillance is given as three days according to Imam Abu Hanifa, which is the relied-upon position. Abu Yusuf ties it to the court schedule, and Muhammad considers the defendant's reputation and the triviality of the claim. The practical implication: in the Hanafi madhhab, a defendant may be forced to provide a surety or be followed for three days, whereas the Shafi'i school would not require a surety until evidence is presented.

Key Terms
كفيل (kafil): A surety who guarantees the defendant's appearance in court.
بينة (bayyinah): Evidence or witnesses in court.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that allows departure from qiyas for a stronger consideration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a defendant required to provide a surety before the claimant presents evidence?
A) Because the defendant is presumed guilty until proven innocent.
B) To protect the claimant's potential right from being lost if the defendant absconds.
C) Because the judge must ensure the defendant's presence for testimony.
D) To punish the defendant for denying the claim.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school, through istihsan, requires a defendant to provide a surety before evidence to protect the claimant's right, differing from the Shafi'i school which follows strict qiyas.

Intra-Hanafi Differences on the Duration of Surety and Surveillance
Intra-Hanafi dispute on duration of surety/surveillance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the views of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad on the duration of surety/surveillance and identify the mufta bihi position.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa fixed three days; Abu Yusuf tied it to court sessions; Muhammad exempted the well-known or trivial claims.

After establishing the obligation of surety, the Hanafi masters differed on how long the defendant must provide a surety or be under surveillance. This passage records the views of the three primary Hanafi authorities, with the relied-upon position being Imam Abu Hanifa's fixed three-day period.

Guided Path
1
Identify the default ruling from Imam Abu Hanifa
Imam Abu Hanifa specified a fixed period of three days for the surety or surveillance, regardless of the defendant's status or the claim amount.
This is the mufta bihi position.
2
Understand Abu Yusuf's contextual view
Abu Yusuf tied the duration to the frequency of court sessions: if the judge sits daily, the surety is until the next day; if every ten days, then ten days.
This view is corrected (sahhah) in al-Khaniyah.
3
Examine Muhammad's exemption-based view
Muhammad held that if the defendant is well-known and unlikely to hide, or if the claim is trivial and the defendant would not flee, then no compulsion to give a surety; but if given voluntarily, it is accepted.
This is recorded in al-Tabyin.
4
Determine the mufta bihi and the reason for tarjih
The relied-upon position is three days (Abu Hanifa) as stated in al-Hidayah and al-Kafi, because it provides a clear, uniform rule.
Tarjih authority: al-Marghinani.
5
Reflect on the wisdom behind each view
Abu Hanifa's fixed period ensures certainty; Abu Yusuf's ties to court sessions align with practicality; Muhammad's exemptions consider individual circumstances.
All views aim to balance claimant protection and defendant convenience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Duration of Surveillance for a Stranger Plaintiff
Ruling on limited surveillance for a stranger plaintiff.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the duration of surveillance for a stranger plaintiff in Hanafi fiqh and explain the rationale behind limiting it to the court session.

Core Idea
A stranger plaintiff's surveillance is capped at the court session to balance evidence collection with preventing harm to the defendant.

When a litigant is a stranger (gharib) — not a resident of the town where the court sits — the Hanafi school provides a special concession to avoid undue hardship. Instead of the standard three-day surveillance, the stranger is only required to watch the defendant for the duration of the judge's sitting. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its reasoning from the classical commentary.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key terms
Note the terms 'gharib' (stranger) and 'majlis al-hukm' (court session). They define the scope of the rule.
The stranger is not a resident; the session is the period the judge is present.
2
State the core ruling
From the matn: 'والغريب قدر مجلس الحكم' — the stranger is held only for the duration of the court session. Also, any surety is limited to the end of that session.
This is the key legal conclusion.
3
Understand the commentary's expansion
The sharh explains that the claimant follows the defendant only while the judge sits. He may follow him as he walks, but cannot confine him to one place, nor follow him into the mosque or into his home.
These details show the practical limits of surveillance.
4
Grasp the rationale
The purpose is to give the claimant a chance to bring evidence. Extending surveillance beyond the session would harm the defendant unnecessarily. Hence the rule balances both parties' rights.
Hanafi fiqh prioritizes preventing harm (darar) when possible.
5
Note the judge's options at session end
If the claimant fails to bring evidence by the end of the session, the judge may either administer an oath to the defendant or release him.
The judge has discretion; the defendant is not automatically liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والغريب قَدْرَ مجلس الحكم، ولا يكفل إلا إلى آخر المجلس
أي إن أبى الخصم عن إعطاء الكفيل لازمه المدَّعي ثلاثةَ أَيَّام ، ثُمَّ عطف على الضمير المنصوب في لازمه قوله : (والغريب قَدْرَ مجلس الحكم ) : أي لازم المدعي الغريب مقدار ما يكون القاضي جالساً في المحكمة ، ) ( ولا يكفل إلا إلى آخر المجلس ) : أي إن أخذ منه الكفيل لا يؤخذ إلا إلى آخر مجلس الحكم، فإن أتى بالبينة فيها ، وإلا يُحَلِّفَهُ إِن شاء أو يدعه.
[1] قوله : أي إن أبى... الخ أي إن أبى الخصم أن يكفل لا يجبره القاضي على التكفيل، بل يأمره أن يلازمه ويدور معه حيث يسير مقدار مدة التكفيل على القولين لا يغيب، ولا يلازمه مكاناً معيناً ولا يلازمه في المسجد ؛ لأنه بني للذكر، وإذا
حتى
انتهى إلى داره.
[٢] قوله : مقدار ما يكون القاضي جالساً في المحكمة ؛ لأن هذا القدر يحصل به النظر للمدعي ، فأما في إمساكه على باب القاضي يوماً أو أكثر ؛ ليحضر المدعي بينة ضرر على المطلوب، فإذا جاء أوان قيام القاضي عن المجلس ولم يحضر المدعي بينة ، فإن القاضي يحلفه ويخلي سبيل المطلوب ليذهب حيث شاء. [٣] قوله : إلا إلى آخر مجلس الحكم ؛ لأنه يلحقه الضرر بالزيادة على ذلك ، فلا يزاد عليه ولا ضرر في هذا القدر ظاهراً.
[٤]قوله وإلى ... الخ ؛ أي وإن لم يأت بالبينة، فالقاضي بالخيار إن شاءَ حلَّفَه إن شاء خلى سبيله.
And the stranger is (held) for the duration of the court session, and he is not provided a surety except until the end of the session. Meaning: if the defendant refuses to give a surety, the claimant is required to follow him for three days; then (the text) attached to the object pronoun in 'yalzamuhu' the statement: 'And the stranger for the duration of the court session' — meaning the claimant follows the stranger as long as the judge is sitting in the court; and 'he is not provided a surety except until the end of the session' — meaning if a surety is taken from him, it is only taken until the end of the court session. If he brings evidence during it, otherwise the judge may or may not administer an oath. [1] Explanation: If the defendant refuses to provide a surety, the judge does not force him, but orders the claimant to follow him and go wherever he goes for the duration of the surety period according to the two opinions, and he should not be absent. He does not follow him in a specific place, nor in the mosque because it is built for remembrance. When he reaches his home, (he stops). [2] Explanation: 'the duration the judge is sitting in the court' because this amount achieves consideration for the claimant. As for keeping him at the judge's door for a day or more for the claimant to bring evidence, that would harm the defendant. When the time comes for the judge to leave the session and the claimant has not brought evidence, the judge administers an oath and releases the defendant to go where he wishes. [3] Explanation: 'except until the end of the court session' because further harm would be caused to him; no increase is made upon him, and there is no apparent harm in this amount. [4] Explanation: '...and if not' etc.: meaning if he does not bring evidence, the judge has the option, if he wishes, to administer an oath or release him.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage provides a special rule for a stranger (gharib) in litigation. The matn states that a stranger plaintiff is only required to remain under surveillance for the duration of the court session, and any surety taken is only valid until the end of that session. The commentary elaborates: if the defendant refuses a surety, the claimant follows him during the court sitting. The claimant may follow the defendant as he walks, but cannot confine him to one place, and cannot follow him into the mosque. When the defendant reaches his home, the surveillance ends. The rationale is that the purpose of the surveillance is to allow the claimant to present evidence; extending it beyond the court session would cause undue harm to the defendant. If the claimant fails to bring evidence by the end of the session, the judge either administers an oath to the defendant or releases him. This rule balances the rights of both parties: the claimant gets the opportunity to bring evidence, but the defendant is not unduly restricted.

Key Terms
غريب (gharib): A stranger, i.e., a person not resident in the town where the court is located
مجلس الحكم (majlis al-hukm): The court session, the period during which the judge presides
لزم (lazima): To follow, surveil, or be bound to a person (here referring to the plaintiff's obligation to follow the defendant)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
For how long is a stranger (gharib) plaintiff required to surveil the defendant under the Hanafi rule?
A) Three days
B) Until the next court session
C) The duration of the judge's sitting
D) Indefinitely until evidence is brought

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school limits a stranger plaintiff's surveillance to the court session to balance the need for evidence with the avoidance of harm to the defendant.

Rules for Claimant Surveillance of Defendant
Practical rules for the claimant following the defendant during surveillance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi procedural rules for claimant surveillance of a defendant and apply them to scenarios involving male and female defendants.

Core Idea
Claimant surveillance is permissible but must respect modesty and avoid unnecessary harm.

When a claimant seeks to secure a defendant's presence before the judge, classical Hanafi law provides detailed rules to balance the right to monitor with the defendant's privacy and dignity. This lesson examines a passage from al-Ziyadat that specifies how the claimant may lawfully follow the defendant in different situations, including emergency entry into abandoned buildings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the context
The passage addresses luzum (following) in qada: the claimant may physically monitor the defendant to prevent flight. Recognize that this is a permissible procedural tool, not an obligation.
Luzum is a mechanism to secure presence; its rules are found in Hanafi books of qada.
2
Distinguish between male and female defendants
For a male, the claimant must either be granted permission to enter the house or sit at the door. For a female, more direct measures (following, sitting, holding clothes) are allowed to prevent escape while maintaining modesty.
The difference reflects societal norms and the need to ensure compliance.
3
Analyze the exception for ruined buildings
If the defendant flees into a kharibah (ruin), the claimant may enter only if he feels safe, and he must keep a distance, guarding her with his eye. This is permitted by necessity (darurah).
The condition of safety is crucial; otherwise, entry is not allowed.
4
Extract the underlying conditions
From the scenarios, derive the general rule: surveillance is allowed but must be limited to what is necessary, respectful, and safe for both parties.
This reflects the Hanafi principle of avoiding harm (la darar wa la dirar).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ذكر في «الزيادات»: إنه إما أن يأذن للمدَّعي في الدخول معه أو يجلس معه على باب الدار ؛ لأنه لو تركه يدخل ربَّما يهرب من جانب آخر، وإن كان الخصم امرأة لازمها وجلس معها وقبض على ثيابها.
فإن هربت ودخلت خربةً لا بأس بدخوله عليها إذا كان الرجل يأمن على نفسه ، ويكون بعيداً منها يحفظها بعينه ؛ لأن في هذه الخلوة ضرورة. ذكره شيخنا العلامة الطحطاوي في حاشيته على الدر المختار.
It is mentioned in al-Ziyadat: either he (the defendant) permits the claimant to enter with him, or he sits with him at the door of the house; because if he leaves him to enter, perhaps he will flee through another side. If the defendant is a woman, he (the claimant) follows her and sits with her and holds her clothes. If she flees and enters a ruined building, it is not objectionable for him to enter upon her if the man feels safe regarding himself, and he stays far from her, guarding her with his eye; because in such seclusion there is necessity. Our teacher, the scholar al-Tahtawi, mentioned this in his hashya on al-Durr al-Mukhtar.
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage from al-Ziyadat provides practical guidance for implementing the surveillance (luzum) of a defendant. It addresses two main scenarios: a male defendant and a female defendant. For a male, the claimant has two options: either the defendant allows him to enter the house, or the claimant sits at the door to prevent escape. For a female defendant, the claimant may follow her, sit with her, and even hold her clothes to prevent flight. If she escapes into a ruined building, the claimant may enter after her if he is safe, maintaining distance and guarding her with his eyes; this is allowed due to necessity. The reference to al-Tahtawi indicates that this is the relied-upon practice in the later Hanafi school. The principle is that the surveillance must be effective in keeping the defendant available while respecting modesty and avoiding unnecessary harm.

Key Terms
الزيادات (al-Ziyadat): One of the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah by Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani
لازمه (lazimahu): He follows him, i.e., the claimant monitors the defendant's movements to ensure he does not abscond
خربة (kharibah): A ruined or abandoned building

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits claimant surveillance of a defendant but with strict conditions to respect privacy and modesty, especially for female defendants.

The Object of a Judicial Oath: Swearing by Allah Only
Rule with proof on the object of judicial oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling that a judicial oath must be by Allah alone and cite the hadith proof.

Core Idea
A judicial oath is valid only if sworn by Allah; swearing by talaq or 'itaq is impermissible in principle.

This lesson examines the foundational rule that a judicial oath must be directed to Allah alone. The text presents clear proof from the Prophet's hadith and explains why oaths by divorce or emancipation are not permitted in the original ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act being ruled upon? It is the act of swearing in a judicial context. What is the hukm? The text states: 'The oath is by Allah, not by divorce and emancipation.' This indicates that swearing by Allah is obligatory (wajib) and swearing by other than Allah is prohibited (haram).
Note: The text does not explicitly say 'wajib' but the imperative in the hadith indicates obligation.
2
Locate the dalil
The author cites two hadith: one from Ibn 'Umar in Sahih al-Bukhari and Muslim: 'Whoever is to swear, let him swear by Allah or remain silent.' Another from Abu Hurayra in Sunan al-Nasa'i: 'Do not swear except by Allah.' Identify these as the proof.
The dalil is ahad but the meaning is clear.
3
Analyse the wajh al-dalalah
How do these hadith prove the ruling? The first uses the imperative 'li yahlif' which in Hanafi usul indicates obligation (wujub). The second uses a prohibition 'la tahlifu illa billah' which negates swearing by other than Allah. Together they establish that the object of a valid oath is only Allah.
This is a clear example of dalalat al-nass.
4
Note the authority weight
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah, the core Hanafi position. It is based on strong hadith and is agreed upon by the early Hanafi masters. The text presents it as the default rule.
The later dispensation does not contradict this but is an exception for necessity.

Source Text

(And it is intensified by His attributes) [1] His saying: And the oath is by Allah, not by divorce and emancipation; meaning the oath for which judgment is passed upon refusal is only by Allah, not by other than Him, and not by divorce and emancipation and the like; because of what is in the two Sahihs, from the hadith of Ibn `Umar that the Prophet ﷺ said: 'Whoever is to swear, let him swear by Allah or remain silent.' And because of what al-Nasa'i reported from the hadith of Abu Hurayra that he said: The Messenger of Allah ﷺ said: 'Do not swear except by Allah and do not swear unless you are truthful.' Thus it became clear that swearing is not permissible except by Allah Most High, not by divorce and emancipation and the like. [2] His saying: If the opponent insists... etc.; meaning if the opponent insists on making him swear by divorce or emancipation or the like, it is said: it is permissible for the judge to make him swear by that; because of the lack of care regarding the oath by Allah Most High. And he who said that it is permissible did not say it is prescribed (mashru`); rather he said it is not prescribed but he offers it to him, perhaps he will desist. For one of lesser piety does not swear falsely by such (divorce/emancipation), because it leads to divorce of his wife and emancipation of the slave girl, or holding them in forbidden marriage, unlike the oath by Allah, for people are very careless with it in our time. Then if he refuses (nukul), judgment is not passed against him for refusal, because he refrained from what is prohibited by Sharia; and if judgment were passed against him for refusal, it would not be executed. And if the defendant requests the swearing of the witness, or the plaintiff (requests) that he does not know that the witness is lying, the judge does not respond to him; because we are commanded to honor witnesses, and the plaintiff is not obligated to take an oath, especially if he has presented evidence. [3] His saying: And it is intensified by His attributes; meaning that the oath may be intensified by mentioning the attributes of Allah, because the states of people are ambiguous. Some of them refrain from the oath when intensified and become bold in its absence. (1) In Sahih al-Bukhari (2:951) and Sahih Muslim (3:1267) and others. (2) In Sunan al-Nasa'i (3:123).
Source pages p. 120

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the proper object of a judicial oath and an exceptional dispensation for later practice. The author begins by stating the foundational rule: an oath (which, if refused, leads to a judgment against the refuser) must be by Allah alone, not by divorce (talaq) or emancipation ('itaq). The proof is cited from hadith literature: two narrations, one in both Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim from Ibn 'Umar, and one in Sunan al-Nasa'i from Abu Hurayra, both explicitly restrict swearing to Allah. This establishes that swearing by other than Allah is impermissible in principle.

However, the passage then presents a later opinion (qil) that if the opposing party insists on the oath being by talaq or 'itaq, and the judge fears the defendant may take a false oath lightly by Allah alone, the judge may allow an oath by talaq or 'itaq. This is not a full endorsement of the practice as legally prescribed (mashru'), but rather a practical concession for judges to discourage false oaths. The rationale is that people in later times (i.e., the author's time) have become careless about oaths by Allah but are deterred by the serious consequences of a false oath by talaq (divorce of wife) or 'itaq (emancipation of slave). The ruling then clarifies that if the defendant refuses to swear by talaq/'itaq, no judgment of nukul (refusal) is issued against him, because he is refusing an action that is itself prohibited by Sharia. Such a refusal cannot be used as a basis for judgment.

The passage also mentions that the judge may intensify (tughliz) the oath by using the attributes of Allah (e.g., 'by Allah, the Mighty, the Great') because people vary in their awe of Allah. Some may be deterred by strong wording. This intensification is a recommended practice to encourage truthfulness.

Finally, the text notes that the defendant cannot request the oath of a witness or ask the plaintiff to swear about the witness's falsehood; the judge should not accommodate such requests because witnesses are to be honored, and the plaintiff is not required to swear, especially if he has presented evidence (bayyinah). The overall authority of the basic rule (oath by Allah only) is from Zahir al-Riwayah, but the dispensation for talaq/'itaq is based on later fatawa (mutakhkhirin) due to changed circumstances.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn in a judicial context, whereby a party affirms a claim or defense.
الطلاق (al-talaq): Divorce; swearing by talaq means making a statement contingent upon divorce.
العتاق (al-‘itaq): Emancipation; swearing by ‘itaq means making a statement contingent upon freeing a slave.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the original Hanafi ruling, what is the only permissible object of a judicial oath?
A) Allah
B) Allah and the Quran
C) Allah, Talaq, and 'Itaq
D) Any sacred object the judge chooses

Key Takeaway
A judicial oath must be by Allah alone; this is the established Hanafi position from the prophetic hadith.

The Dispensation for Oaths by Talaq/'Itaq and Intensification by Attributes
Intra-Hanafi dispensation and intensification of oaths

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi dispensation that allows judges to administer oaths by talaq/'itaq when the opponent insists, and state the ruling on refusal and intensification.

Core Idea
When people disregard oaths by Allah, judges may use oaths by talaq/'itaq as a stronger deterrent, but refusal does not incur judgment.

This lesson covers the exceptional opinion (qil) that permits judicial oaths by divorce or emancipation in later times. It also explains how the judge can intensify the oath using Allah's attributes to increase its effect, and what happens if the defendant refuses to swear by talaq/'itaq.

Guided Path
1
Understand the reason for the dispensation
The text states that in the author's time, people became careless about swearing by Allah. So when the opponent insists on a talaq/'itaq oath, a judge may administer it because it deters false oaths more effectively.
This is a response to changed social conditions.
2
Identify the permissive ruling
The phrase 'qil: sahha bihima fi zamanina' indicates that it is said (by some later authorities) that such oaths are valid in our time. It is a permissible (ja'iz) act for the judge, not an obligation.
This is not the original ruling but a practical concession.
3
Determine the consequence of refusal
If the defendant refuses to swear by talaq/'itaq, no judgment of nukul is passed against him, because he refused an oath that is not originally prescribed. The judge cannot force him and cannot punish him for refusal.
This protects the defendant from being penalized for refusing a non-standard oath.
4
Examine the ruling on intensification
The judge may intensify the oath by mentioning Allah's attributes (e.g., 'by Allah, the Great'). This is recommended to increase deterrence: some people are more careful with a strongly worded oath.
Intensification is not an innovation; it is based on the sunnah of the Prophet.
5
Note the limitation on requesting oaths
The text also addresses a procedural point: the defendant cannot request the oath of a witness, and the plaintiff cannot be asked to swear that he does not know the witness is lying. The judge should not accommodate such requests.
This is to protect witnesses and maintain the proper burden of proof.

This passage is a ruling with proof concerning the proper object of a judicial oath and an exceptional dispensation for later practice. The author begins by stating the foundational rule: an oath (which, if refused, leads to a judgment against the refuser) must be by Allah alone, not by divorce (talaq) or emancipation ('itaq). The proof is cited from hadith literature: two narrations, one in both Sahih al-Bukhari and Sahih Muslim from Ibn 'Umar, and one in Sunan al-Nasa'i from Abu Hurayra, both explicitly restrict swearing to Allah. This establishes that swearing by other than Allah is impermissible in principle.

However, the passage then presents a later opinion (qil) that if the opposing party insists on the oath being by talaq or 'itaq, and the judge fears the defendant may take a false oath lightly by Allah alone, the judge may allow an oath by talaq or 'itaq. This is not a full endorsement of the practice as legally prescribed (mashru'), but rather a practical concession for judges to discourage false oaths. The rationale is that people in later times (i.e., the author's time) have become careless about oaths by Allah but are deterred by the serious consequences of a false oath by talaq (divorce of wife) or 'itaq (emancipation of slave). The ruling then clarifies that if the defendant refuses to swear by talaq/'itaq, no judgment of nukul (refusal) is issued against him, because he is refusing an action that is itself prohibited by Sharia. Such a refusal cannot be used as a basis for judgment.

The passage also mentions that the judge may intensify (tughliz) the oath by using the attributes of Allah (e.g., 'by Allah, the Mighty, the Great') because people vary in their awe of Allah. Some may be deterred by strong wording. This intensification is a recommended practice to encourage truthfulness.

Finally, the text notes that the defendant cannot request the oath of a witness or ask the plaintiff to swear about the witness's falsehood; the judge should not accommodate such requests because witnesses are to be honored, and the plaintiff is not required to swear, especially if he has presented evidence (bayyinah). The overall authority of the basic rule (oath by Allah only) is from Zahir al-Riwayah, but the dispensation for talaq/'itaq is based on later fatawa (mutakhkhirin) due to changed circumstances.

Key Terms
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath sworn in a judicial context.
الطلاق (al-talaq): Divorce; used as an oath condition.
العتاق (al-‘itaq): Emancipation; used similarly.
التغليظ (al-taghlīz): Intensification of the oath by adding attributes of Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens if a defendant refuses to swear by talaq when a judge administers it under the dispensation?
A) The judge passes judgment for nukul.
B) The defendant is fined.
C) No judgment for nukul is passed.
D) The witness is called to swear instead.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school permits, as a concession, judicial oaths by talaq/'itaq when the opponent insists, due to people's carelessness with oaths by Allah.

Taghliz al-Yamin: Time and Place in Oath Intensification
Inter-madhhab dispute on taghliz al-yamin

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on taghliz by time and place and explain the Hanafi rebuttal to the Shafi'i proof using the general hadith and rational arguments.

Core Idea
Intensifying oaths by time/place is not legislated; the general hadith and rational proofs suffice.

This lesson examines a classic inter-madhhab dispute: should oaths be intensified (taghliz) by specifying a particular time and place, such as after Asr on Friday or near the pulpit? The Hanafi school argues that taghliz is achieved through the wording of the oath alone, not by external conditions. Al-Shafi'i, however, requires time/place intensification for serious oaths. We will explore the proofs on both sides and the Hanafi response, concluding with the operative ruling in Hanafi fiqh.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify what the Hanafi school holds regarding taghliz by time and place. Note that the matn says 'not by time and place' and that intensification is only through wording like using Allah's names.
The judge has discretion in wording; some Hanafi views allow increase for certain cases.
2
Identify the opponent and their view
Recognize that al-Shafi'i requires taghliz by time and place for qasamah, li'an, and wealth ≥200 mithqal. Note the specific locations and times: between Rukn and Maqam in Mecca, at the Prophet's grave in Medina, etc.
The Shafi'i view is presented in the passage.
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof
Examine the primary dalil: the general hadith 'The oath is upon the one who denies.' The Hanafis argue this is general (amm) and any specification by time/place is an addition requiring proof, which would amount to abrogation (naskh) without evidence.
Also consider the rational arguments: hardship, delay, and analogy with evidence.
4
Analyze the Shafi'i counter-proof
Understand the Shafi'i basis for taghliz by time/place: it deters perjury in serious cases. The Hanafi response is that the purpose of glorification is already achieved by wording alone.
The passage lists the locations and times for different cities.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Identify the practical difference: Hanafi judges do not require a specific time/place; Shafi'i judges do for certain oaths. The validity of the oath is unaffected, only the solemnity.
The mufta bihi in Hanafi school is that it is not required and not recommended, but permissible.

Source Text

نحو: بالله الطالب الغالب ، المدرك المهلك ، الحيّ الذي لا يموت أبداً، ونحو ذلك، لا بالزمان والمكان)، هذا عندنا، وعند الشَّافِعِي" يُغَلَّظ بالزَّمانِ كبعد صلاة العصر يوم الجمعة ، وبالمكان كالمسجد الجامع عند المنبر. فيغلظ عليه لعله يمتنع بذلك. قال في «الهداية» : والقاضي بالخيار إن شاء غلّظ وإن شاء لم يغلّظ فيقول : قل بالله أو والله ، وقيل : لا يغلّظ على المعروف بالصلاح ويغلط على غيره ، وقيل : يغلّظُ في الخطير من المال دون الحقير. انتهى.
[١]قوله : نحو بالله الطالب الغالب... الخ ؛ ومثل قوله : والله الذي لا إله إلا هو عالم الغيب والشهادة الرحمن الرحيم الذي يعلم من السرِّ ما يعلم من العلانية ما لفلان هذا عليك، ولا قبلك هذا المال الذي ادعاه، وهو كذا لا شيء منه.
وما ذكره الشارح له فيه ، تردد ، فإن أسماء الله توقيفية كما تقرر في موضعه، والاختيار في صفة التغليظ إلى القضاة يزيدون فيه ما شاؤوا وينقصون ما شاؤوا. كذا في «خزانة المفتيين». ثم يحترز عن عطف بعض الأسماء على البعض، كيلا يتكرر عليه اليمين، فإن المستحق يمين واحدة، فلو ذكر بالله والرحمن والرحيم بالواو صارت ثلاثة أيمان ، والمستحق يمين واحدة، ولو أمره بالعطف فأتى بواحدة ونكل عن الباقي لا يقضى عليه بالنكول ؛ لأنه قد أتى ما هو المستحقُ عليه، ولو غلّظ عليه فحلف من غير تغليظ ونَكَلَ عن التغليظ لا يقضى عليه بالنكول ؛ لأن المقصود هو الحلف بالله الله قد حصل. كما صرحوا به. قوله : وعند الشافعي ... الخ ؛ قال الشافعي له : إن كانت اليمين في قسامة أو في لعان، أو في مال عظيم يبلغ مائتي مثقال تغلّظ بالمكان فيحلف بين الركن والمقام
إن كان بمكة، وعند قبر النبي إن كان في المدينة، وعند الصخرة إن كان في بيت المقدس، وفي الجوامع في غيرها فإن لم تكن ففي المساجد، ويكون ذلك : يوم الجمعة بعد العصر.
ولنا : إطلاق قوله : «واليمين على من أنكر ) ، والتخصيص بالمكان أو الزمان زيادة على النص، وهو النسخ ؛ ولأن المقصود تعظيم المقسم به، وهو يحصلُ بدون ذلك ؛ ولأن فيه حرجاً على القاضي حيث يكلف الحضور، وهو مدفوع ؛ ولأن فيه تأخير حق المدعي في اليمين فلا يشرع ؛ ولأنه أحد ما ينقطع به الخصومة، فلا يختص بهما كالبينة. كذا في «التبيين». واعلم أن ظاهر المتن عدم مشروعيته. وذكر في «مجمع الأنهر» نقلاً عن «الحاوي القُدسي»: ولا يستحب تغليظُ اليمين بهما. انتهى. وظاهر أنه مباح؛ لأنه نفي الاستحباب، وهو لا يستلزم نفي الإباحة بخلاف العكس، وينبغي للقاضي أن يعظم حرمة اليمين أولاً ، ويتلو عليه : إِنَّ الَّذِينَ يَشْتَرُونَ بِعَهْدِ اللَّهِ وَأَيْمَهُمْ ثَمَنًا قَلِيلًا) الآية . كما في «الاختيار)، وغيره.
Not by time and place. For example: 'By Allah, the Seeker, the Overcomer, the Perceiver, the Destroyer, the Living who never dies,' and similar expressions, not by time and place. This is our position. According to al-Shafi'i: it is intensified by time, such as after Asr prayer on Friday, and by place, such as in the congregational mosque near the pulpit. So it is intensified upon him so that he may refrain thereby. He said in al-Hidayah: The judge has the choice; if he wishes he may intensify and if he wishes he may not intensify, saying: 'Say: By Allah, or by Allah.' And it is said: It is not intensified upon one known for righteousness, but it is intensified upon others. And it is said: It is intensified in substantial wealth, not in trifling matters. End. His statement: 'For example: 'By Allah, the Seeker, the Overcomer...' etc.; and similar to his statement: 'By Allah, besides Whom there is no god, Knower of the unseen and the witnessed, the Most Gracious, the Most Merciful, Who knows the secret as He knows the public, that so-and-so has no right over you, and you do not owe him this wealth that he claims, and it is such, none of it.' And what the commentator mentioned about it has some hesitation, for the names of Allah are tawqifiyyah (restricted to revelation) as established in its place, and the choice regarding the manner of intensification is left to the judges; they may increase as they wish and decrease as they wish. Thus in Khizanat al-Muftiyyin. Then caution is taken against conjoining some names with others, so that the oath is not repeated, for the required is one oath. If he mentions 'By Allah, and ar-Rahman, and ar-Rahim' with the waw, they become three oaths, while the required is one oath. And if he orders him to conjoin (i.e., say them together) and he performs one and refuses the rest, he is not judged as refusing (nukul) because he has fulfilled what was required of him. And if he intensifies upon him and he swears without intensification and refuses the intensification, he is not judged as refusing because the purpose is swearing by Allah, which has been achieved. As they stated explicitly. His statement: 'And according to al-Shafi'i...' Al-Shafi'i said: If the oath is in qasamah (compurgation) or li'an (mutual imprecation), or in a substantial wealth reaching two hundred mithqal, it is intensified by place: he swears between the corner and the Maqam if in Mecca, at the grave of the Prophet if in Medina, at the Rock if in Jerusalem, and in the congregational mosques elsewhere; if not, then in regular mosques, and that should be on Friday after Asr. Our proof: the general meaning of his statement: 'The oath is upon the one who denies.' Specifying by place or time is an addition to the text, which amounts to abrogation (naskh). Also because the purpose is glorification of the One sworn by, and that is achieved without it. Also because it causes hardship upon the judge as he is required to attend, and hardship is repelled. Also because it delays the right of the plaintiff in taking the oath, so it is not legislated. Also because it is one of the means by which the dispute is concluded, so it is not restricted to these two, like evidence. Thus in al-Tabyin. And know that the apparent meaning of the text (matn) is that it is not legislated. And it is mentioned in Majma' al-Anhur quoting from al-Hawi al-Qudsi: It is not recommended (mustahab) to intensify the oath by them (time and place). End. And it is apparent that it is permissible (mubah) because the negation of recommendation does not entail negation of permissibility, unlike the opposite. And it is appropriate for the judge to first magnify the sanctity of the oath and recite to him: 'Indeed, those who exchange the covenant of Allah and their oaths for a small price...' (Quran 3:77). As in al-Ikhtiyar and others.
Source pages p. 122

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute on the topic of taghliz al-yamin (intensification of the oath) by time and place. The Hanafi position, as stated in the matn, is that intensification is done by the wording of the oath itself (e.g., using Allah's names and attributes), not by specifying a particular time or place. The Shafi'i school, however, requires intensification by time (e.g., after Asr on Friday) and by place (e.g., near the pulpit, between the corner and Maqam in Mecca) for certain serious oaths. The author then quotes al-Hidayah noting that the judge has discretion in choosing the wording. The commentator discusses the permissibility of various wordings and cautions against conjoining multiple names as it multiplies the oaths. The Hanafi proof is based on the general hadith 'The oath is upon the one who denies' and several rational arguments: (1) specifying time/place is an addition to the text amounting to abrogation; (2) the purpose of glorifying God is already achieved; (3) it causes hardship for the judge; (4) it delays the plaintiff's right; (5) like evidence (bayyinah), the means of concluding disputes are not restricted by time/place. The passage concludes that the matn indicates it is not legislated, but other sources say it is not recommended (makruh tanzihan) but permissible. The judge is encouraged to remind the oath-taker of the gravity of perjury by reciting Quran 3:77.

Key Terms
تغليظ اليمين (taghliz al-yamin): Intensifying the oath by using specific wording or conditions to make it more solemn and deter perjury.
اليمين (al-yamin): An oath taken in court by a defendant who denies the claim, or by a plaintiff in certain cases.
القسامة (al-qasamah): An oath taken by multiple people in cases of homicide when there is no clear evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) It is recommended (mustahab) for all oaths.
C) It is not required and not recommended (makruh tanzihan), but permissible.
D) It is forbidden (haram) because it is an innovation.

Intensifying Oaths for Non-Muslims
Explains how to intensify oaths for Jews, Magians, and idolaters based on their beliefs

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on intensifying oaths for non-Muslims and apply the method of tailoring the oath to the defendant's beliefs.

Core Idea
A non-Muslim's oath is intensified by invoking what they hold sacred – the Torah for Jews, fire for Magians, and Allah's creation for idolaters.

In this passage, the Hanafi school explains how to intensify oaths when the defendant is a non-Muslim. The ruling is based on a prophetic practice and on the principle that an oath is most effective when it targets the individual's deepest convictions. We will examine the proof and the reasoning behind each category.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
List the three types of non-Muslims mentioned: Jew, Magian (Zoroastrian), and idolater (wathani).
Each has a distinct belief system that the law considers.
2
Extract the intensification formula for each
From the text, note the specific phrase or object used: for a Jew, the Torah; for a Magian, the creation of fire; for an idolater, Allah as Creator.
The formula is based on what they revere or acknowledge.
3
Examine the hadith proof for Jews
Analyze the hadith from Abu Huraira where the Prophet adjured Jews by Allah who sent down the Torah. This establishes the precedent.
The hadith is reported by Abu Dawud, indicating its authenticity for practice.
4
Recognize the ta'lil (reasoning)
Understand the rationale: the oath is intensified according to the belief of the oath-taker to increase its solemnity and deter perjury.
This is a form of istihsan (juristic preference) based on custom and effectiveness.
5
Apply the principle broadly
If a non-Muslim belongs to a group not explicitly mentioned, analogize based on their core beliefs: identify what they hold sacred and invoke that.
The underlying illa is that the oath should be meaningful to the taker.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحلف اليهودي ؛ لما روي عن أبي هريرة أن رسول الله ، قال لليهود: «وأنشدكم بالله الذي أنزل التوراة على موسى ما تجدون في التوراة على من زنى، أخرجه أبو داود ؛ ولأنّ أهل الكتاب يعتقدون نبوة نبيهم فيؤكد عليهم بذكر المُنَزَّل على نبيهم. والمجوسي يعتقد تعظيم النار فيؤكد عليهم بذكر خلقها، والوثني وهو الذي يعبد غير الله تعالى يعتقد أن الله تعالى خالقه، وإنّما يشرك مع الله تعالى غيره بدليل قوله : وَلَين سَأَلْتَهُم مَّنْ خَلَقَ السَّمَوَاتِ وَالْأَرْضَ لَيَقُولُنَّ الله ) (٧)
And the oath of the Jew: because it is narrated from Abu Huraira that the Messenger of Allah said to the Jews: 'I adjure you by Allah Who sent down the Torah to Moses: what do you find in the Torah regarding one who commits adultery?' Reported by Abu Dawud. And because the People of the Book believe in the prophethood of their prophet, so the oath is emphasized upon them by mentioning what was revealed to their prophet. The Magian (Zoroastrian) believes in the veneration of fire, so the oath is emphasized upon them by mentioning its creation. The idolater (wathani) who worships other than Allah believes that Allah is his Creator, but associates others with Allah as evidenced by His saying: 'And if you ask them who created the heavens and the earth, they will surely say: Allah.'
Source pages p. 123

Commentary
This passage provides the ruling on how to intensify oaths when the defendant is a non-Muslim. The Hanafi school prescribes that the oath should be tailored to the beliefs of the individual to maximize its solemnity and deter perjury. For a Jew, the intensification is by invoking the Torah revealed to Moses, based on the hadith where the Prophet used this formula. For a Magian (fire-worshipper), the oath is intensified by mentioning the creation of fire, as they hold fire in high regard. For an idolater (one who worships other than Allah but believes in Allah as Creator), the oath is intensified by reminding them that Allah is their Creator, using the Quranic verse that even idolaters acknowledge Allah as the Creator. This approach is reasoned (ta'lil) by considering the psychological impact on the oath-taker.

Key Terms
الوثني (al-wathani): An idolater who worships idols or other beings besides Allah but still acknowledges Allah as the Creator.
المجوسي (al-majusi): A Zoroastrian (fire-worshipper). They are not considered People of the Book but are treated similarly for certain legal purposes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi ruling to intensify a non-Muslim's oath by invoking their sacred beliefs is rooted in the Prophet's practice and designed to ensure the oath's solemnity and deterrent effect.

Oath Formulas in Claims: Sale, Marriage, Divorce, and Usurpation
Explains oath formulas and an intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi oath formulas for sale, marriage, divorce, and usurpation, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute on administering oaths in marriage claims without a financial component.

Core Idea
The oath formula depends on the type of claim; in marriage without financial claim, the mufta bihi position is to administer the oath.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya details the proper judicial oaths for various claims. It introduces the distinction between swearing on the existing state (al-hasil) and on the cause (al-sabab), providing specific formulas for sale, marriage, divorce, and usurpation. A key intra-Hanafi dispute is highlighted regarding whether an oath is administered when a woman claims marriage without a financial demand, with the relied-upon position being that of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (Sahibayn).

Guided Path
1
Distinguish the two oath types
Identify the two types: swearing on the existing state (al-hasil) and swearing on the cause (al-sabab). Understand that al-hasil refers to the current state of the claim (e.g., 'no standing marriage now'), while al-sabab refers to the originating act (e.g., 'I did not make the contract').
2
Apply the general rule for determining type
Learn the rule: If the cause cannot be removed (like the act of sale itself), the oath is on the cause by consensus. If it can be removed (like a continuing contract), see if the plaintiff is harmed by an oath on al-hasil. If not harmed, Imam and Muhammad say oath on al-hasil; Abu Yusuf says on the cause.
This rule is the basis for the formulas that follow.
3
Memorize the specific formulas
For sale: 'By Allah, there is no standing sale between you two currently.' For marriage (with financial claim): 'By Allah, there is no standing marriage currently.' For divorce (single irrevocable): 'By Allah, she is not irrevocably divorced from you now.' For usurpation: 'What you must return.' For trust/debt: similar denial formulas.
The formulas are precise and vary by claim.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute on marriage claims
Imam Abu Hanifa holds that if the claim is purely for marriage (no maintenance), no oath is administered because marriage is a continuous state. Sahibayn hold that an oath is administered based on the general principle that any denial of a claim requires an oath. The mufta bihi is the view of Sahibayn.
This thamarah al-khilaf affects judicial practice.

Source Text

(۲)
الإمام، فعنده حينئذ لا يحلف لما مر سابقاً. وقال شيخنا العلامة الطحطاوي (٢) : وهذا في النكاح على قوله محمول على ما إذا كان مع النكاح دعوى المال، أما إذا تجرَّدَ عنها ، فإن الإمام لا يرى الحلفُ فيه والمذهب قولُهما، وهو التحليف، قاله المقدسي. انتهى.
[٤] قوله : ما هي بائن منك ؛ أي بالله ما هي بائن منك ، وهذا في البائن الواحد، وأما إذا كان بالثلاث يحلف بالله ما طلقتها ثلاثاً في النكاح الذي بينكما، وفي الرجعي : يحلف بالله ما هي طالق في النكاح الذي بينكما، وهو معنى قوله الآن.
[0] قوله : وفي الغصب... الخ ؛ وفي الوديعة بالله ما له هذا الذي ادعاه في يدك وديعة، ولا شيء من الذي في يدك ، ولا له قِبَلَك حقٌّ، ويحلفه في الدين : بالله ما له
Book of Claims: Its Definition and Rulings 127 [2] : [4] And he swears upon the existing (state) in sale and marriage: 'By Allah, there is no standing sale between you two' or 'no standing marriage currently.' In divorce: 'She is not irrevocably divorced from you now.' In usurpation: 'What you must return.' And he swears upon the existing (state) in sale and marriage: 'By Allah, there is no standing sale between you two' or 'no standing marriage currently.' In divorce: 'She is not irrevocably divorced from you now.' In usurpation: 'What you must return.' [1] His saying: 'And he swears...': This is another type of oath method, namely swearing upon the existing (state) and upon the cause. The rule is: The cause either can be removed by a remover or not. If the latter, then swearing upon the cause is by consensus. If the former, then if the plaintiff is harmed by swearing upon the existing (state), then it is for that reason; and if he is not harmed, then he swears upon the existing (state) according to Imam and Muhammad. According to Abu Yusuf: He swears upon the cause. As you will know. [2] His saying: 'upon the existing (al-hasil)': i.e., upon the form of the defendant's denial. And this is its technical meaning. Its linguistic meaning is: what remains, is established, and what other than it has gone. As in al-Qamus. And it can be considered here, for he swears upon what is firmly established and stable now. [3] His saying: 'currently' (fi al-hal): is related to both statements, and the estimated speech is: 'By Allah, there is no standing sale between you two currently' or 'By Allah, there is no standing marriage between you two currently.' The first (sale) applies when he claims that he bought it; the second (for marriage) when she claims maintenance. If she claimed the marriage itself, then the example is according to the opinion of the Two Companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), not according to Imam (Abu Hanifa). According to him, then he does not swear, as mentioned previously. And our teacher al-Allama al-Tahtawi said: This in marriage according to his (Abu Hanifa's) statement is interpreted as when along with marriage there is a claim for money; if it is free from that, then Imam does not consider swearing in it, and the school (for fatwa) is the statement of the two (Sahibayn), which is to administer the oath. Al-Maqdisi said that. End. [4] His saying: 'she is not irrevocably divorced from you': i.e., 'By Allah, she is not irrevocably divorced from you.' This applies in a single irrevocable divorce. For triple divorce, he swears: 'By Allah, I did not divorce her three times in the marriage between you.' And in revocable divorce, he swears: 'By Allah, she is not divorced in the marriage between you.' That is the meaning of his statement 'now'. [0] His saying: 'And in usurpation...etc.': And in trust (wadi'a): 'By Allah, this property which he claims is not a trust in your hand, nor any of what is in your hand, nor do you owe him any right.' And he makes him swear in debt: 'By Allah, he has no...'
Source pages p. 127

Commentary
This passage details the proper formulas for judicial oaths in various claims. It begins by introducing the distinction between swearing on the existing state (al-hasil) and on the cause (al-sabab). The general rule: if the cause cannot be removed (like the act of sale itself), oath is on the cause. If it can be removed (like a standing contract), there is a disagreement. The commentary defines 'al-hasil' linguistically as what remains and technically as the form of denial. Examples are given for sale ('no standing sale'), marriage ('no standing marriage'), divorce ('she is not irrevocably divorced'), and usurpation ('what you must return'). A key intra-Hanafi dispute is highlighted: In a claim for marriage (without money), Abu Hanifa does not administer an oath because marriage is a continuous state that cannot be denied by oath? Actually the text says according to Imam, if the claim is purely for marriage (not for maintenance), he does not swear the defendant, while Abu Yusuf and Muhammad do. The relied-upon position (madhhab) is that of the two Sahibayn. Further details: for triple divorce, the oath is 'I did not divorce her three times'; for revocable, 'she is not divorced'. For trusts and debts, additional formulas are given.

Key Terms
الحاصل (al-ḥāṣil): Linguistically: what remains and is established; technically: the form of the defendant's denial of the claim.
السبب (al-sabab): The cause or act that gave rise to the claim (e.g., the act of sale, marriage contract).
البائن (al-bāʾin): Irrevocable divorce; the divorce that severs the marriage bond immediately, as opposed to revocable divorce.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school (mufta bihi), what is the oath formula for a claim of marriage where the wife demands maintenance?
A) By Allah, she is not your wife
B) By Allah, there is no standing marriage between you two currently
C) By Allah, I have not married her
D) By Allah, she is not irrevocably divorced

Key Takeaway
The oath formula in Hanafi procedure is specific to the type of claim, and the relied-upon position requires an oath even in pure marriage claims.
